Forward by Author Janice Livingston
	
When I first met Mr. Tokio Jones, I was immersed in research for my book detailing the life and times of Chris and Janique Turner. But he proved to be an exceptional figure in his own right, with a story that fascinated me. I knew I had to tell his tale as well, one filled with drama and hyperbole. We were of different worlds, now occupying some unlikely intersection of the two. I did my best to leave things in their rawest form, and to not color the text via the lens of my own upbringing. In some respects, I feel I have succeeded. In other ways, this work is but a thumbnail sketch of a complex and admirable individual.
Over time, the little notes we made for each other in the draft became such a part of the flavor of the story, I ultimately decided to leave them in. An unconventional format for the tale of an unconventional man. I think that is somehow both fitting and poetic. 
- JL
	 

Chapter 1 – Tokio Motherfucking Jones	

My motherfuckin’ name is Tokio. At least it was. Clarence James Jackson, since you get to know the real me. We might as well start there. I’m a motherfuckin’ pimp. That is to say, I used to be, could be, and thus, still am. Be that as it may. You cannot take that title from me, nor disparage what I did. But that’s what it was. This is what it is now.
	So, there I was. Little twelve-year-old Clarence. Wide-eyed. Positive. Thrilled with the world. Happy-go-lucky-ass. Good grades. B’s, you know. You don’t want to be too square. You’re taught that, somehow. Not just in school. To me, it likely comes about with each generation being smarter than the last, evolutionarily, and via media amplification. When the child is shown to be smarter than the parent, gaslighting begins. But this is starting to sound like some fuckin’ pity party. Shit ain’t like that. 
	I never got in any real trouble. Rough housing. Out after dark. Teasing. A kid living a kid life. 
	No dad to speak of. But no dad doesn’t mean an absence of a male presence, does it? On the contrary, it often means a smattering array of the drunken, the violent, the twisted, and the literal evil. Plus, the occasional decent cat who nevertheless didn’t stick around for long. 
Now that I’m getting it all out, I can see that it’s not sympathy I want to invoke. I mean, I’m Tokio Jones. Who are you? I don’t know how much money I have because I don’t care to check. From my end, it appears to be infinite, and growing. As opposed to what? Some soccer mom reading a book written by a white lady as told to her by a black man? Feeling sorry for me, who at times traded women like baseball cards?
	Because what I seek to promote is not sympathy. But anger.
	Now, I guess, how should I put this? When you start getting hair on your balls, you begin to look at the world differently. Not just about how you want to put your dick in just about anything qualifying as either round, wet, or female. That’s not even what I mean, though. You start to get a chip on your shoulder. In some more mild cultures, under different conditions, this may just lead to slight rebellion. A protest over bedtimes or a curfew. 
In my case, things sort of amplified. I started to see that there was such a thing as school smart, but there was also smart about school. I knew that a straight job would be more of the same. Manageable, but repugnant. To fall in with the dumbasses, in a dumbass society?  That ain’t Tokio. That ain’t even Clarence.
	Meanwhile, they had some giant Cadillacs that rolled down my street. I mean some eighth-of-a-block vehicles with paint jobs that shone like armor. Motherfuckers would time their rides to throw the biggest bling based on the position of the sun. Gold rims. Literal gold fucking rims. If one didn’t have diamonds, a green, sparkling Town Car with whitewall tires, they were expensive fakes. Cats was fly. And that’s what it became all about.
	Suits. Man, people laugh about pimp clothes, but I don’t know what you’re talkin’ ‘bout. If these cats didn’t have a suit worthy of Nipsey Russell, they weren’t stepping outside. Brooks Brothers, tailored shit like that. Lookin’ better than the Italians they copped the look from, adding a big-ass hat like a clown. It was their uniform. Let me tell you about shit like that. If you’re wearing a hat, you’re a sitting duck. I don’t know what it is, but dudes was more on guard for their hats falling off when they were standing out, profiling, playing Big Bank Take Little Bank, swapping hoes, than they were about any possible threat to their being.
	Best believe they had the hottest bitches imaginable with them. Sometimes more than one. White. High School. Bitches. Now, I know every single one of you knows what I mean. I’ll thank you for not pretending otherwise. Teenage pussy makes the world go ‘round.
	In the ghetto, rebellion was a religion. We did it big, or not at all.  I wasn’t rebelling against my mostly hard-working mom, who scarcely imposed any rules on me anyway. Or school, which was just boring, and required more patience than brains. 
	We rebelled against the world.
	If you were of the age that you were born in the early sixties, you likely escaped that hippie shit, which wasn’t really part of our culture anyway. We were the cats selling weed and coke to any long-haired white boy with money. Mostly, I guess we probably mostly sold it to each other. We didn’t charge the girls cash, but they paid, all the same. What you got during that period instead was the beginning of the radical era. Not so much black-fist-in-the-air Kumbaya as motherfuckin’ TNT, pipe bombs, machine gun the pigs kind of shit.  
	You mix all that up together and put a hormone-laden young black man such as myself into the fray, and almost anything might happen. And did. But for me, it didn’t manifest as impulsive violence.
	I got colder. I don’t know how else to describe it. It could, in some circles, be considered psychopathy. More properly, sociopathy. I suppose that’s true to a degree, but that also means that each and every one of us is a sociopath, adapting to our own personal situations. I feel there is an unfairly imposed connotation associated with sociopathy. You’re viewed as a dangerous miscreant. One can be a sociopath and follow what is really the Hippocratic Oath: First, Do No Harm. That didn’t mean I was a pussy. It meant I wasn’t going to jail, or get took, or taken out. 
Instead, I began to plot in secret. In my head, at first. Later on, I wrote some down. It became some heavy shit, some of which is transcribed directly in a later chapter, I believe. Like, I would play ball with you, and we could talk about fuckin’ Fat Albert or whatever, but I was thinking about how them cats had all that money. It became what you might term an obsession with me.
That is, when I wasn’t thinking about their bitches. Leaning out the window, lampin’. Cats spendin’ a thousand dollars on a car stereo to blast Parliament Funkadelic, paradin’ around a bunch of blonde, brace-faced fifteen-year-olds. And I didn’t know anyone else could fuck them. Fucking goals. Some shit my grand-nephew taught me. Hashtag: goals. If I didn’t do anything else, I hipped him to the whole game before anyone else got to read it. He remains committed to law school, however, to my delight.
Now Janice, she is a book by herself. She just said she would shoot me if I ever printed a word about her life, and laughed.
The money, undoubtedly, got the bitches. But there was more to it than just that. A celebrity. Pimps were the superstars of our little subset of the world. More so even than drug dealers, who were a lot less high-profile. 
For fucking real, cocaine. A woman will do much more for cocaine than she will for money, initially. At that point, it becomes academic. Fifth Ward, Texas was dusted with cocaine. Baggies were regularly swept up by street cleaners, in some place in quantities enough to consider recycling. It would have been a smooth-ass hustle to collect them by the thousands, wash them en masse, and resell them. The best businesses are started with some crimes, I’ve found. Little to no startup cost, and a one-time investment. 
What was weird about my change in personality was that it made me more attentive in school. To be honest, I didn’t give a fuck about anything but Math and English. History, we know, is bullshit. But on those subjects, my thirst for knowledge was unquenchable.
It all began to relate to the hustle. Numbers and words, man. A pimp’s main two bitches. You have to be a sexy fucking poet Romeo, and more shrewd than Jewish accountants. It was, for me, becoming a relative man of the world in comparison to my peers. Once I began to link the two, education and game, I took to them both with the same deadly seriousness, and inadvertently learned a whole lot more in the process. I became a young scholar in service of the pimp hustle.
Now, about the white girl thing. It was never anything against the sisters. Sometimes when you’re at that in-between age, they feel more like cousins. But the ones that were fuckin’ were fuckin’, and I wasn’t one to discriminate. I can’t even compare black women and white women. They’re just two classes of ultimate beauty, each uniquely different. And I will tell you that about each of your major, how do they say that shit now, cultures and national origins. If I believed in God, I would say the feminine of the species is the closest thing to sacred that exists.
I know how that sounds on one hand like proselytizing a religion, but is also absurd, given the nature of my desired profession. Nevertheless.   
Man, then my cousin moved to town.

Chapter 2 – Cuz

	My cousin Tracy moved down to H-Town from Detroit. He didn’t take to the heat very well, and had no idea how to deal with Mexicans. Basically, ignoring them is somewhat showing them respect. Other than that, a no-mercy beating is in order for confrontations. But that inevitably led to deaths. Death wasn’t where I was headed. 
	See, Tracy might have sweated, but he had silk handkerchiefs for that. Cat always stayed clean. That is to say he was never seen in anything less than the same sort of clothes worn by my local heroes. 
	We were cool, but right away, he started asking me about bitches. I mean, he hadn’t been off the airplane for more than an hour or so, and this cat wants to know what girls are fuckin’. Like a fool, I told him. First rule of pimp game: tell nothing to no one. 
	Motherfuckin’ Tracy stole my best girl. Plus two more, eventually. I mean cut off, for me. This dude didn’t give them flowers and stupid shit. They gave him presents. A fuckin’ Gucci watch. Real shit. I could scarcely fathom the concept of giving less and getting more. Once I got past my initial flush of anger, an emotion that at best only simmered in me at a low heat, I confronted him about it. Not concerned with why or what. But how. How in the fuck, man, do you just straight pull bitches like that? Nigga you fly, but you ain’t that fly.
	He laughed. “You know that bitch Janet?”
	Janet was so beautiful. I had treasured her over all of the others put together. She was already planning on nursing school. Just textbox future sexy wife and mother material, in stark contrast to what amounted to hoodrats for the rest of them.
	“Yeah, motherfucker, I know Janet.”
	“I’ll let you fuck her for $50.”
	I turned red, and I know he could see it. I almost swung on dude right there. Which probably would have ended this tale, one way or another. Instead, that cold part of me listened. Tracy might have killed me if I had. He was as serious as I was. Except he knew what he was doing.
	“Fuck you,” I said instead. 
	“Understandable, cousin. Now let us move on.” With a single, pimp-like phrase, he had defused the entire situation. The power of words, man. His tone said the show must go on, regardless.
	I split, still fuming. But thinking. If you want to know the truth, I went as far away as I could so no one would see me cry. I probably walked ten miles that day. When I got back home (he and his mom was stayin’ with us until they got a place), I decided to call his bluff.
“Forty,” I said.
He laughed. “A first-time, family discount.” Then he put out his hand, and I gave him all the money I had in the world. When he had pocketed it, he told me, “You already failed your first test, cat. Don’t ever pay for it. That makes you a mark.”
Another wave of pain crossed my face. I aimed for anger, but I think he saw the hurt, and tried to console me, in his own way. But first he picked up the phone.
“Yes, hello, sir. May I speak to Janet, please? Thank you.”
Eloquent as hell. My man could flip modes like it was no one’s business. 
“Hey, babygirl. Daddy has a job for you… Yes. You won’ believe. Clarence.” He laughed, and she was undoubtedly laughing with him. At me. “Bring your big ass on. When you’re done, Daddy will take you shopping.”
I was stunned. 
“Now, look. I don’t feel bad. It’s game. But I will hip you to this. It’s possible for you to steal her back. You would have to put it on her so hardcore as to change her entire mentality. But it can be done. I did it.”
I didn’t say anything, but absorbed what he was saying. He had dick-whipped my best girl into being a whore, and was giving me the opportunity to try and do the same. It was, in a twisted sense, a noble gesture. 
“I’m goin’ cop some herb. Have fun.”
He shot out, and I sat on my bed, contemplating the direction my life had taken. I no longer felt the coldness I had. This was no longer theoretical, but real-life shit. And it hurt. Not twenty minutes later, and mom had let her in the house. She knocked on my door briefly, and then showed herself in.
“Hey, Clarence. Did you miss me?” She laughed. A sweet, light laugh that had always captivated me.
I stood and faced her, then moved in to kiss her. 
She stopped me. “I don’t kiss…” Instead, she leaned over my bed and pulled her skirt up.
Now, the sight of her firm, young, cinnamon ass was definitely something to behold, hurt feelings or not. But my dick was not responding in the least. This was all wrong, for me. The only thing that excited me was the thought of whipping the shit out of her. I knew that was a terrible idea, for various reasons. Not the least of which was that I was scared I wouldn’t be able to stop.
I moved up behind her and grabbed her hips. She wiggled that ass like she used to do when she was all mine, pushing back against my khakis. 
Instead of rising to the occasion, a tear fell on her. Then more followed.
I pushed away from her.
“Get out,” I said flatly.
She sat up, at first confused. Then she looked into my eyes, turned her back, and left.
That was our last conversation. I would catch her looking at me sometimes at school, but I ignored her like she didn’t exist. And that was the end of my first foray into the pimp game. I didn’t have the heart for it. Too soft, too sentimental. Too full of love.
The next week, I signed up for welding classes.

Chapter 3 – Rebranded

	I reassessed everything I knew about life, and myself. Looked at where I had been headed. Changed direction entirely. Now this ain’t some miraculous, uplifting story of turnaround, so don’t stop readin’. We will get to the good shit eventually. 
	But I considered my situation extensively. I was fourteen years old, not a year out from being a virgin myself. I had limited resources, limited opportunities, and nothing like the cut-throated soul of a player that I thought I was. On the other hand, I had a stable family life, was popular, did well in school, and still caught the eye of any local girl who had even started to have sexual thoughts of her own. Tracy couldn’t get them all.
	In other words, I was wise enough, maybe enlightened by the experience, to see that I had it pretty good, and could enjoy my youth while I had it. That didn’t mean I could coast like a square.
	Welders, you see, had it pretty good. It was hot, hard work, but it was skilled work. Mostly I knew that they made more than my teachers, looked cool as hell, and every baby mama in the hood would land one as a husband if they could. That counts for somethin’.
	I didn’t talk to Tracy much, especially a few weeks later when he and his mom moved out. Instead, I embarked on a new program for myself. Much like my drive to be a criminal womanizer, I put everything I had into learning a legal trade. My initial push to absorb all I could about the lettered arts had put me in a position where schoolwork was merely a few minutes out of my day. I looked forward to two things at school: shop and gym.
	Tracy and Janet had soured me on girls to a degree. Oh, I still fucked them. In fact, I fucked even more of them, harder and rougher. But when I wasn’t fuckin’ ‘em, I was mostly ignoring ‘em. All that shit somehow worked in my favor. Don’t ever try and apply logic to human behavior, because it clearly don’t apply.
	I took to welding pretty good. Within a few weeks, I could run a decent structural bead. And I kept getting better. Applying my drive and determination to welding guaranteed me a future. At the same time, I was at the point in my physical development that I started to make serious gains in the weight and muscle department. By the end of the year, I had shot up in height as well, and was looking close to a full-grown man by the time I was 15.
	People down age differences, role-play, intergenerational romance, all that shit, in public. The stark truth of it is, most girls (women) want a daddy, and all that represents. For such a wide-spread phenomenon, it is wildly misunderstood. Also, though, America is full of women with daddy issues. When you get to be a certain age, you learn there are grandfather issues beyond that. But whatever it is, when I wasn’t working out, or in school, I was laid up with some girl, usually one older than I was. It was probably one of the greatest periods of my early life.
	I had hit a wall at school, vocation-wise. I graduated the class with one of my only As, and found myself without access to the shop. I thought about talking to my mother for money, but I knew that was dead-end. Not that she wouldn’t give it to me, but she didn’t have it to give. I considered hustling again, briefly. My hunger was that great. Instead, I finally decided on hitting up Tracy.
	“How much you need, cuz?” It was so casual of him.
	“A machine is two hunnert.”
	He peeled off two crisp one-hundred-dollar bills.
	“I’mma need three back.”
	I looked at the money dangling in front of me. “When?”
	“A month, let’s say. Can you do that?”
	“Maaan,” I said. “I dunno.”
	“Then I’ll give you two. And, bonus, your first beatdown if you fuck me.”
	I snatched the bills from his hand, challenged like that. Like Tracy’s soft ass could beat me down.
	“I got you,” I said. Went straight to the pawn shop. 
	Now, what he didn’t know was that I had saved $70 toward it already. Plus, I had overestimated the cost. I left out of there with my first welding machine for $150, a little cheap Chinese unit small enough that I could carry it home. I used this unexpected dividend to cop a grinder for $20.  But I also had $100 toward paying him back. Everything else I had stolen from school when I still had a chance. A hood, a few pair of gloves, and some 6010 and 7018 rods.
I was going to learn to weld pipe, which is where the big money was. A subject only briefly mentioned in school, and not taught at all. As much as I wanted to get to burning rods, though, I cleaned up the machine, organized all my stuff, and hit the streets. Instead of everything around me looking like something I wanted to fuck, I suddenly only saw things that needed welding. A broken stoop railing. A fallen brace meant to hold a gutter in place. People that needed their front doors reinforced.
	Now, I know how shit works, a little bit. It’s a ghetto. Knocking on doors and asking people if they wanted their nuttin’-ass little railing fixed for $10 was never going to fly. Instead, I started looking for maintenance men. A lot of the buildings had a regular, live-in dude who fixed small things. But they spent most of their time unclogging drains. I only wished I had thought to start networking sooner.
	It took a few days of bush-beating (during which time I did very little bush-beating of the other variety), but by at the end of the week, I had made my first $30. Then I hit the motherlode. I watched an old white dude pull up to a project building in a work truck, and followed him until he was almost inside. 
	“Excuse me, sir,” I said, employing every bit of the charm I had borrowed from Tracy. Except his ass sounded fake and sarcastic compared to my sincere effort. “Do you need anything welded?”
	He stopped, entirely undisturbed by my abrupt attempt at conversation and employment, and just stared at me for a little while.
	“Yep. I actually do. Come see.”
	I followed him inside, and we took the elevator up to the rooftop mechanical room. We were almost inside when I got a tinge of fear. It was too easy, and despite the cool and easygoing vibe, it suddenly felt like a trap. I walked in behind him with my fists clenched and my muscles tensed, half-convinced he was a queer, and I was going to have to fight for my life.
	Instead, he walked me over to a stacked contraption that was the size of four big refrigerators.
	“This shit is all fucked up,” he told me. “I inherited it from the last asshole who had this job.”
	“What’s wrong with it?” I asked, finally relaxing.
	“Everything. Look. See the vent on top of the bottom one? They’re installed wrong. That’s supposed to be the top unit. Then the dumbass didn’t bolt the frame together, but welded the whole damn thing out. Not just wrong, but kinda locked in. These new boilers are useless like this.”
	I studied the situation. “So, what do we do?”
	“What do you do, you mean? I have toilets to fix, air conditioners to replace, a laundry room that is constantly falling apart, and a ball-busting wife that says I work too much as it is. What I need is for you to cut the frame apart so we can rearrange the units. Then weld it back together.”
	Suddenly I had more work on my hands than I knew what to do with.
	“Yes, sir,” I said. “I can do that.” 
	“You’re gonna have to grind out the welds so we can reuse the frame. Can you handle it?”
	I nodded, already turning the problem over in my head.
	“Good. You seem to have a good attitude. I mean, at least you don’t spit when you see me like some of these folks. You in school?”
	I nodded again.
	“Then be here tomorrow after school and start. I’ll leave a key under this trashcan. I have some grinding discs in the truck, too.”
	“I’ll be here,” I told him. Then, as an afterthought, “How much?”
	“That, son, is a good question. The best I can do is $20 a day.”
	It was fair. Pretty good money, in those days. It probably came out of his pocket, furthermore. Old George was my first real mentor, and it was kind of cool to meet someone who cared more about getting a job done than the economics behind it. Plus, he left it wide open as to how many hours I would have to work to make $20.
	“Thank you, sir,” I said, and put out my hand. My first grown-man handshake.
	“George,” he said, accepting my gesture. “And who are you?”
	“Clarence Jackson. Future welder of America.”
	He laughed. “Well, the future is now. Handle up on this, and we’ll see what else needs fixin’.”
	I walked home with my head and chest inflated, and could hardly sleep that night.

Chapter 4 – Everybody’s Workin’ For The Weekend
	
I grew up in that mechanical room. Not that I spent a protracted period there, although I did some time, fa’ sho. But that is where I became a man. Not a parody of a man like the pimps I admired. A for-really, hard-working, honest craftsman.
	Because I was fully on the honor system. That is where your true character is made, when no one is watching. Morality, Janice told me once, is what you do when people are looking. Ethics is when no one else is around. Your true self. I like that.
	All I knew was that I had to get that frame taken apart. There was no one to push me, motivate me, cheerlead me, or anything else. So, while I could have dicked around, done less, and still got paid, I saw nothing to do but get to work. After I copped a buzz and explored the mechanical room a bit. That paid off right away, because what I found, tucked away in a corner changed my life. Fucking steel pipe, man. Exactly what I needed to start working on the next level.
	Enthused by this, I attacked the frame with gusto, and by the time I was ready to go home, I had the top entirely removed, angle iron pieces cleaned up and neatly stacked, and already found myself out of work. Because the next parts were supporting the top boiler.
	When I came back the next day, George was waiting for me.
	“Good job,” he told me, and I felt a warm satisfaction at that. “But cut off the vertical pieces or we’ll never get it out of there.”
	“I was a little worried it would fall,” I told him.
	“Ha. No, that thing weighs over a thousand pounds. It ain’t going nowhere. The frame is for when it’s operational, so it doesn’t vibrate loose.”
	“A’ight.” We were already so casual I dropped the formality. We were practically peers. Just two working tradesmen, trying to tackle a project.
	“Look,” he said, “It’s Friday. I have to take my bitch-ass wife to her bitch-ass sister’s. Are you working this weekend?”
	“I can.”
	“Good.” He handed me $40. “Put in full days, and I’ll give you double for the weekend work. Capiche?”
	I didn’t know what the fuck capiche was, but I did know what double meant.
	“Hell yes,” I told him. “But what do I do? Once I get these side pieces cut off and cleaned up, I’m two-blocked until the top boiler is moved.” (Now, Janice, you know I didn’t know what that meant back then. – Tokio.) (Artistic license. – Janice.)
	“Everything you need is in that big maroon toolbox under the fan. Construction work is problem solving. You can do it.”
 	I wasn’t at all convinced that I could, but I said, “A’ight,” anyway.
	“Good. Alright, then. I’m going to sneak in a beer before I go home. Have a good weekend.”
	Just like that, he was gone, and I had potentially gotten myself in over my head.
	Shit was already givin’ me anxiety. What I found in the box didn’t help a whole lot with that. It had some wire and nylon slings, and a couple of old, rusted-up devices with levers and chains on them. Nothing we had ever covered in school, somehow.
	So, I went back and tackled what I already knew how to do. Removing the four upright pieces didn’t take too long, even cleaning them up and buttering up the spots I had cut into. The first time I ever really used my welding machine was on my first job. That’s pretty cool. But I was ahead of schedule, and back to the unknown. I made sure the door was fully shut, and lit my last reefer. If George had ever smelled it that year, he didn’t say anything about it. But I found that it helped me figure out new and complicated things, properly applied. Most people just watched cartoons and shit when they were high. For me, it was stress relief, and a tool.
	The joint dangled from my mouth as I unpacked everything in the toolbox. I laid out all the slings neatly, arranged by material, length, and color. I untangled all the damn chains and shit, unhooked the hooks from each other, and then laid out the lifting devices. Underneath all that, I found a bunch of horseshoe-lookin’ things that had threaded pins across their open ends.
	Things started to make sense. There were labels on the slings that told you what weights they were rated for. They even had examples of two ways to use them. Likewise, the mechanical things in the box (Okay, I know what they’re called now, but I didn’t back then. Plus, Janice says ‘Housewives don’t want to read about construction work, not even construction workers do.’ So, I’m trying to be brief. Not that I really care all too much about what housewives want.) had weight ratings on them, too. ¾ ton on the levered thing, and 2 tons for the chainwheel device.
	(I told Janice that I thought was way too much of a parenthetical aside, above, and she said, “I don’t tell you how to mack, so don’t tell me how to write.” She is the only person who ever talked to me like Hazel used to talk to me, and I love it.)
The only unanswered piece of the puzzle were the horseshoes. (Okay, the fuckin’ shackles.) But when I took the pin out of one, their utility became apparent. So, a gameplan had formed. I was still way too nervous about the operation, though. I had gained a little confidence now that I knew what I had to do, but not enough to move forward. Instead, I went home for dinner.
“Where you been, boy?” my mom asked me. “Out there runnin’ them streets…”
I never really let her know different. My newfound persona was still secretive as hell, and, I don’t know, life seemed simpler if she thought I was a normal hood kid doing normal hood shit.
“Sorry, mom. You know.”
I doubt she believed me, because I was dirty as hell. But she let it go. 
“Wash your hands,” was the only other thing she said to me before retiring to her room for whatever people watched on a Friday night when they only had four channels to choose from.
I emptied out the pot, which was still warm enough for me. It was chicken-n-dumplins. Shit was out of a can, but still good as hell. Anything your mother touched with her own hands was slightly better than before she had, anyway. I had a mother who cared enough to work and cook, and that was something. After I ate, I rinsed everything and left it in the sink. Now, many years later, I feel like an asshole for it. But I was still a kid, for the most part. Dishes were a mom thing, to me. I showered, and fell asleep straight away, at least as frazzled from stress as I was from the actual work.
The next morning, though, I bounded from bed, refreshed and ready to kick that job in the ass. I was in so deep, I had forgotten about weed, or girls, or anything, really. I had something to do, and something to prove, furthermore. Not to George so much as to me.
Everything was still laid out the way I had left it, so I got the tallest ladder I could, and set it up next to the boiler. Then found I couldn’t really reach out that far. So, I brought it back and got the extension one. I ran it up to the ceiling and rested it against the roof support, then climbed up to test it.
I ain’t gonna lie, it was scary. Now, imagine that. A kid runs through what is practically a warzone daily, and the first big fear of his life was climbing 14 feet in the air on a ladder.  But one does what one has to do.
I climbed all the way up to test it out, and it more or less stayed in place. I went down, put a shackle on a sling, and climbed back up. Soon, I had a place to hang something from. Prolly 50 pounds, which is a lot when you weigh 150 at most yourself.

I used the chain to run the hook down until it was resting on the unit, and then slipped the two longest slings underneath it, bringing them together up top, as best I could. But they were too short. I solved this by running a third through them both, and connecting the whole thing with another shackle. (Horseshoe thing. – Janice.) (Oh, come on! – Tokio.)

Fuck, man.

Chapter 5 – Hard-Ass, Pipe-Hittin’ Nigga

I was done fuckin’ around. When I got back the next morning, I had two choices: cut the rest of the frame apart, or use the tools I was given. Because I had a revelation on the way over. I could hoist the first unit, cut out the rest of the frame, slide the bottom one out to the side, and then lower the first back down. Then deal with the rest. Or I could let the tools work for me from the start.
The first option held a lot of appeal. Because I understood the process, and it revolved around what I knew best so far, grinding and welding. But something my shop teacher used to say to people in class resonated hard with me: “You have to be smarter than X.” X was whatever was proving to be difficult in class. Trying to grind something while you hold it one hand? Put it in a clamp. “You have to be smarter than the angle iron.” Weld is too runny? You’re operating your machine too hot. “You have to be smarter than the metal.”
I had to be smarter than these boilers. Cutting the frame apart and doing all those things, then reassembling it, might not even be doable in one day. I had a self-imposed schedule.
I ran the hook back up and elevated the unit, for what I hoped was the last time. 
First, I tried a rope. I was mostly able to pull the boiler to one side enough to lower it back down onto flat ground. But it was tough. I could only use one hand, and the other had to operate the chain. Then I kicked myself.
Ten minutes later I had figured out the levered device (Come-a-long. - Janice), and recognized its importance. I used the wire rope to attach it to a column (one that was structurally sound, lucky for me), attached the hook, and then pulled the machine solidly to the side. It was trivial after that to lower it to the floor.
I did the same thing again, lifted out the other unit, then put it on the other side. All I had to do was put the first one back in place. It wasn’t even two hours later when I had the boilers stacked the way they were supposed to go. Things were all downhill from there. In a good way. I started tackin’ the frame back together, which was pretty easy since all the pieces were already cleaned up. 
You couldn’t even see the dent I had put in the top of the unit too much unless you were looking for it. Since I had it whipped, I took my time and made some nice, pretty welds. I even capped them with the hard rods, and I feel sorry for the next kid that ever has to cut it apart. 
When it was 100%, I stood back and surveyed my work. It’s a good feeling, man, doing something like that and seeing the results. I brought my machine over to the toolbox, and put two pieces of pipe on it. For the first time ever, I dropped my hood and welded a tack, connecting them. I rolled it 180 degrees, and added a second. It wasn’t twenty minutes before I had joined my first two sticks. 
Then I tried to weld it out. It is a lot harder to weld a curved surface like that, as I soon found out. I had to make lots of tiny welds only an inch or two long before I had to turn the pipe. That led to a lot of what we call tie-ins. Tie-ins are a weak point. But after almost a grueling hour of difficult welding, it was done. I looked inside and saw that my bead penetration was decent. No stalactites. Stalagmites. Whatever the fuck. (Drips? – Janice.) But I could do better. 
I finished my second attempt, and improved at running a continual bead, rolling the pipe in one hand as I welded with the other. It was probably nine o’clock or so when I decided I was just going to lay down and rest my eyes for a minute…
The next thing I knew, the door to the mechanical room was opening, and daylight was flooding in.
“Hol-ee shit, boy. I mean, kid. I mean, son. I mean… Clarence.”
I sat up, alarmed. I didn’t go home last night! I missed school!
“Shit,” I said, getting up.
But George didn’t give a fuck about all that.
“You weren’t supposed to finish,” he said.
“What?”
“I gave you something I didn’t think you could do. Hell, I would have had trouble with it. I just thought you would learn a lot by trying.”

“Naw. I welded some pipe. I hope that’s okay. Man, I missed school. Moms’ll be pissed.”
He pulled out his wallet and handed me a hundred-dollar bill. “Buy her something nice. Pipe welding, eh? Show me.” 
I led him back to the toolbox where I had two welded out sticks of pipe.
“Not bad, not bad,” he said. But he was nowhere near as impressed as he was with the angle iron. I thought I had done pretty damn good. “A little out of square, but maybe within tolerances. What sort of gap did you use?”
“Gap? I just welded the shit together.”
“A bevel?”
“What’s that?”
George laughed. “The wonder of a bear riding a bicycle is not that he rides it well, but that he rides it at all.”
“What’s that mean?”
“You did good. But if you’re serious about pipe welding, you have a lot to learn. Let’s take a ride.”
“What, now?”
“You got somewhere else to go?”
I thought about it. Mom was at work; school was a lost cause.
 “A’ight.”
We hopped in the truck and drove about twelve miles out of Houston proper. Suddenly I was in a world of parks and women walking dogs with strollers. A stark contrast to my usual surroundings. No boarded-up windows, no trash blowing in the gutters. People waved and smiled as we drove past.
He backed into a driveway and got down. “Come grab this box with me.”
We lifted it onto the tailgate, and the shit was heavy, for something as small a dorm fridge.
“Let’s get out of here before my wife comes out…”
Back at the apartments, George unfolded a little dolly, and we loaded the box onto it, taking it with us on the elevator. When we had it inside the mechanical room, he said, “Open it.”
That thing was slap-full of tools I had only read about, at best. 
“A pipe welder has to be a pipe fitter, just as much as he has to be a welder. In fact, for commercial work, you have to be both just to get hired.”
“What do you know about all that?” I asked him.
“Oh, pretty much everything. I fit for years, before I had to settle down and leave the life.”
“The life?” That was sounding like the way pimps talked.
“I’ve done my time on the road, making $75,000 a year.”
“Holy shit.” That was serious money back then. Doctor and lawyer money, almost.
“These people don’t pay me half of what I used to make. So, I do half as much. If they need real pipefitting and welding, they can pay for it. Go ahead and unpack the box.”
I started pulling stuff out, and he explained what everything was as I did. A lot of things I knew about, but not necessarily how to use. Levels and squares, he told me, are the first things a pipefitter needs. Other, he said, than a damn brain.
“So, look. These don’t do me any good anymore. If I tried to go on the road, my wife would leave me. Because road life is not really compatible with her lifestyle. She’s scared I would get some pussy.”
I laughed.
“You’re also getting expensive. But I can use the help. We have to pipe these boilers in, for one thing.”
“Okay…” I said, not sure where exactly he was going with this.
“If you work for me this summer, apprentice, let’s say, gratis, I’ll give you the toolbox.”
“What’s gratis?”
“Free. I would pay you with the toolbox.”
It sounded like a hustle. At the same time, I never wanted anything more in my life.
“And the tools?”
He laughed. “Of course, and the tools. I have well over $1500 in that box. Probably closer to two.”
“Damn.” It was too good of an offer to pass up. “I have school still, you know.”
“I know. I’ll come in later and you can do two hours a night during the week. Sixteen hours on the weekends. Deal?”
“Capiche,” I said, and we both laughed.
“Good deal. I have to go deal with idiots who pour hot grease down their drains and then complain when they get stopped up. But let me show you something.”
He plugged in my grinder, squinted, and held a piece of pipe with one hand while he worked the edge with the other. He rolled it as he worked, and a few minutes later, there was a sharp, even edge all the way around. It looked easy as hell, but I could already see how much better welding it would be.
“Practice making a few bevels. Then weld another two joints together. And get home before your mom does. I don’t want to catch a charge for doing a good deed.”
“I’m on it.”
“Wear some safety glasses!” he said as he walked out the door.
I was now an apprentice fitter/welder, and not too sure about going back to school at all.

	“Can I think about that?” It was a tough call. I could see how having a job would be good, and also learning more than a school could teach. But I’d be missing out on a lot. Girls, primarily. That was my real concern.
	“Of course. You have time. I’m sure they would be glad to have you work at the shop even until school starts, if that’s what you want.”
	Aunt Tilly was real cool like that. She was like, I don’t know, a Montessori mom (A what? – Tokio.) (Go with it. – Janice.) or some shit. I guess that’s how they were out west. It was in stark contrast to the “My way or no way” of southern parents. At least in my limited experience.
	“I’m going to bed,” she said shortly after our conversation. “You can use Maxxy’s bathroom to take a shower. I hope you like pink soap, pink shampoo, and fluffy pink towels…”
	I laughed. “Thank you, Aunt Tilly. I’ll be fine.”
	“Look,” she said. You’re almost a grown-ass man, and our great aunt/nephew status makes me feel really old. How about you just call me Til?”
	“I’ll try,” I said. Like I told you, she was just cool like that. “Goodnight… Til.”
	“Don’t take any shit from Maxxy,” she said, walking away. “Lord knows she’ll give it.”
	Aunt Tilly had put all the food away while I ate, and then washed my dishes as soon as I had finished eating, so there wasn’t much left to do but shower and sleep. Just like she said, that bathroom looked like a unicorn got sick in there. If something wasn’t pink, it was sparkly. I came out of it smelling like fruit and bubblegum. 
	I sat my ass on the couch in fresh clothes, and was about to see what L.A. TV had to offer, when Maxxy made an entrance.
	“Come see,” she said, in her commanding little girl way that left no room for argument.
	“These are my stuffies. If you molest them, I will kill you while you sleep. This is Klip-Klop. He looks like a horse, but he has powers. He’s like a unicorn, but more low-key. So, no horn. This is Mrs. Beasley. She was my mom’s, and I hate her. Not my mom, but Mrs. Beasley.”
	It was a pretty ugly and scary white lady doll, to be fair.
	“This is the Bear family. Barry White, Barry Manilow, Barry Goldwater, and Barry Gibb.”
	I couldn’t stop laughing. Maxxy was doing a damn comedy routine for me.
	“These are my Barbies. There’s white-ass Barbie, other white-ass Barbie, even whiter-ass Skipper, and black Barbie. Who looks just like white Barbie, but a little more tan. It’s bullshit.”
	I laughed again.
	“Hands off. Absolute. Now, I have decided that you can use the radio. When you finish, you are to put the dial back to 95.6, and the volume is to remain on a minimum of 4, and a maximum of 6. Got it?”
	I shook my head, smiling. 
	“Repeat it back for me, then.”
	“Niny-five six, 4 to 6.”
	“You could enunciate a bit better, you know.”
	This little kid was more eloquent and demanding than my teachers were. 
	“Now, note the sad and small black and white TV in my possession.”
	It was indeed tiny and colorless. But I didn’t have my own TV back home at all. 
	“It is entirely off limits. And here’s why.”
	She removed a little plastic slipcover thing from a square, flat box. It said “Timex-Sinclair ZX-81.”
	“What’s that?”
	“That, my rube of a cousin, is a computer. The future.”
	“It don’t even look like a typewriter, hardly.” But I had never seen a computer in someone’s house, ever. I didn’t know that was a thing.
	 “Observe.” She turned it and the TV on, and in a few seconds, there was a black screen with a flashing white underline. Maxxy pecked away at it, and the little tape recorder hooked up to it started whirring.
	“Okay,” she said, “It’s a little slow.”
	When it had stopped, she typed something else on thekeyboard, and the screen cleared, replaced with a question. “What is your name?” the screen said. 
	“Go ahead. Answer it,” she told me.
	I typed out “clarence”, and she reached over to press enter since I didn’t. 
	“clarence,” the screen said, “you are a butt.”
	Then Maxxy giggled her ass off.
	“Very funny. You wrote this?”
	“Of course. I have a lot of little programs like that. When mama gets off her own butt and gets me a printer, I’m going to put them all into one big one.”
	“She lets you curse like that?”
	“What’s she gonna do, banish me to Rigel-5, or the outer kingdoms?”
	“Wut?” 
	“Cousin, you are a delight. Tabula rasa. I’m going watch Johnny Carson. Night night.”
	Maxxy was wild. I wasn’t going to even ask her what tabula rasa was. I was having enough of an inferiority complex with her as it was. Johnny Carson was some boring adult stuff that I couldn’t imagine any kid watching for fun. 
	When I got up later to go to the bathroom, I saw her asleep on the couch, TV blaring, and the light still on. I covered her up a little, shut everything off, and went back to my unicorn pillow and blanket. 
	It already felt more like home than home did.

Chapter 9 – Welcome to the Jungle

	Aunt Tilly didn’t seem to work, as such, but that didn’t mean she stayed home all the time. I woke up to a note, and a bowl of cereal. Maxxy got up a little while later, and fixed her own breakfast. After we had eaten in silence, save for the constant Captain Crunching, I washed the dishes and put everything away.
	Maxxy got dressed, and came back into the living room.
	“Cousin,” she said. “I require your services. I need you to, oh, how would one say this in a way that someone such as yourself could understand? Take me to da sto’.”
	She could be, at the same time, melodramatic, funny, and insulting. 
	“I can do that,” I said, and moved to put my shoes on.
	“You can’t wear that, though,” she said. “Change your shirt.”
	I looked down at my Coca-Cola shirt, which was clean, and I had always thought looked fresh.
	“What’s wrong with it?”
	“Just find a neutral color. Trust me.”
	I came back with a grey Cowboys jersey. 
	“That is just barely acceptable.”
	I thought she was being funny.
	When we stepped outside, Maxxy said, “The store is six blocks away. One street up to the left is Piru set. The parallel street in the other direction is Crib. After three blocks, we will pass into a side street controlled by some minor league Mexicans known as Kings Beyond Control. Choose wisely.”
	This sounded like more fanciful Maxxy storytelling. So, we started down the street to the right.
	“Interesting,” she told me. “Try not to make eye contact.”
	I started to understand what she was saying. It was beginning to sound like Fifth Ward, all of a sudden. I stopped walking and said, “What are you talking about?”
	“Gangs, cousin. Surely you’re no stranger to that.”
	But I was. We didn’t really play much gang shit where I lived. If we lived on the same street, we were likely a loose-knit fraternity, but there wasn’t anything formal about it. The higher-level criminals all seemed to be solo and independent. Suddenly, a walk to the store felt like the most dangerous thing I had ever done. At least since climbing my first ladder.
	“This is a neighborhood,” I said.
	“That’s a great observation, Clarence.”
	“I mean, these are houses.”
	“Excellent deduction.”
	“Fucking stop it, Maxxy.” That got her attention. “What I’m saying is that this hardly seems like the place for a bunch of gangs.”
	“Nevertheless. Tread lightly, that’s all.”
	This time, I made her hold my hand as we walked, and she didn’t shrink away from that. When we turned down the first side street, she said, “Blue”. I didn’t pay much attention. About halfway down, though, I noticed that almost everyone I saw, mostly dudes sitting on the porch, were wearing blue plaid. And sunglasses, thinking about it now.
	At the second block, she said, “Left here. How’s your Spanish?”
	“Pretty not good.”
	“Pobrecito…”
	I would have learned a lot more that year if I asked Maxxy what she meant when she said shit that I didn’t understand, instead of playing it off and ignoring her.
	We made our way down the block, and it was indeed a Mexican street. It was kind of wild how segregated things were like that. We were almost to the next turn that would put us in sight of the convenience store that was our destination when a dude stepped onto the sidewalk in front of us. He had a brown plaid shirt with the top two buttons connected over a white t-shirt and khakis.
	“¿Órale. Adónde vas?”
	“Nos vamos a la tienda,” she rattled off without hesitation.
	“Bien,” he said, and smiled as he stepped back out of the way.
	When we had turned the corner, I said, “What the fuck was that about?”
	“He was a lookout. Just doing his job. He asked where we were going, and I told him.”
	“That’s crazy. We wouldn’t take no Mexican sweating us where I’m from.”
	“Well, here, you would be shot dead. So, there’s that.”
	I found I didn’t have anything else to say. What little bravado I had was fading fast.
	Maxxy was literally a kid in a candy store when we got there. She wanted everything. The first thing she did was get a little brown bag from the clerk and start filling it with a big assortment of the nickel candies like Now-N-Laters. Then she grabbed a candy bar. Then she grabbed some kind of rainbow fruit candy I had never seen before.
	“Woah, I only have three dollars,” I told her.
	She laughed. “Literally a poor little thing. This one is on me, noble escort.”
	That was both demeaning and sweet at the same time. Even at her age, she was a master of keeping you off-balance. She ran a strong mental game, which I had never really encountered before in anyone. I got myself a Coke and a honeybun, and we went to the counter.
	“$6.56,” the clerk said. Holy shit, that was high.
	I pulled out my little nothing-ass money, and Maxxy said, “Keep it.” Instead, she handed him a twenty. When he gave her the change, she said, “Incorrect. You owe me four cents.” Then she just stared him down until he slid four pennies over the counter to her. “That’s better. Have a superlative existence.”
	Outside the store, she peeled the wrapper off her Hershey bar. “Gotta eat this before it melts. Let’s go back the other way. You might as well get the full ride.”
	I didn’t really like the sound of that. Already, I felt better about the relatively known qualities of the streets we had already safely navigated. “You sure?”
	“As sure as one can be in this chaotic world of random chance.”
	I mean, who talks like that? 
	But we headed back on the second route. When we turned onto the street that connected to our home block, Maxxy said, “Red,” through a mouthful of Laffy Taffy. At this point, I didn’t even question what she was saying. Of course, she was right. But it was the same shit, just a different color. No one really wanted to fuck with us. 
	Until someone did. 
	We were almost to the end of the second block, when a cat my age stepped up to the sidewalk before we passed.
	“What set you claimin’?” he asked me.
	“What?”
	“Gangs are gay,” Maxxy said.
	“Bitch--“ the kid said. But before he could say more, I had busted him in his mouth. Hard. When he hit the ground, a pistol fell out of the back of his waistband.
	“Get it,” Maxxy said. “Let’s go.”
	Dude was laid out. But it seemed to be the sensible thing to do. Plus, Maxxy was so sure of herself. I grabbed it and stuffed it into the front of my pants.
	Once we were safely back inside, she said, “Cousin, could we perhaps keep this adventure to ourselves, and away from the delicate ears of my parental unit?”
	“I think that’s a good idea,” I said.
	“She wouldn’t like me eating all this candy.”
	“What about the gun?”
	“What gun?” Maxxy said. 

Chapter 10 – Dead Poets Society

	I checked out the pistol in my room. Maxxy’s room. A .45 semi-automatic. I didn’t know that, then. But it was a serious hand-cannon. I hid that shit deep in the back of the closet, and hoped I would never need to use it. But I had it, all the same. From what I could tell, that was a good thing, too.
	Aunt Tilly rolled back at about noon. I helped her bring in about twelve bags of groceries. Enough to fill the whole back seat, and restock her already laden larder. (I would never say some shit like that. – Tokio.) (You just did. – Janice.)
	I also helped put everything away, guided by the constant corrective direction of Maxxy. She was super organized like her mother. I know it’s wrong to say, but I began to admire Aunt Til more than I did my own mom. That’s probably normal for most kids, I don’t know. She had instilled some great habits into her daughter at an early age, and those habits later took her far in life. I felt a little neglected in that I hadn’t really ever been taught shit as far as valuable life lessons went. But I was making up for lost time.
	After we had sandwiches, which was the closest thing Til ever made that came close to pre-cooked, she said, “Clarence, I have a few more things to attend to, today. Would you mind staying with Maxxy at the library with her little peer group for a few hours?”
	“No, of course not.”
	“Good. I’ll drop you guys off. And I’ll be back before five.”
	“The library closes at six, mama bear,” Maxxy told her.
	“I said five.” And that was the end of the discussion.
	Maxine went into her room, and came back with a pile of books that was almost half as tall as she was. 
	“Okay, I’m ready,” she said.
	“Did you really read all these?”
	“We only go once a month. Mama says it’s to ‘socialize’. But, no. I read the important parts of all of them, read three of them completely, and skimmed the rest. If you must know.”
	I hadn’t read that many books in my entire life, and the ones I had were assigned in school. I grabbed her stack, and we went out to the car. Aunt Til didn’t notice, but I looked up and down both sides of the street as we approached the car. Maxxy did, though, and I sensed a slight bit of approval from her, furthermore.
	The library was a few miles away, which was good. Too far to walk, at least with a little girl. I was relieved. I had enough excitement for one day. Maxxy read the whole way over there, finishing the last little bit of a book as we pulled up. It was called “The Crying of Lot 49” by a cat named Thomas Pynchon. I tried to read that shit twenty years later, and I still didn’t understand it. (I’m not sure anyone fully understands Pynchon. Even Pynchon. – Janice.)
	At the library, Aunt Til kept the car running. “Here’s five dollars for snacks. And a gas bill. Show it to the librarians, and tell them you’re with Maxxy. They’ll probably let you get a library card. Maxine keeps mine so full I can hardly squeeze a book in for me from time to time. I’ll be back by five.”
	“Six,” Maxxy said.
	“Five.”
	 Inside, we put the books on the return counter, and Maxxy ran squealing to the kid section, which was outfitted with little kid couches and chairs, where she was greeted by her friends. They were all cute, well-dressed little girls like her, but ran the gamut of races. Mostly black girls, but two white ones, an Asian, and I guess a Latina, South American or something. Not exactly what I was expecting.
	“Sup, sup?” one of the little white girls asked her.
	“Ain’t no thang, can’t complain, get passed like a muhfukka.”
	“You know that shit.”
	“Mm-hmm,” the Asian girl said.
	“Had to break a fool off,” Maxine told them. “Nigga jaw got jacked.”
	“Handle yo’ bizness,” one of the black girls said. The sight of her in her flowered-print dress, looking like something out of a painting, stood in stark contrast to her speech.
	“Maxxy,” I said. “What are you doing?”
	“We’re role-playing, cousin. Preparing ourselves for the world at large.”
	It was too weird for me. I went to get a library card, which was pretty painless, and only cost a dollar. The kid books didn’t interest me. I really missed out on a good opportunity as a result. I’m not ashamed to tell you that I now love Judy Blume’s stuff. Not the ones where she got all teenage and freaky, writing about wet dreams and shit. The simple, wholesome ones about eating turtles and getting food dumped on your head. It almost reads like sci-fi, to me, it’s so foreign to my upbringing. But they make me nostalgic, all the same. 
	I wandered the stacks for a while, not really knowing what I was looking for. There were too many choices, and I had nothing to go off of but titles and genres. I was interested, but not equipped enough to know what I was interested in, exactly.
	“Psst, cousin,” Maxxy said from behind me. I turned around, and she had a small pile of books in her hands. “Come read with us…”
	I took them from her and followed her back to their little nook. Far from the noisy and chattering group they were twenty minutes earlier, they were all nose-deep and engrossed. It was so surreal, I would have thought it was a prank if I hadn’t known Maxxy.
	One was reading a book called “The World is Full of Married Men”. I could tell from the cover that it was way too adult for her. Adult in an adult way, not that it was beyond her capacity. It was by a woman named Jackie Collins, who I found out much later in life was my friend Janique’s favorite author. Well, aside from Tom Robbins and Chris Turner. In other words, it was trashy as hell.
	Another one had “Wuthering Heights”, and I still don’t know how anyone can read that shit. I never even tried, with stuff like that. It might be a classic, but I can’t get into stories like that.
	One girl had a book almost as big as she was. It was so thick, she had to put it on a table to read it. The fucking Gulag Archipelago, by an author who’s name I still can’t pronounce right. (Aleksandr Soul-Za-Neat-Sin. - Janice.) (You turned my attempt at a joke into a public shaming. – Tokio.)
	Little Maxxy was apparently taking it light, reading something called “The Moon is a Harsh Mistress”. She told me about it later, and I eventually read it myself before she turned it back in. 
	I sat down and looked at the books in my lap.
	“The Art of War” by Sun Tzu. “Don’t You Turn Back” by Langston Hughes. “Pimp” by Iceberg Slim. At the sight of this one, I glanced over at Maxxy, who was too focused to notice. Had she read this shit herself? The bottom book was called “A Wrinkle in Time” by who I guess is a French lady, Madeleine L’Engle. 
	As bad as I wanted to check out the pimp book, I was way too self-conscious of my situation and setting. Instead, I hid it under the other ones. The Wrinkle in Time one actually looked interesting. But that would have made me the only person reading a kid’s book in the entire group. Not cool.
	Instead, I settled on Art of War.
	It was easy to get into, in a sense. The language was very clear and concise. But I wasn’t involved in war or politics, so it didn’t take hold right away. A chapter in or so, and I realized this cat was talking about life. Treating the world at large as a battlefield. That shit stayed with me to this day, and I used to make other people read it, later on. Hazel read it. Maybe she took to it too well, herself.
	 I didn’t look up again for a long while, until Maxxy jumped up and said, “Snack time!” and the other girls cheered. It was just crazy how they flipped back and forth between what you expected of kids that age and stuff that you would never imagine. 
	She put out her hand, and I gave her what was left from the five Aunt Til had given me. “Never spend your own money when you can spend someone else’s,” she told me. Then they all ran to the stairwell vending machines. I gotta say, the librarians were real cool with them. They never told them to be quiet, stop running, no eating in the library, nothin’. And it wasn’t out of laziness or a lack of concern. They seemed to know these girls were special, and treated them like little grown-ups. They were given a respect that I rarely saw adults granted. I guess to a librarian, they were pretty remarkable all on their own, just because of the stuff they read. 
	They came back loaded down with cookies, cakes, candy, drinks, chips. Way more than the little four dollars could have afforded them. Later on, I learned that only Maxxy lived in the relative hood we occupied. What was a suburb to us in Fifth Ward was still pretty much just ghetto plus for L.A.
	“Thank you, Clarence,” the little Asian girl who was reading the Jackie Collins told me. The way she looked at me, and the purr in her voice made me uncomfortable. They ate and chattered, talking about what they were all reading, school stuff, boys. The usual kid talk, in part. 
	After a few more minutes had passed, Maxxy jumped up. “Shit, moms is comin’.” She unfolded a list from her pocket and went to retrieve her month’s reading material. Most of the other girls did the same. The little Asian girl stayed behind.
	“So,” she said, “What do you think of Soon Zoo?”
	“Which one is that?” I asked her. “The one in the African print?”
	She laughed. “The book you are currently reading so intently that you have failed to notice me.”
	“Oh. Haha. It’s real cool. I never would have picked this one up on my own, but now I can’t put it down.”
	“That’s a good indication of character,” she told me. “You might read ‘The Prince’ by Niccoló Machiavelli after that one. They’re practically companion pieces.”
	“You’ve read these?”
	“Of course. Who recommends a book they haven’t read themselves?”
	Damned if this girl wasn’t more sophisticated than anyone outside of Maxxy and Aunt Til, and maybe more so. It was unnerving. We talked for a while, but I was glad when Max and the others came back. Maxine had to make two trips, she was so loaded down.
	They talked about their new titles and I read some more, until I heard the voice of Aunt Til say, “Hey, kids. Did you have a good day?”
	“Yes!” they all told her at once. 
	“Good to hear. Y’all ready? Does anyone need a ride?”
	They shook their heads no, said their goodbyes, and then the Asian girl, Sarah, I learned, piped up. She was actually Vietnamese, by the way. “I could use a ride…”
	Gulp. 
	“No problem,” Aunt Tilly told her. “Bye, girls! Have a good month.”
	Maxxy and her friends hugged and said their goodbyes, and one of the white girls actually kissed her on the lips. I thought that was a little weird, but Aunt Til didn’t seem to think so. We all carried armloads of books to the car, and had to put them in the trunk, there were so many.
	“Clarence, you can ride in the front again if you want,” Til said.
	“No, he’s riding in the back with us,” Maxxy told her. She got in, sat in the middle, and I got in beside her. But before I could close the door, Sarah caught it. “Scoot over,” she told me. I was trapped. Looking back now, I think they planned that shit.
	We drove north on the freeway until the city faded away a bit, and I started to see the ocean again. Aunt Til wove her way up some hills, and she obviously knew her way around the area. I found Sarah’s hand on my leg. Maxxy giggled. I took it off and put it back in her lap.
	She moved it back, and this time squeezed my thigh. I was going to move it again, when Maxxy said, “Clarence, tell mama about our day…”
	It was so deliberate of her, in tone and phrasing, I knew what she was doing, even if Auntie Til didn’t. I was checkmated once again. I really needed to finish The Art of War just to deal with Maxxy’s Machiavellian machinations. (Okay, that was a good one. I wish I had actually said that shit. – Tokio.) I just bullshitted her about the library trip, of course. There was no way I was telling her we had walked into some crazy gang territory.
	We dropped Sarah off at this place that looked like some movie star shit. I mean, perfect lawn, giant house behind a big gate. I bet the view was amazing, the ocean in one direction, and L.A. in the other. She gave my thigh one final squeeze, said, “Nice meeting you…” and hopped out. To my great relief.
	They got out and took her books from the trunk, walking her to the front door.
	On the way back home, Aunt Til said, “Who’s down for ice cream?”
	“Oh my god, me!” Maxxy said. And that was all it took. We pulled into a Sonic and ate it all on the way home, Aunt Til included.
	When we got back home, I lugged books into Maxxy’s room, and when I had her alone, hissed, “If one of your friends ever touches me like that again, you can go to the library by yourself.”
	She laughed. “What? She was just practicing on you. How did she do?”
	“A little too good, if making me feel awkward as hell was her goal. You need to tell her to stop. And if any of her friends want to ‘practice’, tell them to look elsewhere.”
	Maxxy saw how serious I was. “Fine. It won’t happen again.”
	It’s a good thing, because damn, she was better at it than I would have ever admitted to anyone before now.

Chapter 11 – Watt’s Up

	Aunt Til fried chicken, whipped potatoes, made gravy, and boiled ears of corn and sliced carrots. The woman was a dynamo. To this day, I have never eaten better in my life. Maxxy sat on the couch finishing her first new tome, and I was in her room, anxious to check out the Iceberg Slim, a sort of book I had never known existed. 
	Maxine came in shortly after I went in, and saw me with it.
	“Entirely predictable,” she said. “But take this, and come read with me,” she said, handing me the Hughes book instead.
	“Why?”
	“Respect the hustle. Read ‘Mother to Son’, first. And be ready.”
	I had no idea what she was talking about, but she had such a commanding tone and a surety when she spoke, I didn’t argue. We sat down and I looked for that poem. It was good, and made me a little sad, really. Pretty soon I was engrossed, flipping through it and picking out whatever caught my eye to read. Aunt Til eventually noticed.
	“Oh, Langston Hughes! How delightful, Clarence. What do you think?”
	I hadn’t had a lot of time to actually think, but damn if Maxxy wasn’t the craftiest kid I had ever met. 
	“It’s cooler than I thought,” I said. “Some of the language is kinda weird.”
	“Ah, yes. He does get a bit overblown in his usage of colloquialisms. Some of it reads like a parody of black speech, today. But, as we say, it was a different time.”
	“Amos and Andy-ass,” Maxxy said.
	“Maxine! I would be upset, except you’re right, in a sense. But you have to consider his intent.”
	She went back to taking out hot pieces of chicken and putting them on paper towels to drain.
	“See?” Maxxy said. 
	“Da fuck is a colloquialism?”
	“Informal speech.”
	She went back to her book, and I to mine. It was a far cry from Fifth Ward, in every respect. But it was also nothing like I had imagined L.A. was going to be. We were both lost in other worlds when Aunt Tilly said, “Come eat!”
	“Thank you, mama,” Maxxy said. But brought her book to the table.
	“No,” her mother told her. “Not tonight, and not with chicken. It’s a library book.”
	“Fine…” She closed it and brought it back to the couch.
	“We can talk about what you’re reading, though,” Til offered.
	Max brightened at this prospect considerably, and I just ate and listened.
	“Well,” Maxxy said, “It’s really more of a novella. Robert Heinlein.”
	“Your father loved Heinlein!” Til said. This was the first time either of them had mentioned him.
	“I know, mama. His copy was the first one I read, remember?”
	“The hippie one.”
	“Yes, the hippie one.” Maxine looked at me, “It’s not really a hippie book, but those people loved it. It’s actually a repackaging of the Christ mythos, as many good sci-fi stories are. Childhood’s End by Arthur Clark, for example.” She ended her brief attempt to educate me on the subject, and turned back to her mother. “This one is an odd mix of views, somehow solidified into a single coherent one. It is anti-state, but at the same time, there is a serious ethos at work that becomes effectively a state, while remaining at the same time, anarchic and laissez-faire.”
	Maxxy was way over my head. 
	“Interesting.”
	“So, yeah. They go to the moon to get away from Earth oppression.”
	“Oh, like The Other Foot by Bradbury.”
	“Well… Kind of. For one thing, there aren’t really any black people in any Heinlein stories, from what I can tell. And that Bradbury one is kind of heavy-handed. He should have called it ‘Last Nigger to Mars’. I would have. This is more generalized, I guess you’d say. But they do share the same spirit, overall.”
	“It sounds good,” I said, in an effort to contribute to the discussion.
	“It is! This one has the most glorious phrases ever conceived. It has replaced my previous favorite of his, ‘Specialization is for insects’, as my mantra.”
	I didn’t ask what a mantra was. I think this is how ignorant people stay ignorant. They refuse to let their guards down and admit they don’t know something, when they could instead cop to it and then actually learn.
	“What is it?”
	“TANSTAAFL.”
	“Sounds like some Lebanese food,” Til said.
	“Ha! It stands for ‘There Ain’t No Such Thing As A Free Lunch’.”
	“Oh, that is a nice one, Maxxy. Words to live by, indeed. It’s kind of like ‘There’s always free cheese in a mousetrap’.”
	“I never heard that one! I wonder if that is the etymology of the phrase?”
	“Probably so,” Tilly told her. “I think it’s a pretty old one.”
	“It would be an interesting thing to study,” Maxxy said, and resumed her chicken leg.
	“Clarence, do you know anything about the history of Watts?” Aunt Til asked me.
	“I ain’t never heard of Watts. Unless you mean electricity.”
	“Heh. No. The city of Watts is right by where we live, Lynwood. But it kind of relates to what Maxxy and I were talking about. In the 40s, Watts was a thriving black part of L.A. They had black-owned stores, black doctors, black law offices.”
	“That sounds real cool.”
	“It was. A little too cool for some, apparently. Because by the 60s, Watts was more or less destroyed by riots. Now it’s a wasteland of a sort. Comparatively speaking. And there are some of us here, mostly people older than myself, that feel this was by design.”
	“On purpose?”
	“Right. Watts took that ‘separate but equal’ concept and ran with it. People say it was amazing. More of a mecca than even Harlem at its heights. It makes me sad to think of it, seeing where the world is at now. Really, it makes me angry.”
	“I bet.”
	“Now, as it relates to what Maxxy was saying, in part, ‘free’ things are what got us here. Because there used to be all married couples raising children back then. Now women are rewarded for kicking the men out. It’s a trap, but they’ll never see that.”
	She sighed.
	It seemed funny coming from her, a woman raising a child on her own. And Aunt Til didn’t seem to have a job herself. I know regular people, at least the ones I knew, didn’t buy carloads of groceries unless it was with food stamps.
	“Those who forget history are doomed to repeat it,” Til said. “Those who remember, though, have to sit idly by and watch it repeat itself, helpless to change anything. We were once dependent on others in this country, and now we’re back full circle. It’s a mess.”
	This was all a pretty heavy topic for a family dinner. It was turning into a roundtable discussion with soul food.
	“Knowledge is power,” Maxxy said.
	“It is,” Til agreed. “But it is of far more benefit to individuals than groups. I only see bad things ahead for a lot of people.”
	It was good foresight on her part. Because we all know now, things did get a lot worse, and not just in Los Angeles. Changing the subject, and maybe sensing that she was getting a little heavy, Aunt Til said, “What do we think about taking Clarence to church tomorrow?”
	“Nooo, mama! I hate it so much!”
	“He could meet some people his own age. Ones who wear belts and shoes, instead of sagging chinos and flip-flops.”
	“Not this week, okay? Please? I have to break him in before you ruin him.”
	Tilly laughed. “I guess. Plus, we really should get him something nice to wear. There will be girls there…”
	That got my attention, and I think she knew that.
	“Thank you!” Maxxy said. “I hate church. And I hate church people.”
	“That’s not very Christian of you, Maxine.”
	“That’s because I’m not a Christian…”
	“Touché.”
	“Clarence, do you think that would be something you’d be interested in?”
	“I’m not a big fan of church, for real. But I would go.”
	“Great! We’ll go shopping this week, then.”
	“Woo,” Maxxy said, less than enthused.
	We finished eating, and thankfully the conversation steered away from heady discussions about race and politics. Maxine filled her mother in on the lives of her friends, and I mostly just ate. After dinner, though, Aunt Til came into Maxxy’s room, where I was laying on the bed, stuffed. She shut the door behind her.
	“I thought I owed you a little explanation of where I’m coming from,” she told me. I sat up, and she settled in on the bed with me. “I feel like I may be viewed as a hypocrite, otherwise.”
	“Why would I think that?”
	“Two reasons. For one, despite what I was saying about cheese and lunches, I do take state money. Every dollar I can get.”
	“I don’t see anything wrong with that.”
	“No, and most people don’t. But from an ethical standpoint, I don’t agree with it. At the same time, we all do what we have to. The other thing is, I don’t want you to think I’m some sort of black militant. I don’t have a lot in common with those folks, as such. I mean, I support what they do, largely. But some of it is misguided, and they go about things in the wrong way. They’re just in a bigger trap and don’t know it.”
	“We don’t really have that back home,” I told her.
	“You likely will. But what I’m getting at, really, is that Maxxy’s father was white.”
	“For real?”
	“Yes. And she knows. In fact, she has a few memories of him, and I’m so glad for that. But your mom and aunt, or anyone in the family, doesn’t. I’d appreciate it if we kept that to ourselves.”
	“That’s your business.”
	“I’m glad you see it that way. I’m afraid they just wouldn’t understand. I know they wouldn’t, actually.”
	“What was he like?”
	“He was a great man, I can tell you that much. But married, for one thing. I didn’t care. We were very much in love. He was an engineer for Boeing-Lockheed, in fact. Brilliant. Maxine takes after him as least as much as she does me. And we were going to get married, although that was, and still kind of is, a great taboo to the world at large.”
	“What happened?”
	“A car crash basically destroyed all my dreams. When I became pregnant, he had started making arrangements to get divorced. We were going to get a place far from here. Alabama, maybe. NASA has some offices there.”
	“Fuck,” I said, and then became conscious of it. Auntie Til paid that no mind at all.
	“Part of me wants to believe it was just an accident, and maybe it was. But somewhere in me, there remains a suspicion that there was more to it. Because the defense industry is a monster. And if you shake the tree high enough, you’ll find the CIA behind it. It could have been related to that. Or it could have been racial. Or, like I said, it was probably just a thing that happened, with no real reason behind it.”
	“I’m sorry, Aunt Til…”
	“I had to move on. But I have never met a man to replace him. Lord knows I looked. Your mom and her sister think I like women, I’m sure. I never told them differently.”
	This was all a bit more of an adult conversation than I had ever been involved in.
	“What he did do, though, was set up a trust for Maxxy, even before she was born. He put a decent chunk of money into stocks for us. I get dividend checks. Well, she does. Which means my income is officially zero. So, I get my little government cheese, and I get to care for Maxine full time. Which is what he would have wanted.”
	“That’s real cool. I mean, not cool that he died, but… sweet.” 
	“Yes. It is, and he was. Anyway, I don’t mean to make things awkward. I just wanted you to know where your crazy Aunt Tilly was coming from.”
	I laughed, and the moment passed, ending on a lighter note.
	“Goodnight, Clarence.”
	“’Night, Til,” I said, using just her name, really for the first time. 
	She treated me like a grown man, and for the first time, I felt I was actually becoming one.

Chapter 12 – Bullet Points

	Sunday morning, was, like every day in L.A., a different day entirely. I awoke to my bedroom door being kicked in, and a gun in my face. 
	“Break yo’ self, fool!” 
It’s a good thing my own piece was buried in my closet. In later years, it would have been on the nightstand with one in the chamber. It was Maxxy, and the plastic gun was Han Solo’s. Shit looked real as hell, though. Like a tactical weapon of today, scope and shit.
	“Max, what the he-“
	“Shut the fuck up and get down on the goddamn ground!”
	“Maxxy!” Aunt Til yelled. “You said not until nine! It’s… eight forty-three!” Then she went back to sleep, I guess.
	“Education is no motherfucking joke!” To emphasize her point, she clocked me in the side of my forehead with her pistol. “Put your goddamn hands where I can motherfucking see them!” 
	Look. I could have gotten mad. I probably should have. I don’t know that anyone has ever put Maxine Jackson fully in check. It occurs to me now that maybe someone should have. I also think Aunt Til had some good ideas about childrearing, unconventional though they were. It’s hard to rectify the two thoughts. Maxxy was in many ways far more equipped for the real world than I was.
	Instead, I put my hands up. You know how little kids are, and she was still a little kid. I played along.
	“That’s real good, Daquan. Now don’t move, and maybe I won’t shoot you. Take your ass in the kitchen. We need coffee in here!” she added in a shout directed at her mother.
	“Goddamnit!” TIl said. But she came out in her robe and slippers anyway and started brewing a pot.
	“Sit the fuck down at the table and keep your hands where I can see them.”
	She went into her mom’s room and came out with two books, college algebra, the kind of hardback book that probably costs hundreds of dollars today, and what looked like some eight-grade math shit. She slammed them on the table, and then slid the kid one to me.
	She put hers in front of her chair, sat, and put the toy gun to the side. “Okay, good,” she said in a new, even voice that was an uncanny imitation of a real teacher, “Let’s begin, shall we?”
	“That wasn’t even that funny, Maxxy,” Til told her. Then they both started laughing. I was still a little hot, myself, and not the least of which was because of her treating me a like kid when it comes to math.
	“I only get one day of school a week,” she informed me. “I have to make the most of it.”
	“One day a week? What do you call what you do every other day?”
	“That’s just her playing. Unschooling. Whatever,” Aunt Til said.
	“Why one day a week, though?” 
	“That’s all she requires. How much more high-end math does she need to absorb at this age? It’s really just family time, but focused.”
	I couldn’t really argue with that. Maxxy learned more in a week undirected than we did in school in a month. I still thought of school in terms of sitting at desks for eight hours a day.
	“Plenty,” Max said. “Fourier transforms. Matrix theory…”
	“Which one?” Aunt Til asked her.
	“Both. Algebraic topology. I don’t even know calculus yet! I don’t know machine language. I’m falling behind!”
	“You have all week for that, if you want. It gives me a headache. Statistics was enough for me.”
	“Scrub-ass statistics. Damned lies and statistics!” Maxxy yelled, shaking her fist in the air.
	“Are you done?” Til asked her.
	“Fine. So, Clarence, I can’t help but notice you mad-dogging me over there.” Which was a wild exaggeration, but shows how perceptive she was. “Is there anything you want to discuss?”
	“I just think, terrifying wake-up call aside, this book is a year or so behind me, in school.”
	“I don’t expect you to start at the front. Work the last five pages, and we’ll get a new one. Mama still has all my old stuff.” 
	Aunt Til took that moment to put some notebooks and pencils on the table, and for a minute I thought they were working together on me, for some odd reason. But they weren’t.
