Just like I had done, she let Maxxy go and do what she was going to do, curious to see the results. Later on, she explained her philosophy in that regard a bit more. “If you had busted her in the mouth, I wouldn’t have have gotten mad at you. I’d just put some ice on her and tell her that’s what she gets for acting crazy. Anticipating the consequences of our own actions is paramount for survival.”
	I never heard people just casually talk about life in terms like that before. Survival seemed a given, even in Fifth Ward, for most people. But she was talking about a manner of thriving. In essence, a hot stove is the best way for a child to learn about avoiding flames. Those are the lessons that we take to the easiest, and stay with us the longest. 
	I put my head down and worked the last five pages of problems. Damned if it wasn’t harder than I thought. Maxine read, and seemed determined to ignore me until I was done. When I finally put my pencil down, she came and leaned over me, ran through the pages, and marked five wrong. This took her about a single minute.
	“Honestly, you passed. But just barely. It took longer than it should have, and you really got more of a B, were I grading this. And I am. I’d like to work through this book with you, and really make sure you have a good handle on all of it. It’s pre-algebra. The next level really is the one I’m working through.”
	She sounded more like a career counselor or some shit than a kid who still played with dolls. What could I tell her, anyway?
	“Okay.”
	“Good!” She pulled her chair next to me, and I started on chapter one while she worked on stuff that looked like a foreign language compared to what I was doing. A few times, she explained a concept more fully to me, gave some theory not in the text, or showed me a faster way of doing something.
	“Aunt Til,” I said. “Don’t you teach Maxxy?”
	“Teach her what? Not math, for sure. She tries to teach me. I don’t want to know any more. I can honestly say that differential equations will not factor heavily into the remainder of my life.”
	“Ten o’clock,” Maxine said. “Time for English”
	“What are we doing this week, Max?” Til asked her.
	She went to the bookshelf in her mother’s room and came back reading.
	“I saw the best minds of my generation destroyed by madness, starving hysterical naked,
dragging themselves through the negro streets at dawn looking for an angry fix, angelheaded hipsters burning for the ancient heavenly connection to the starry dynamo in the machinery of night, who poverty and tatters and hollow-eyed and high sat up smoking in the supernatural darkness of cold-water flats floating across the tops of cities contemplating jazz, who bared their brains to Heaven under the El and saw Mohammedan angels staggering on tenement roofs illuminated…”
	She went on like this at length for some minutes, and then passed the book to Til, who picked up where she left off, never missing a beat.
	“…who lounged hungry and lonesome through Houston seeking jazz or sex or soup, and followed the brilliant Spaniard to converse about America and Eternity, a hopeless task, and so took ship to Africa, who disappeared into the volcanoes of Mexico leaving behind nothing but the shadow of dungarees and the lava and ash of poetry scattered in fireplace Chicago, who reappeared on the West Coast investigating the FBI in beards and shorts with big pacifist eyes sexy in their dark skin passing out incomprehensible leaflets…”
	Not only was the wildest shit I had ever heard in my life, the part Til started with was weirdly appropriate in its mention of Houston and then the west coast. She did another long part, and when she put the book down in front of me, she put her finger where she had left off, and I did my best to pick it up there with the same flair that they had delivered their lines with.
	“…Pilgrim State’s Rockland’s and Greystone’s foetid halls, bickering with the echoes of the soul, rocking and rolling in the midnight solitude-bench dolmen-realms of love, dream of life a nightmare, bodies turned to stone as heavy as the moon, with mother finally fucking, and the last fantastic book flung out of the tenement window, and the last door closed at 4 a.m. and the last telephone slammed at the wall in reply and the last furnished room emptied down to the last piece of mental furniture, a yellow paper rose twisted on a wire hanger in the closet, and even that imaginary, nothing but a hopeful little bit of hallucination— ah, Carl, while you are not safe I am not safe, and now you’re really in the total animal soup of time—…”
	They had me read almost until the end, but for the last stanza, Maxxy took over for me. I didn’t know it beforehand, but she did so because the end was really difficult. It had a lot of loud lamentations about Moloch, which she explained to me about later. The performance she put into it at that point drove it home almost as well without me having a full understanding of what she was saying. I felt that shit. 
	By the end, I had learned everything, and nothing. If you had asked me what it was about, what the themes were, I couldn’t have told you. All I knew is that I had experienced it, and that experience left me slightly more fulfilled than before we had started. At the same time, I felt sadder. That is some heavy shit, able to make you feel things like that.
	“This has been a reading of Howl by Alan Ginsberg,” Maxxy said, bowing slightly.
	“Well done!” Til told her. “That was fun. The best reading yet, I think.”
	“You’ve done it before?” I asked them.
	“This is the fourth time…” Aunt Tilly said.
	“Still the best poem since The Odyssey,” Maxxy said.
	“It’s her favorite. Also, that’s entirely subjective, Maxine. But, yes, it’s a pretty great one, as poems go.”
	“Kahlil Gabran is bullshit,” Maxxy said.
	“He’s not that bad,” her mother opined.
	“A book you put on the back of your toilet, next to your seashell-shaped soaps, nestled smugly beneath an unread copy of ‘Jonathan Livingston Seagull’.”
	“That’s…pretty on the nose, actually.”
	“’The moving finger writes, and having writ, moves on.’ That’s the real shit.”
	“Agreed. How is Canterbury Tales? Still hate it?”
	“No. I got used to the fart jokes, the wiener jokes, and everything else, written in such an archaic language that the book creaks when I open it, and have to spend five minutes to decode a quatrain.”
	“Good. It sounds like you’re understanding it pretty well, then.”
	“James Joyce, now that guy could write a fart joke,” Maxxy added.
	“I don’t think that counts, if I’m thinking of what you’re thinking of. Chaucer isn’t that different from Shakespeare, in a number of ways. Look at how the style shifts from character to character.”
	“Mama, he’s no Shakespeare. Let’s just agree to disagree, here.”
	“How about finish that one, and you can pick twice in a row?”
	“Fine.”
	“No Allen Ginsberg.”
	“Damn it. Okay.” Maxxy let her features collapse for a moment to indicate her displeasure, and then said, “Welp, that’s school. See you guys next week!” She put the books away, mine included, and that was it. A little more than two hours on a Sunday was her official school time.
	Meanwhile, Aunt Til had made pancakes to go with the coffee everyone was drinking. I say everyone because Maxxy had a cup, too, and had refilled it twice. Only during school, apparently. She really didn’t need any energy boost anyway.
	Even Aunt Til pancakes weren’t just pancakes, as good as they can be. She had put cooked bacon slices in the batter, and two sunny-side-up eggs on top. I could eat that every day of my life. But it was never better than when Til did it. 
	When we were finishing up, Maxxy said, “Mama, did you get my colors and stuff like I asked you to?”
	“Oh. Yes. They’re in the trunk. I forgot. There’s two bags of dollar store stuff in there. Clarence, will you go get them with her?”
	She handed me her keys, and Maxxy ran out ahead of me. “They better not be melted!”
	I popped the trunk, and Maxine grabbed her shiny new box of 64 Crayolas. She headed back inside as I tried to carry both bags and close the trunk. But I’m glad she had. Because before I had quite reached the door, the bullets had started flyin’.	

Chapter 13 – Honeybear

	We got caught slippin’. I wasn’t in the habit of being hyper-aware of traffic, and Maxxy had no real reason to fear anything, either. I just heard someone yell, “Punk motherfucker!” and then three shots. Maxine screamed, and the car started accelerating away.
	I pushed in the front door, still holding the bags, somehow, and I heard breaking glass, and three more shots, louder, in rapid succession, from Aunt TIl’s room. I dropped what I was holding and ran in.
	Aunt Till was standing there with an AK, muzzle still sticking out the window.
	“Is everyone okay?” she said. Like she had to stop short while driving or something.
	Maxxy ran in with a non-toy pistol. And not the one I had jacked. A different pistol.
	“Motherfuckers!” she yelled.
	“Clarence, can I talk to Maxxy a minute?”
	I closed her door behind me and went into Maxxy’s room. I was shook.
	A few minutes later, they emerged. Maxxy was smiling. There were no guns. Like it never happened.
	We picked up the groceries, and she said, “Oh, no! Honeybear!”
	There was a hole through the bag, and the little plastic container of Sue-Bee honey.
	“I had to tell mama about the trip to the bodega,” she whispered to me, conspiratorially. “It’s no big deal. You may hear about it.”
	Aunt Til was already cutting out a piece of cardboard to cover the window she had broken. 
	“We can’t get a glass repairman out on a Sunday, I’m not even going to try.”
	She never did say a word about, well, anything, really. I guess not much needed to be said. I didn’t look at her the same after that. And the way Maxine brandished her weapon, I knew she would use it, given a reason. She carried it like she knew what she was doing. Which shouldn’t surprise me. When she tackled a subject, she mastered it. It was a real crazy day, but their immediate reaction went a long way toward normalizing it. Almost anyone else would have been flipping out.
	I was the only one that tried to talk about it.
	“Shouldn’t we call the police?”
	Aunt Til looked at me. “We don’t do that, here.”
	She transferred the honey she could save into some Tupperware after she patched the window, and set about washing the things that were in the bag. Meanwhile, my shock was turning into anger. All I could think was that I had that gun, and I knew what that dude looked like. I had to let it go, at least for the moment.
	At almost six, Maxxy turned on the TV and sat on the couch. “Mama, Muppet show!”
	“In a minute, baby. Clarence, come see.”
	I followed her out the back door, into the little fenced-in yard that I didn’t really know existed until then. I became aware of my lack of situational awareness. To my utter amazement, Aunt TIl pulled out a joint and a lighter, and sparked it up.
	“Here,” she said through the breath she held, and handed it to me. “Mostly I don’t, but when I do, it’s before the muppets are on. And Disney. Plus, today isn’t the average Sunday.”
	I nodded and passed it back. After a few more hits, she said, “I’m good, baby. Finish it if you want, and then come watch TV with us. We could all use a laugh.”
	After she went inside, I put it out, then stashed the rest in my room. I was learning more all the time about ole Aunt Til. The muppets were funny. I couldn’t bring myself to laugh too much, but I tried. The Disney was far less so, being one of those weird Witch Mountain movies. 
	We went to bed after that, and the next few days were a return to normalcy.
	The next day, Aunt till brought me out front. I was a little nervous, but she had no fear of anything, apparently. 
	“Take this tire off for me,” she said, unlocking the trunk and pointing at the front passenger side one. Then she went back inside.
	Now, this was some shit I should know how to do, but didn’t, fully. It took me a while. Once I figured out where the jack went and got the tire lifted, I tried to break the lugs loose, and the tire just spun. So, I had to drop it back down again, loosen them, and then raise it back up. But I got it done, and it was in a fairly reasonable amount of time. But since I didn’t know what else she wanted me to do, I went in and told her it was off.
	She followed me back outside. “Now put it back on again, please.”
	“Huh?”
	“You are going to meet your new employers tomorrow. They said as long as you can change a tire, they can use you.”
	“Heh. Well, damn. Thanks, then.”
	Aunt Til had applied the same sort of teaching method she used on Maxine to me, and it had worked. I put the wheel back on, dropped it again, tightened the lugs, and then put everything back in the trunk, suddenly a seasoned pro.
	“Nice work,” she told me. “I’m sure you’ll do fine. They’ll teach you whatever you need to know. Long sleeve shirt and pants. A half day of orientation tomorrow, noon to five.” 
	Once again, I found myself in an apprenticeship program for a trade. I didn’t know then that all the things I learned in both would eventually apply to the same job, but it did work out nice like that.	
	We went back inside, and I could finally try and relax. I didn’t sleep very well the night of the shooting, I’ll say that. 
	Man, I took that Iceberg Slim book, finally, and went out back. Neither Aunt Til or Maxxy came and bothered me. I stretched out on one of them long wooden chairs with the deep cushions, and smoked that joint down to the nub reading that thing. It opened with some of the bleakest quotes you could imagine, immediately destroying your notion of what pimping is. Then it breaks into the coolest, grittiest, jive storyline. I mean foul, from the get-go. Hard. Harder than L.A. and Houston put together. 
	This spoke to me in a way Langston Hughes or Alan Ginsberg never could. Of course, that was pretty much the sum total of poets I knew of. Edgar Allan Poe and shit. Who was cool. As artform, it was an epic book. What it showed about The Life was that it was fun to read about, but nothing I could tolerate. Every bit of it rubbed wrong against my being. I wanted the pussy and the money, that’s it.
	After that, though, it changed gears wildly again. Pages in, and I had already been whipsawed twice by this book. Thinking about it, the California indo probably added to my awe. But the language was raw and rich, and the pages were packed with crazy stories, all told rapid fire and poetic. It also involved prison and savagery. Even those parts were unfathomably cool. I think I read half the book that day. 
	When I went to bed that night, I laughed at myself. None of that pimp shit matters to me. I’m a welder. And maybe a mechanic, too. But it was still fun to read about. And I sure did want some pussy.
	Ain’t no pussy in an auto mechanic shop. The only women I got to see were a few ladies dropping their cars off, and that was at a distance. Plus, I ended up so oily, I wouldn’t want any to see me anyway, after a while. But the lack of distractions did allow me to focus well on the work. The owner cats were cool, two black dudes, these were the friends of Aunt Til. Then I met their mechanic, who they called Cooter. It was only years later that I realized it was after a character on a TV show.
	He was what you would call your southern California redneck. They definitely exist. But he was alright, and knew his shit. He also knew enough to teach me how to do the dirtiest parts of his job. During my ‘orientation’, I changed three tires, learned how to change oil, and swept the hell out of the shop. By the time Aunt Til came back, I was exhausted. Exhausted, but happy.
	After I had washed up and ate, there was a knock at the door. When I opened it, the cat who I had knocked out, and had then tried to shoot me, was standing there. On crutches. One eye was swole up big, with a bandage over it. His lips were busted, nose broken. He had a brace on his knee.
	“I’m sorry, man,” was all he said, never making eye contact, and then limped away again, having fulfilled whatever obligation someone had assigned him. Maxxy was sitting on the couch, and I saw her smiling a tiny bit, but neither she nor Aunt Til commented on the situation.
	All I knew, really, is that we never had any trouble like that again.	

Chapter 14 – Just A Good Ole’ Boy
	
	So, every morning, I would get up early with Aunt TIl, we’d have coffee and breakfast, and she would drop me off at work with a little lunch she had packed for me. It didn’t take too many days of that before I realized I loved the routine. It was a preview of what domestic bliss could be like, and I enjoyed it thoroughly.
	The way it worked on the job was that there were two bays. One had a space underneath where the cars went, with a ladder, and that was mostly my side. All the basic stuff was done there, the things I readily knew how to do. I got pretty fast at most of it, too. I was already pretty good with tools from my time doing maintenance, and I had plenty of practice during any given day.
	I also learned a bit about customer service, too. The owners made sure every vehicle that came through got their seventeen-point treatment. It didn’t matter if it was a vehicle inspection (I even did that, but Cooter had to sign the actual sticker), the windows were cleaned, fluids topped off, floorboards vacuumed. I even had to empty the ashtrays. It was a physical checklist, and the first few times, Coot doublechecked me on every item.
	Other than lunchtime, which was different for us mechanics than it was for customers, the cars in my bay were sporadic. During lunch, though, we both serviced the cars that lined up outside. The  rush of people trying to remove an item from their own personal checklists went on for about two hours. We worked as hard during those two as the rest of the day put together.
	When we weren’t swamped, though, and my side was empty, I got to assist Cooter with the more involved repairs. To me, he seemed like something of a savant. Every job was different. Even doing the same thing on two different cars wasn’t the same process. We changed starters and distributors. I learned how to gap spark plugs (and learned how damn car engines worked in the first place). Most of all, I think, I learned how to think logically and methodically about a problem. 
	I found out later that one of the reasons Maxxy was so adept at things, her natural intelligence aside, is that she had picked up a logical mindset as a result of teaching herself computer programming. In mechanic work, like in programming, there was a logical order to things. A flow. If you didn’t follow the steps in order, things either got harder, or didn’t work at all.
	Sometimes a bolt head would snap off or something. It was pretty much unavoidable. Cooter never got mad at it, or cursed, anything like that. He’d just have me get his little extraction toolkit, and he’d do whatever it took to get the broken piece out. Sometimes this took longer to do than the rest of the repair combined. If the more high-strung of the two owners ever came out and tried to get him to hurry up on something that had been taking a while, he would just stand there and listen, shutting down what he was doing. No matter what they told him, he would say, “It’ll be finished when I finish,” and then wait for them to leave again before he went back to it. I don’t know why they ever bothered trying to rush him, because all they ever did was slow him down.
	At the end of the first week at quitting time, Aunt Til picked me up with Maxxy and a fresh change of clothes for me. The owners had shook my hand, and given me a hundred-dollar bill for the week. Cash money. 
	We went and got ice cream again, and although I tried to pay, Aunt Til wouldn’t let me. “Save it,” she said. “We’re going clothes shopping next.”
	She took us to some little boutique place that was pretty far from home. All the stuff was really nice, though, and they left me to my own devices as they engaged in a mother-daughter spree of trying on clothes, critiquing each other’s choices, and trying to decide on what complimentary outfits to get.
	Meanwhile, I struggled with the entire concept of fashion. In the time they had taken to check out probably five outfits each and pick something out, I had assembled a single ensemble. When I went and tried it on and then walked out in it, Maxxy laughed and her mom shushed her.
	“Let’s use those pants, and try it this way,” Aunt Til suggested. She got me an undershirt, vest, and suit jacket that more fully went with them. Instead of a belt, she gave me suspenders. I have to admit, when I came out the second time, I looked pretty sharp. I even felt better wearing it. She topped it all off with a bow tie. I felt like a huge square between that and the suspenders. The dress clothes were bad enough, to me. Her judgment was sound, though. I had no idea just how sound until later.
	Even Maxxy gave me the thumbs up. When Aunt Til added a hat, handkerchief, and glasses (that were only for show and didn’t actually do anything), I felt like I was undercover. This wasn’t Clarence at that point, but some other cat entirely. I looked like a very upscale, church-going nerd. But a stylish one. That shit was thirty-eight dollars. This time, Aunt Til made no motion to pay, and she was silently teaching me another life lesson, there. Almost half of my money was gone, the same day I had gotten it. But I looked good, and more importantly, I felt good. And different.
	We hung our new clothes from the little hanger thing in the backseat, and piled in. I rode up front with Til, and she said, “Clarence, how do you feel about hibachi?”
	“What’s that?”
	“Oh, you are in for a treat,” she told me.
	“Where dinner is the show!” Maxxy yelled from the backseat.
	We pulled into a Benihana somewhere on the way back. I really had no idea what that place was like, at that point. It was packed, there were people with reservations, waiting. But at the sight of Aunt TIl, we were brought to a big stovetop table that was opened up just for us. I don’t think Til had reservations, either. They knew who she was, and was treated differently than the other patrons. Whatever was going on there, it was real cool. I thought maybe she was some kind of regular. And didn’t think a whole lot about it, anyway.
	It was hard to think about anything other than your surroundings, in there. Everywhere you looked, people were throwing food, chefs were making giant flames, or big walls of steam. Knives and hatchets were chopping food like a kung fu movie. Before we even had ordered something to drink, I was captivated by all the activity. My eyes didn’t even stay in one place for too long before something new got my attention, often punctuated with a cheer from a table of patrons.
	It got even better when a guy rolled a little cart in, loaded with food. He didn’t talk to us, ask what we wanted, anything. He just went to work cooking. But the way they cooked wasn’t just cooking. It was part juggling, part daredevil, and pure showmanship. Dude made, like, a train out of rice. But it didn’t just have steam coming out of it, it moved on the stovetop. A lot of what he did was so fast, you couldn’t even follow what he was doing. 
	What he was also really doing was keeping our attention as the food was being cooked, so that we hardly noticed that part. Before I knew it, he had dumped a big pile of rice on there, warmed it, and put it on our plates along with a gang of shrimp. It was only then that I realized how hungry I was.
	In a very Maxxy-like way, she turned the tables on the cook. She picked up a shrimp, and made a motion with it to him. He opened his mouth, she tossed it at him, and he caught it! We weren’t the only table cheering that move, even. He refried noodles, and cooked beef medallions, scallops, all kinds of stuff. We just kept eating, watching, and laughing until we couldn’t do it anymore. 
	Then he boxed up the rest, gave a little bow…and left. It was a whole different level of cool that guy operated on. Maxxy yelled out something that I assumed was Japanese, but I later learned was Korean. Hearing her, he turned and smiled, and blew her a kiss. She swooned.
	“Ready, kids?” Aunt Til asked. Then we just got our boxes and walked out. I figured she had paid with a credit card or something. Let me just say right here and now, whatever it was, it wasn’t no dine and dash shit. Don’t get it twisted.
	We laughed and cut up all the way home. It might have been the single happiest moment in my young life. 
	“I love you guys,” I said, overcome with emotion, and unable to contain myself.
	“We love you, Clarence,” Aunt TIl said. That did my heart so much good.
	“Yeah,” Maxxy said. “You big scungilli-head.”
	We actually went home and watched Johnny Carson with Maxxy. Aunt Til was drinking a beer, and I went to the fridge and popped the top on one myself. She didn’t even appear to notice. Goddamnit, Aunt Til was so cool. I’m crying right now and shit. This is embarrassing. I miss her so bad. 	

Chapter 15 – Hue-Colored Glasses

	Even though I was dog-tired some days after work that week, I had read. Sometimes only a page or two, some nights a full chapter. It was a combination of me loving the Iceberg Slim book, and Maxine’s influence. Other than the occasional TV show or the odd, random instances of her playing with actual toys, it was what she did. Aunt Til read far more than anyone else I had known up to that point, too, but I think in a sense, I was trying to keep up with Maxxy. She was family, and I loved her, but at the same time, it works on you, having someone half your age who is twice as smart and educated as you are.
	By Saturday afternoon, I had finished reading the pimp book, though. Aunt Til was out doing Aunt Til shit, and I went back to The Art of War. It had a whole new context, given the events that had transpired, and what I had absorbed about The Life. The more I read it, the more I related it to what I had just finished reading. There were a lot of parallels with the two, but a gulf of worlds existed between them, at the same time.
	It occurred to me to ask Maxxy about the gun she had ran up into her mother’s room with, since we had the house to ourselves.
	“What gun?” she said, not for the first time.
	“C’mon, Maxxy.”
	“Snitches get stitches, cousin,” she told me. And it wasn’t little girl Maxxy saying it. She was serious. I let it rest.
	Aunt Til came home not too long after that, looking a combination of tired and elated. Years later, I came to understand that it’s what a woman looks like after a particularly rewarding sexual encounter. But you never think of your family members as people who do that sort of thing. 
	“I’m sorry, kids. I ran late.” She offered up a bag of McDonald’s burgers and fries in compensation for a lack of dinner, and that suited Maxxy and I just fine.
	At ten-thirty, Aunt Til put the TV on NBC, and we watched a new show. It was the debut of Saturday Night Live. Til laughed at everything far more than we did, and it was good to see her so happy. But when it was over, she uncharacteristically told us it was bedtime.
	“Church in the morning, remember?” she told a protesting Maxine.
	“How could I forget?” she said.
	The next morning, I awoke to the smell of coffee to find the two of them already dressed. Til looked amazing in her Sunday best, and Maxine the child equivalent. I sipped a little coffee, something that was now becoming a habit, and then got dressed myself.
	I hadn’t asked anything about where we were actually going, but it was a long drive, and we ended up in the same area we had dropped Maxxy’s friend off, eventually pulling into a huge lot outside of a place that looked part cathedral and part skyscraper. Up to that point, if you said church, I would have thought of some non-descript V.F.W. hall sort of place, or maybe some tiny wooden building in the country.
	This was the opposite of that in every way. The lot was full of Mercedes and shit like that, too. It looked like my dream of a car dealership. Til’s humble vehicle was way out of place. When we got out, Maxxy said, “Bye!” and tried to take off ahead of us.
	“Maxine! Get back here!” Til yelled at her, and she stopped and waited for us. “We’re a family,” she reminded her, “and we’re coming here as a family.”
	“Fine.”
	But when we got closer to the front doors, we were greeted with a chorus of squeals as she and her friends spotted each other. This time, Maxxy ran off, and Aunt Til let her go. Instead, she and I went inside.
	“They have some class thing for kids, here,” she told me. “The only way Maxine could sit still for adult services would be if I let her bring a book.”
	Inside, it was festive. My only experiences with churches were funerals, and the occasional holiday where my mom took me for some reason that I had never understood. Here, though, it was like Easter Sunday, except nowhere near as solemn. People milled about, talking and laughing, and it felt far less formal than I had expected. Everyone looked amazing, dressed to the nines. You could almost smell the money, there. At the same time, everyone was very down-to-earth. 
	There were hot-ass women as far as the eye could see. 
	The church, Aunt Til had told me later, was what was called Unitarian. She explained a little to me about it, and it really was a little cooler than your regular sort of church. They didn’t just talk some game about universal values or whatever, they practiced it. It was also much more youth-oriented.
	The upshot of all this is, I had died and gone to heaven. Far from being a white church, or a black church, as I was accustomed to, this was the International House of Pancakes of churches. It was probably largely white, by the numbers, but there were Black girls, Asian girls, Latinas. All looking more beautiful than I could have imagined. Suddenly, I had no game whatsoever. I found that all I could do was soak it in and dream.
	After a few minutes during which Aunt Til was greeted by some people she knew, we all took seats and listened to the sermon, sang a few songs, and split out again after forty-five minutes. It was a far cry from my Easter and Christmas experiences, which had seemed to drag on forever as a kid. The minister’s talk was a bit slicker and, I don’t know, progressive, but to me, it was just the same old bullshit repackaged for a different market.
	As a social event, though, it had enormous value. Aunt Til talked to a few more folks she recognized, or had recognized her, and I just stood by politely and looked around in wonder. It was like starving, and looking at food behind glass that you couldn’t actually get to.
	Eventually, we left the main building, and went to the little side ones where Maxxy and her friends had spent their time. Sarah was there, which shook me the tiniest bit. She was already hard to take, but encountering her in that environment made things even more uncomfortable. Lucky for me, Maxxy had apparently given her a talking to, and she didn’t act the same way she had before.
	“Noooo! Mama, I don’t want to go yet!” Maxxy protested.
	“Before, you didn’t want to come,” her mother said. “Besides, we’re going to Waffle House…”
	Even that wasn’t enough to convince Maxine. It was the first time that I realized that she read and did the things she did, in part, because she didn’t have anything else to do. 
	“Can we just wait until Sarah leaves? Pleeeeese?”
	Til sighed. “Okay.”
	“Yay!”
	So, we sat in some plastic chairs and waited a few minutes, while Maxxy, Sarah, and a few others chattered on and laughed. Eventually, Sarah’s parents walked in, and Til stood up, so I followed suit. She and the woman gave each other a kind of awkward hug, and I shook the man’s hand as she introduced us.
	“Sarah’s father owns the Benihana we ate at the other night,” she told me. And another number in a combination lock clicked into place. 
	They spoke for a little while, but no one motioned to go. “We’re waiting on Hue,” the mother said. “She is signing up for a mission trip in the fall.”
	“Oh, that’s great!” Til said. “Where to?”
	“Egypt, of all places.”
	“Interesting!”
	Then Hue walked in. My breathing stopped. Maybe my heart did, too, I don’t know. All I know is that she was a vision. Far more lovely than even her mother, who was beautiful in her own right, this girl literally took my breath away. And she walked right up to me.
	“You must be Clarence,” she said.
	“What? I mean, yes, hi.” I stuck out my hand, and she shook it, which was somehow awkward as hell, but also the right thing to do. I remember how her hand felt in mine, to this day. 
	“Sarah wouldn’t stop talking about you.”
	“Hahaha,” I said. “Yeah, she’s great.”
	Hue’s smile indicated that she knew a little more than she was copping to.
	“Okay, Maxxy,” Til said. “Let’s go. You promised.”
	Both families walked out together, and we said our goodbyes in the parking lot.
	In the car, Aunt Til said, “Well, that wasn’t too bad, was it?”
	“It was great!” Maxxy said. 
	“If I had listened to you, we never even would have come,” she chided her.
	We drove to breakfast on the way home, but my head was in the clouds. I think maybe you could say I developed a fetish that day. I didn’t know what that shit was back then, of course. But maybe call it a preference. A strong interest in women of the Asian variety. Most of all, in Hue herself.
	Going to church didn’t change Maxxy’s Sunday program, though, it only delayed it. When we got back home, we all changed, and then she broke out the books. I think she really enjoyed being in charge of things, and schooling me was a way to exercise that a little bit. This time, it was almost all math. We worked through pages for nearly two hours, and I started to gain a deeper understanding of it all than I had when I studied things in school. 
	Maxine didn’t let the occasion pass by without a little English, though. When we were done, she read me a shorter piece that wasn’t performance art like the last poem. It was Robert Frost’s “The Road Not Taken”. 
	I mean, it was cool. It didn’t speak to me in a way that the Ginsberg did, for sure. The imagery was northern and wintery, and that was an alien world compared to both the south and L.A. It was kinda… white people shit. The ending, though, made up for a lot. Even at my age, I knew what he was talking about. At least I thought I did. 
	“A very good reading, Maxine,” her mother said. Aunt Til never held back on praise when it was justified, and she also didn’t say something was good when it wasn’t. The crayon drawings on the refrigerator were curated. “So, did you know, one time, there was a gathering of English lit people, poets, and the like, and they were arguing about what Frost was saying in that poem. And Robert Frost himself stood up in the crowd, and told them, ‘That’s not what I meant at all.’”
	“Hahaha,” Maxxy said. “I like that. You know who I wish had written more poetry?”
	“Who?”
	“Bertrand Russell. That Frost story reminds me of when the lady told him ‘It’s turtles all the way down.’”
	I had absolutely no idea what they were talking about.	
“Ah. Well, the one I did read from him was beautiful, you’re right. He was more of a mathematician and philosopher, though. Did you know he was friends with T.S. Eliot?”
	“I did not,” Maxxy said. “Interesting.”
	“Eliot even wrote a poem about him. It’s called ‘Mr. Apollinax’.”
	“Oh my god, do you have it? Why have you withheld this information from me my entire life, mother?”
	“No, I don’t think I do. Put it on your list for when we go back to the library. I think you’ll like it. It’s catty. Apparently, Russell rented him and his wife a room, and was sleeping with her.”
	Aunt Til was the only person I ever knew who was so matter of fact about adult matters with kids. Of course, Maxine was the only sort of little kid I knew who was mature enough for that anyway. The statement required no further elaboration.
	“Poets…” Maxxy said. “Always the drama.”
	“That’s right,” Til said. “Stay in school, don’t do poetry.”
	Maxine laughed, and even I had to smile at that one.

Chapter 16 – Babygirl

	I didn’t go back to school, obviously. Aunt Til handled whatever needed to be done with the state so we wouldn’t be harassed, and I guess my mom did something in Houston to indicate I was in L.A. Whatever it was, it worked out pretty well.
	Work continued, I studied a bit so I didn’t fall behind, and I read. That was life in L.A. But we did keep going to church weekly, and it’s crazy, but that’s what I looked forward to most, much more so than payday. I think we all know why.
	The next time Maxxy went to the library, Hue was there…
	Man, that was a great day. When I saw her, I knew she was there to see me. I walked into the kid’s section, and she was curled up with her legs on her chair, book in lap, with glasses on. She looked down over them at me, and said, “Hello, Clarence…” 
	Sarah must have taken her seductions lessons from her big sister, because lord, at that moment, I would have given anything for her. But she wasn’t practicing, nor was she cattin’. It was something in-between. Whatever it was, I was the grateful beneficiary. It was different, with her. I mean, I wanted her. Bad. I can’t stress that enough. At the same time, it was enough to just spend time with her. 
	That’s what we did. We enjoyed each other’s company, and got to know each other. I never thought that when faced with someone I wanted with every ounce of my being (which had also never happened before) I would find myself entirely contented with just having them in the same room with me. The talking and smiling were just bonuses. Went Aunt Til finally came and got us, without saying so, I knew Hue would be back the next time, too. 
Plus, we had church. The next time we went, she sat next to me, and all of the nonsensical promises of heaven suddenly seemed real. I became an avid churchgoer, and probably for the least pious reason anyone could ever have. It wasn’t just Hue, but both families, minus the youngsters, every week after that. It made things a little more awkward than they would have been otherwise, but I was not complaining. Everyone involved seem to approve of our friendship, which is all it really was, so that helped.
I didn’t want to be just friends, though, and neither did Hue.
One unexpected side effect of my job was that I learned to drive. I would pull cars out of bays, I would park them, I would pull them in for me and Cooter to work on. It wasn’t long before it was a casual thing, for me. When I turned sixteen, Aunt Til took me to get a learner’s permit.
Other than trying to stay fresh looking for Hue, my expenses were nil. Consequently, I had slowly stacked almost three thousand dollars, at that point. I tried to give Til money, but she wouldn’t accept it. I did send my mom five hundred, though, once I had a nice little bank for myself. It was the only time she called me the whole time I was there. 
The other thing I did was buy Maxxy her printer. Plus, this thing she called a memory expansion that she really wanted. That felt good. It seems odd to me now that Til had never done it. I don’t think money was the issue. More than likely, knowing her, there was some bigger plan at work. Something to do with not spoiling her completely, or making her work toward a goal. 
Maxine was overjoyed, though. Her focus shifted away from fiction and poetry a bit, until half the books she got each month were on computer science topics. Kid tried to show me what machine language was, and it looked harder than Chinese arithmetic. Even with me studying algebra, what she was doing was beyond what I was capable of comprehending. 
Her little joke conversational program she had showed me early on grew into a thing that was capable of a fairly realistic dialogue between a human and a computer, as long as you played along with it. It was all smoke and mirrors, she told me. But it was impressive.
One night, Aunt Til sat me down at the table to talk.
“There are two things I’d like to see you do soon, Clarence,” she told me.
“Sure.” I didn’t know where she was going with this, but I was already willing to do anything Til asked of me.
“One, I’d like to see you get your G.E.D. It’s fine that you’re not going to school, and working. Plus, you’re still studying. I love that. But if you ever end up wanting to go to college, or maybe get a different sort of job, you’ll need one.”
“That sounds good.”
“I looked into it. You could probably do the whole thing on a Saturday afternoon. In and out. One and done.”
“Am I ready, though?”
“You are. You were probably ready when you got here. Standards, I’m sorry to say, are low. That’s not commentary on you, just the facts. Maxxy could get her G.E.D. if they would let her.”
“Cool. I think I’d like having one, really.”
“Great! The other thing is, I think we should get you a car.”
“Wow. Now you’re talkin’.” My head was already filling with visions of taking Hue out somewhere for dinner and a movie. Anywhere but a Hibachi joint.
“I thought you’d like that. Which is why I saved that for last. In fact, if you had resisted my suggestion of a diploma, I wouldn’t be bringing a car up.”
“I would do a whole lot more than that for a ride.”
“Well, as long as you’re committed, we don’t have to do it in that order. Your word is good with me. We can get a car for you, and you can drive yourself to get your G.E.D.”
“That sounds incredible.” And it really did. Not just because of my designs on Hue, but just the whole thing of reaching a new level of achievement and freedom. And responsibility. Every milestone like that made me feel a little more capable and adult. I don’t think I would be the same person now if I had never left Houston. No, I know I wouldn’t. If I had even made it out of there alive.
“That’s nice to hear, because your Auntie Til likes sleeping in, when Maxxy lets me. I have a friend who has a car he’s selling, if you’d like him to bring it over so you can take a look.”
“Really?” I didn’t care what it was. It was a car, and that’s as far as my vision extended.
“Of course. I think you know me well enough. If I’m lyin’, I’m dyin’.”
“I can’t wait.”
I didn’t have to wait long. Til took me out into the neighborhood, with me behind the wheel. It was pretty basic traffic, stop signs, speed zones. But it was my first venture into real world driving, and she said I did well.
Two days later, after she picked me up from work, there was a car in the driveway when we got home. But it wasn’t just any car. It was a blue ’63 Impala. Ragtop. Candy paint. Leather seats. A little steering wheel made out of chain. It was fresher than anything I had seen in L.A., and they were serious about their cars there. I was in love. Like if Hue was a car. It was deep and immediate. But my doubts immediately set in. 
“Aunt Til. I can’t afford this.”
“You’d be surprised,” she told me. “Never say never. I can probably get it for five thousand. You put in three, I’ll kick in a thousand. And we pay the rest over time.”
“Would they do that?” I didn’t try and think about how even five thousand wasn’t enough for a car like this. And I didn’t know at the time how customized it actually was. She could have thrown in the deed for the Brooklyn Bridge at that point, and I would have signed on the dotted line.
“I told you, it’s a friend’s. He’s a good friend.”
“Who do I have to shoot?” I said. She laughed. But it was a self-conscious laugh.
“Just make the payments and keep doing good. Auntie Til will handle the rest.”
I think y’all know by now how much I had come to love my aunt. But at that moment, she was queen of the world. An empress. Goddess.
“I won’t let you down.”
“I know you won’t. Want to drive it?”
“Now?”
“The keys are on the table. You have your permit. Why not?”
“Hell, yes. Please.”
We went inside, and when she handed them to me, said, “Just make the block a few times. You know what streets to avoid.”
And I did. L.A. operated off of a different sort of color code than the black, white, and brown of Houston. I knew enough that the color of your car mattered in some places. At the very least, I didn’t want a repeat of what had happened to me and Maxxy. I nodded and kept listening, taking things as seriously as I could, despite the fact that I wanted to run back to the driveway.
“Keep the top and the windows up, for now. Doors locked.”
“Jackers,” I said.
“That’s right. Go slow. But not too slow. Watch out for little kids. Kee-“
“I’ll do right, Aunt Til. I promise.”
“Okay, then. If you’re not back in twenty minutes, I’m coming look for you…”
“Yes, ma’am.” That old, southern honorific made its first appearance in L.A. almost since I had been there. That’s how solemn an occasion it was to me.
“Have fun…” she said. And I was out the door.
Man, I got in it and just sat there, stunned. But it was hot, so I rolled down my window and started it. Air conditioning! It blew hard and cold, too. Plus, a damn Alpine radio with a tape deck. When I had steadied my nerves enough, I rolled the window back up and put it in reverse. 
I was in the habit of zipping around in the cars pretty fast at work, but it was just on a tiny lot, and we had stuff to do. Suddenly, I was an old lady driver, on my own for the first time. I looked both ways twice, adjusted my mirror, and let it drift off the driveway via gravity alone. When I did apply the gas, it was lightly. But it was all good. The street was pretty low traffic. I shifted into forward, and I was off on my own for the first time. Basically, in my dream car. My dreams were kind of pedestrian, back then.
I decided to take the only route I knew by heart, which was to the nearest little store. Lucky for me that the drive was compatible with the car’s color. Which sounds like a ridiculous thing to say. At the same time, if you’ve been there, you know that shit is real as hell. 
	I made it there okay, but it was already getting attention. Cat’s heads turned when I drove past, and their eyes followed me up the block. When I pulled into the parking lot, I couldn’t bring myself to actually get out. I put it in park, though, and turned on the stereo.
	It was loud as hell. Too loud, for me. But it sounded clean, too. I had to turn it down, but the people in the store noticed, even. That’s not the crazy part, though. It was a tape by a cat named Curtis Mayfield. The song was ‘Superfly’. This was some player’s anthem shit. Between that and the ride, I felt like Superman.
	Then I noticed the switches. 
	I had never seen anything like it in a car at work, for sure. It was a lot of Toyotas, Fords, stuff like that, there. My curiosity got the better of me. I pushed the first one up, and the fuckin’ whole car raised up by six inches. Suddenly I was in Chitty Chitty Bang Bang or some shit. I laughed out loud. When I pushed it down, it returned to normal.
	I mean, the only thing I could do at that point was to push it down again. When I did, it sank to the asphalt. Fuck Chitty Chitty Bang Bang. I was James Bond. Luke Skywalker. I was in a lowrider hovercraft. I laughed again, unable to even believe what was happening, and did it a few more times. And probably would still be doing it, if I hadn’t heard a knock on my window.
	“Hey, daddy,” a voice said.
	Man, this girl was fine. If we’re just comparing stats, she made Hue look like a damn boy. All tits and ass, separated by an hourglass waist. She was wearing a velvety looking track suit, but the top was a long-sleeve half-shirt, and the waistline was rolled down. She had long, straight hair that went past her shoulders. That wasn’t even common, then. Shit, even now, it’s mostly fake. Her skin was a beautiful caramel color, just creamy and perfect. Her face was sweet as hell to match, with smiling, full lips. The herringbone necklace she wore over her top was definitely real, too, and probably a half inch wide. She had a tattoo across her stomach that said ‘Babygirl’…
	This is what a fool I was: I put it into reverse, and gave her a wave as I pulled out. She was a sure thing, one way or another, if not my first trick, had I played my cards right. And I ran away like she had pulled a gun on me. I didn’t even care. When I was rolling again, it was the furthest thing from my mind. It was only later that I kicked myself. I had the look, but I was turning fully into a square.   	
	 I worried more about getting back home in time.
	I turned the music back up some, and if you’re waiting for me to do something stupid, this is it. I hit the second of the four switches. I don’t know what I was thinking. Clearly, I wasn’t thinking at all. Just high on the whole scene. The right front tire lifted off the ground. I looked at it in amazement, and watched in slow motion as I drifted off to the right and hit a trashcan on the curb. I hit the brakes just in time to not knock it over.
	I can see the whole scenario now as it could have played out. Knock over the trash can (it was plastic, at least, so the car wasn’t hurt), get out like a fool to pick it up, leave the door open, car running, lose my five-thousand-dollar dream vehicle that isn’t even paid for. As we all know, when things get too good for too long, something bad is bound to happen. But I wasn’t thinking that way. I was on top of the world, and nothing would ever bring me down.

Chapter 17 – When in Spain

Chapter 18 – Inscrutable

	By Sunday, I had forgotten about anything but Hue. All it took was me seeing her again. She asked me what we were doing Friday, and I had to think fast. “Minigolf and ice cream,” I said.
	“Yay!”
	Minigolf and ice cream were all I could afford, at that point, so I was glad she was enthused. The work week went by in slow motion, and at the same time, effortlessly. I don’t know how it is for women, but for me, at least, when you’re in love like that, there is no labor or challenge that you can’t handle with a smile on your face. Everything is lighter and easier; the air is cleaner. Things even smell and taste differently. 
	The second date was even better. No incidents, everything was just fun as hell. There was a point when I stood behind Hue and helped her make a hole in one, and it was somehow more intimate than anything I had ever done with a girl in Houston. And I had done plenty. But there was never any real closeness there. Not like this.
	Our basic routine went on for months. We got closer, in a lot of ways. In some ways I am not going to sit here and tell Janice about. Honestly, not quite as close as all that. But a reasonable substitute. (You are rather reticent for a pimp and partial owner of an adult film company… – Janice.)
	One Friday before our now regularly scheduled dates, I had finally paid my car off. Even though the title had long since been in my name, it was still an amazing feeling. The only thing better was Hue. Maxxy presented me with a sheet of paper. “I need two copies of this book when you go out tonight, please. Any major bookseller should have it.”
	“Why two copies?”
	“I have my reasons.”
	I didn’t think much about it. Maxxy was a weird kid, but requesting a book was the most natural thing in the world, with her. It would have been stranger for her to not ask for a book, at times. This wasn’t the first time it had happened. Two copies was odd, to me, but not exactly very note-worthy.
	So that night, it became part of our date. In fact, it mostly was the date. I shouldn’t have been surprised that Hue took to the bookstore in the same way Maxxy would have. We browsed for a while, and she told me about ones she had read, ones she wanted to read. The one she picked out was Foundation, by Isaac Asimov. She said it was really more about psychology, but that the author was a biochemist himself.

	“Your guess is as good as mine,” I told her. It was thick, and, yeah, it was probably a lot to take in even for an adult. But we all know Maxxy was different.
	On a whim, I went to the “S” section. To my surprise and delight, there was another Iceberg Slim book that I hadn’t known about. I don’t know why, but I thought he was some sort of one-off author. Because, she said, she was in an advanced program there, and her parents didn’t want her to burn out. I was wondering what it was like to have parents like that. But then I thought of Aunt Til, and it was exactly the sort of thing she would do. So, I kind of knew.
	We stayed until closing time. After that, we drove to a park and did the things we usually did before I dropped her off at home. (Made fondue? – Janice.) 	“Aww. You’re so sweet.” She kissed me once more, and went inside.

They called her Nu-Nu at home. More confusing to me was that Nu meant ‘girl’ in Vietnamese.), Aunt Til said, “So, getting excited about Stanford?” to Hue.

	Back at home, after loading up at Waffle House as was our routine, Maxxy announced a change in plans. “Today,” she said, “I am teaching you my unbreakable cipher.” She put the two copies of the book I had gotten her in front of us, along with two notepads and pencils.
	“What’s that?”
	“A variation on a classic encryption technique, made better by yours truly.”
	“First lesson, then. What the hell is encryption?”
	“Encoding. Disguising a message.”
	“Spy shit.”
	“Exactly, cousin! Spy shit.”
	“To what end, though?”
	“Because I can. Actually, it has applications in data processing. But also, hope for the best, and prepare for the worst.”
	Aunt Til was cooking dinner, and chimed in, “If you want peace, prepare for war.”
	“So, this book can teach me about encryption?”
	“Ha! No. That’s one thing it can’t do. But you’re going to read it.”
	“What’s it about?”
	It was called “Atlas Shrugged”, and it was thick as hell.
	“Oh, all sorts of things. But the main premise is deciding things for yourself. I can’t tell you what it’s about. That is open to interpretation.”
	“And misinterpretation…” Til added.
	“I chose it as the basis for our lesson because it’s so long.” She opened her notebook, and tore out a sheet of paper. It had a page full of numbers, all in one big block. “This is the message,” Maxxy said. “No one could ever crack except for you and me.”
	“And me,” Til said.
	“Right. Because mama knows what the seed book is. And she helped me write the cipher.” 
	I looked at the page she had slid over to me. It was definitely incomprehensible. A lot of zeroes, and random looking numbers, with no breaks in between anything. It looked impossible to figure out, to me, anyway.
	“This is a time-honored technique. Each of those numbers refers to a word on a page. Decode it, and you will reveal a sentence. If you don’t know what book was the basis for the message, the numbers have no meaning. Even a different version of the book will render it useless.”
	“So, all you have to do is keep the source secret,” I said.
	“Ordinarily. But I made mine even harder.”
	She explained her process to me. The zeroes were used to pad out the lengths, which broke each word down into a ten number block. That part was easy enough to understand. But even the order of the page number and the word number could be reversed. Whatever the pattern of the first word was would be reversed on the second one, and then kept alternating like that. So even if you had all the information I had, there would be two possible messages, and one would be gibberish.
	“That is way too complicated,” I said.
	“That’s why it’s so good! Anyway, try it…”
	I made some marks between the blocks of ten letters on the first few lines so I could make sense of them. When I looked up the first word, it was ‘the’, and I wrote it down in my notebook.
	“Good!” Maxxy said. “Now work it the other way.”
	I tried that, and got ‘the’ again.
	“It’s the same word.”
	“Ha! I did that on purpose. That way you can’t even figure out the pattern from the first word.”
	“It’s not perfect,” Aunt Til said. “That book isn’t the best choice, because the page numbers exceed the possible word counts. Ideally, you would have an eight-digit pattern, and a four-hundred-page book or whatever, so the two numbers stay in a more similar range.”
	“Don’t take this away from me, mama.” Maxxy said.
	“Just an observation. That’s how you could improve on it.”
	Aunt Til was wild. Any other parent would be baffled, or piling on the praise, in the usual best case scenario. Til was picking apart Maxxy’s system and telling her it could use some work.
	“Noted. But nevertheless, this is the seed book for us. Clarence, please continue.”
	I set about figuring out the second word. The first pass, I got the word ‘a’, and the other attempt resulted in the word ‘person’. Based on that, I decided what the pattern was and decoded the rest of the sentence. Before I was all the way done, I saw that the message was uniquely Maxxy’s.
	“The person reading this is a butt,” I finally said.
	“Hahahaha!”
	It was pretty funny. “That was a long way to go for a joke.”
	“The best kind of joke, I think. But you have to admit, it’s a good system.”
	It really was. I didn’t see how anyone would ever need it, but as a secret code went, it was pretty solid.
	“Class dismissed,” Maxxy said, cutting our day short. “Good work, cousin.”
	I went back in my room and read more of my new Slim book. For dinner, we had short ribs that had been cooked down all day, and some loaded baked potatoes. In the course of conversation, Til said, “So the Nguyens are having a family dinner Tuesday after next to celebrate Hue’s college acceptance, and they’ve invited us.”	
	“That’s really nice,” I said. And it was. It kind of made us family.
	“The semester starts in two weeks…” Aunt Til said.
	“Cool,” I said, continuing to stuff my face.
	“Clarence, Stanford is by San Francisco.”
	The color probably drained from my face when she told me that. Maybe Hue had assumed I knew. But she never said she was going away. I figured she was going to live at home, or in a dorm nearby. That nothing would ever change.
	I couldn’t have been more wrong.

Chapter 19 – What The Pho

	The next couple weeks were hard, for me. Mostly because nothing had changed, and everything had changed. I still saw Hue twice a week, and everything was as great as it had always been. But she acted like nothing was happening. Meanwhile, my little world was shattering. The only time I wasn’t kind of morose was when I was with her, and it took a lot of conscious effort on my part to hide my feelings on the matter.
	I read the Hustling book with an intensity that even the Iceberg books had failed to generate. In part because it was from Hue, and thus a part of her, somehow. But also, because it showed me an entirely different level of the game.
	The difference between it and Slim’s take on The Life was as different as black and white, if you’ll forgive me. The author had spent time with a Jewish New York pimp named David, and interviewed him extensively. He had a stable of upper-class whores, and as a result of his efforts, paid no rent on his condo and nothing for the limos that they rented nightly. Not that the girls paid for it, but that he used them as the payment themselves.
	Their nights consisted of clubbing and money-making. He even described picking up other women to party with them, and making bank without them ever knowing they had been pimped out. That is some deep game. In other words, it had changed my view of that lifestyle entirely. It had none of the ugly aspects of Iceberg Slim’s take. No violence, police, shit like that. Just pure hustling. It opened my eyes, man. Opened them wider, I should say.
	I did my best to keep my feelings to myself. Not just around Hue, but at home with Til and Maxxy. I thought I did pretty good, but inside, I was a mess. Love hurt a lot more than I had ever thought it could. That last Sunday at church was one of the hardest things I had ever done.
