	So I did, even though I wasn’t feeling it at all. Imagine my surprise when we pulled up at the hibachi place… That just made me feel so much worse. It was kind of a slap in the face, and I really thought Til was more sensitive than all that.
	They ushered us in to the big cooking surface tables that Maxxy loved, but before I sat down, Til said, “Over there…” and pointed to the far corner of the booths on the other side of the restaurant. I could see Hue sitting down facing in the other direction. My heart tried to climb out of my throat. I didn’t say anything, just went to her.
	When I walked up to the table, she said, “Clarence!” and jumped up to hug me. I squeezed her so tight, I’m surprised I didn’t hurt her. Then I started sobbing.
	She just held me. She didn’t care who was looking, where we were. She just held me and I got it all out. When I finally stopped, she wiped my tears away with a little handkerchief. It was a pretty beautiful thing. She did understand, and she did care. That was something. She slid back into the booth, and I tried to sit opposite of her, but she said, “No. Sit next to me…”
	This time, she ordered for me. She knew what the best plates were there, of course. Over dinner, she finally did talk about Stanford with me. Not about school, but about how she was going away, and how that had been her parents’ plan long before I had ever come along. She said she had even tried to change schools to UCLA. Her father had listened, even if he hadn’t been very enthusiastic about it. Her mother, though, had put her foot down. 
	One thing she didn’t talk about, though, was me coming visit her. Five hundred miles wasn’t really all that far. I didn’t understand why it never came up, and I didn’t say anything about it either, as a result. My heart was already stretched thin enough as it was.
	We talked and ate, though, and it was still nice. A real bonus, seeing her like that, and at least sort of putting some sort of closure to things. In fact, we talked so much, that I hadn’t even noticed the time. Aunt Til and Maxxy walked up to get me, and when I looked around, the restaurant was empty.
	“We’d better go,” she told us. “Tuesday will be here before you know it, kids.”
	I kissed Hue deeply right there in front of them. Maxxy let out a long, comical whistle. 
	“See you soon,” she whispered to me. And then we were out.
	It was a little better for me, after that. I was still losing her, it felt like, but I had already come to terms with that. Knowing she really did care, and that it had affected her too, took some of the edge off the whole thing. Not enough to mend my broken heart, but it was something, at least.
	That Monday, I spent the afternoon picking out a new outfit for the dinner. It was easily my freshest and most expensive set of clothes. I think in some silly way, I wanted Hue to think about what she was losing. Childish of me, but men are like that. Big children. 
	And Til was right, just like she always was. Tuesday night came up quick.
	After work, I rushed home and got ready. They wore their best Sunday clothes, too, and it was a good feeling to see that they (or Til, at least) understood how important this was for me. What I hadn’t expected, for whatever reason, was that the going away dinner was at Hue’s house.
	The driveway and lawn were packed with cars. Expensive ones, of course. I’m not sure why that surprised me. Not flashy ones, but the sort of ones that were high-end tasteful. There were so many that they were lined up on the side of the street outside of the gates, which is where we pulled in. You’ll forgive me for saying that I was glad about that. Their maid probably drove a nicer car than Til had.
	The gate was left open, though. When we went to get out, Maxxy gave me a bouquet of roses that she had hidden in the backseat with her. “Here, knucklehead,” she told me. It was so perfect. I could have kicked myself for not thinking of that.
	When we rang the doorbell, Hue opened the door herself. When she saw me, she rushed forward and squeezed me tight, and put little kisses all over my face.
	“Oh, are these for my mother? How sweet!” she said, taking the flowers. I laughed. She grabbed my hand and pulled me inside, and Aunt Til and Maxxy followed, more or less ignored. It wasn’t rude of her, really, she was just excited to see me, and I was flattered. 
	There were a few people in the living room, family, but not people I had ever met before. Heads turned. The conversations were all in Vietnamese, and I bet there was a lot of talk about my presence. Nothing too bad, I think, because I had learned Maxxy knew how to say ‘nigger’ in about twelve languages. Not to mention every other curse word you can think of.
	Hue ignored them all, and pulled me into the dining room, which was loaded with more family, all sipping drinks and talking to each other. 
	“Everyone, this is Clarence,” she said. Then she repeated it in Vietnamese. Heads nodded, and there were a few polite hellos. It was at that moment that I began to understand how much she really did care, because it was what you would call a cool reception. The fact of the matter was, Hue was going against everything her family believed in. Immigrant families like that, I think, expect their children to stick to people like themselves. And that’s reasonable, I would say. But the fact that I was black was probably far more than they were prepared for. 
	Still, it wasn’t ugly or anything. Just… reserved. I picked up on the slight tension, for sure. Maxxy broke the ice by saying something cute in Vietnamese, and people laughed out loud. Whatever it was must have been a real icebreaker, because the sort of stunned silence of moments earlier turned back into smiles and discussions, and I felt a lot more welcomed after that. 
	Then she yelled out, “Do-yun!” and ran over to someone sitting at the table. He stood up and she hugged him. It was the chef from the restaurant, and I thought it was pretty cool that he was invited. It said something to me that he wasn’t just an employee to them. 
	“You sit by me,” he told Maxxy, and pulled out her chair, and then took Til’s hand and seated her on the other side of him. Hue had never let go of mine, and she led us to the seats opposite of them. A few minutes later, the others came in and seated themselves.
	Man, when that food started coming out, I didn’t think it was ever going to end. Four people brought out dish after dish until the entire long table was covered. Despite the setting, it was all really informal, and people just started loading up their plates. It was entirely different food from the hibachi stuff I associated with Hue, but of course that was a Japanese restaurant. And the chef was Korean, which is funny, when you think about it. 
	Hue took the time to explain what everything was, and dishes were passed up and down the table in both directions. There were lots of sticky rice dishes, and I tried to at least get a spoonful of each one. She filled a bowl with soup for me, and explained it was called “fuh”, but was spelled “pho”. “Never call it pho,” she told me. “None of them will ever respect you if you do.”
	Maxxy, though, said, “This is pho-king great!” and Hue and I laughed so hard, along with Til and Maxxy’s apparent love interest.
	There was another soup I liked, it was served cold, but had braised ribs in it. Some fried dough balls that were really good. The best were these fried spring rolls. They didn’t look all picture perfect like the spring rolls I had ever ate before, but man. They tasted twice as good. About the only thing I didn’t try were the snails.
	When everyone was settled into eating and talking among themselves, Hue whispered in my ear. “Eat everything on your plate, but do it kinda fast,” she said.
	I don’t know. I thought it was some sort of etiquette, so of course I did. Before I had finished, though, her father gave a little speech about her going away to college, how proud they were of her, and what a great daughter she was. I thought that was real cool. 
	After an appropriate amount of time had passed, Hue said, “Come on,” while people started having after dinner drinks.
	“I’m going to show Clarence my room. Be right back…”
	She led me upstairs by the hand, and when we got in there, she didn’t even switch on the light. To this day, I can’t tell you what her room looked like.
	“We have ten minutes at most,” she said, and laid down on the floor. (Oh, my… - Janice.)
	I lasted about four, maybe. 
	I also did the dumbest thing I could have ever done in that situation. She was a smart girl, and nothing ever came of it, but wow. I had completely lost my mind. If she had gotten pregnant, though, I would have been the happiest man in the world. In my head, she would have been mine forever, and we’d probably have the cutest baby in the world. A whole bunch of them, eventually.
	“That was nice,” she whispered in my ear and held me close to her for a minute. “I love you, too…”
	When we finally got up (and I’m surprised she didn’t have her watch set), she smoothed out her dress, and we walked back downstairs again like nothing had ever happened. Her father paid no real attention when we got back, but I did see her mother eyeing me with suspicion. Not that I gave a damn about that, really.
	The end of the night came around eventually, though. People started leaving, and we all said our goodbyes to the family. Aunt Til and Maxxy walked out ahead of us, and left Hue and I outside the door.
	“I’m really going to miss you, Clarence. I’m sorry about all of this. I didn’t know things would be this way, you know? It was just fun, at first. I want you to understand, it’s hard for me, too. And who knows what the future might bring?”
	I liked the sound of that. I think she knew that it was exactly what I needed to hear. She squeezed me tight, pecked me on the cheek one last time, and that was it. She went back inside, and I caught up with Maxxy and Til. We drove home, and everyone was in good spirits for a change.
	When we got back to the house, Maxxy went into her room and stayed there.
	“Sit down, Clarence,” Aunt Til said. And it was a little ominous. Not her tone, which was warm, but the whole scenario. I thought maybe she had figured out what had gone on upstairs, and I was going to get a lecture on responsibility or something.
	I wish that was all it had been.
	“I wanted to wait until Hue was off to school to tell you. Your mother and her sister got a house together. They’re ready for you to come back…”
	All of a sudden, losing Hue was the most minor thing in the world.

Chapter 20 – Such Sweet Sorrow

	I didn’t really say shit to Aunt Til when she broke the news to me. But I didn’t cry or anything, either. I was tired of crying. The past year or so had contained enough of that. She let the moment pass without embellishing, and allowed me to grieve in silence.
	I was still working. But Friday was to be my last day. I spent an inordinate amount of time in my room that week, and that’s something else I regret in life. I was so torn up about leaving my adopted family, I squandered my final week there by being withdrawn and feeling sorry for myself.
	After the last day of work, I got paid, and a round of handshakes from the owners and Cooter. They all said they were sorry to see me go, and that I always had a job with them if I ever came back. In fact, if I didn’t have a mom to take care of, I don’t think Til could have made me leave. When I opened my pay envelope later, there was an extra two hundred dollars in it. Once again, work gave me a feeling of accomplishment that few things ever had.
	When I got back to the house, I was finally feeling a little bit better about everything. It had taken all week for me to come to terms with it, but I needed to grow up. It helped that I was going back to a real house and not some shitty apartment. I had a dream ride unlike any I had ever seen before. I had been in a shootout. Got my G.E.D. Romanced a beautiful Asian girl. Learned a new trade. What the fuck did I have to feel sorry about, really?
	A lot, honestly. Til and Maxxy were the most amazing people I had ever known. But like Maxine’s stuffies, they weren’t leaving the planet. I was just going back home. Things can always be worse, and that’s an important thing to remember. Things can always be better, but they can also always be worse. 
	When I walked in the door, Maxxy blew a paper horn in my face and threw confetti at me. She and Aunt Til were wearing party hats, and there was a big banner made out of colored letters that said, “We Love You, Clarence!”. 
“Surprise!” they both yelled out together.
	Shit. Damned if I wasn’t crying again anyway. But it was a different kind. The good kind. And it was just a few damn tears. If you can’t cry in front of the people you love sometimes, what’s the point of anything at all?
	Maxxy insisted I wear my own hat, a blue-sequined one like something the Monopoly man wore. It was ridiculous, and I loved it. She led me by the hand to the table, where Til had a big cake she made. It probably took all day. Not just some shit out of a box with a can of frosting on it. It had a map of the U.S. with a dotted line going from L.A. to Texas, complete with a little green Matchbox car that was pretty close to the one I owned.
	“Good luck on your next journey!” it said. “Love, Maxxy and Aunt Til.”
	Even that was special. It really underscored the fact that it wasn’t just a routine act of moving back home. It was me going on to new things. Maybe not better things, from my perspective. But it was far from the end of the world, just like losing Hue hadn’t been. Even though both of those things had felt like they were, at the time. And, again, it wasn’t like I was going back to Fifth Ward. I could have dealt with that, even, by then. I had a house to go to (Which they were actually buying, but I didn’t know that at the time.), I had two different sets of job opportunities, and I had been through it in Los Angeles. I wasn’t even seventeen yet. 
	My acceptance of things really made our little going-away party light and fun, as it should have been. We had chocolate-chip mint ice cream and cake, which was red velvet. Pretty much my least favorite cake, but I couldn’t complain. I made myself eat a big piece with a smile on my face. Maxxy spent the duration sitting a lot closer to me than she usually did, and I kept seeing Aunt Til smiling at me when my attention was drawn elsewhere.
	It was the perfect going away party. 
	“You know, Clarence, you can always come back in a few years if you want to. But your Aunt and mother need you, right now. Plus, me and Maxxy might be living in Alabama by then, which is a damn sight closer than L.A. is. We might even turn up in Texas at some point.”
	“Yee-haw!” Maxxy said. For once, she didn’t sound sarcastic.
	When we were done eating and talking, Til went and got presents for me out of her room. They were all wrapped up real nice, and I understood that they had spent all day working on this party for me. There were even packages for my mother and her sister. It was real cool stuff, too. A whole new outfit, new sneakers, which I knew for a fact were pretty expensive, and best of all, I think, a really fancy shaving kit. 
	It sort of signified my transition into adulthood. Instead of using some plastic disposable razor, I now had something I cherished. Because it was from Til and Maxxy. It also said that I should expect and strive for great things in life, instead of settling for the norm. I don’t know. Maybe I am ascribing too much significance to it. But it became symbolic of that whole part of my life, to me.
	Maxxy brought a present for me out of her room. She had wrapped it herself, and used the Sunday comics instead of Aunt Til’s wrapping paper. It was appropriately cute in that, although she had tried her best, it looked like a little kid had done it. That day just sticks out to me, still. I have seen and done a lot, and probably even had better days since then, but every detail remains sharply focused in my mind.
	It was a book. I knew that before I opened it, as soon as I saw it. I mean, it was Maxxy. When I unwrapped it, it had the unlikely, lengthy title “Blake; or The Huts of America: A Tale of the Mississippi Valley, the Southern United States, and Cuba”. Later, I learned that it was considered the first black science fiction novel. It was originally published as a serial, and fell into the alternate history category, which I hadn’t known was even a thing, at the time.
	I read it later when I was back in Texas, and man. It was written in part before the Civil War, and this cat was heavy. It was about a slave revolt, and the foundation of a black country. Not only that, but it used dialect in the same sort of way that Iceberg Slim did, even though it was entirely different language. It also dealt heavily with the south. It was thoroughly black, but also thoroughly Maxxy. Just the damn perfect gift, somehow. Some eye-opening shit, to me. Not just because of what it was, but when it was from. I still marvel to think that there was a dude way back then that was such a heavy writer. That he would write such a thing back then almost feels like an alternate history sci-fi book itself, if that makes any sense.
	After that, Aunt Til cleaned up everything. She wouldn’t even let me help. She was trying to teach me something else back then, I think. Looking back at life now, I think about how many women I knew who thought that something like that was beneath them. But with her, it wasn’t something she did because she had to or anything. She did it out of love, and I always felt that she honestly enjoyed doing things like that for me. It probably sounds a little weird that my aunt set the stage for the qualities I would later seek out in women, but to me, it all feels perfectly natural. That’s what her actions all said to me, taken as a whole. I don’t think it’s unrelated that she was one of the most content women I have ever known. Maxxy’s father, whoever he was, must have been one of the happiest men alive.
	It’s probably also seems strange that I came to feel love for a man I had never even known, and a white one, at that. But I don’t give a fuck what you think about all that. He loved Aunt Til, she loved him, and they made Maxxy. That makes him more important to me than motherfucking Jesus, Einstein, and Martin Luther King put together. That man means far more to me than my own father, if you can dig it.
	The whole weekend, we spent almost all of our time together. We went and played minigolf, finally. When Saturday Night Live rolled around, Aunt Til pulled me to go in the backyard and smoke our second-ever joint together. That show has never been funnier than that night. Garret Morris played a black spaceman, and ended up flying a dick-shaped spaceship through a circular one. Even Maxxy’s presence didn’t make it as awkward as it should have been. We all laughed together, and really hard, at that. (No pun intended. – Janice.)
	On Sunday, though, we didn’t go to church, and I was grateful for that. It wasn’t a terrible experience, but without Hue, I had no interest in sitting still and listening to what I considered bullshit platitudes. I would have sat there feeling bad about it the whole time, anyway. The fact that Maxxy let that pass without complaining about not seeing her friends really says a lot about her maturity.
	Instead of breakfast, we all slept in for a change, and had a late lunch, or brunch, I suppose. It was the most unlikely meal ever, to me. Fried chicken and waffles, which Til explained was sort of an L.A. thing. It was amazing. You would never in your life consider putting syrup on fried chicken, I bet. But damn if it doesn’t taste like they were made for each other.
	Since I seem to still have your attention, here’s an aside. I know you motherfuckers like to joke about fuckin’ fried chicken and watermelon. Hell, I’ve probably laughed about it at some point, myself. That is a southern thing, not a black thing, as such. It might be ascribed to us, but if you’ve never had the simple pleasures of chicken battered and fried by a sweet and beautiful southern woman, cold watermelon in the summer, and sat on your porch cracking pecans and eating them out of the shell, you ain’t from the south. I don’t care what color you are. 
	For that matter, things in the south are not what people say they are. I notice that people up north like to look down their noses at us. They claim some sort of superiority, and believe they are somehow more enlightened. Texas has it’s problems, for sure. When you get a little more into the deep south, east of that, people down there really are a lot more integrated and friendly than folks up north.
	What I have noticed up north is that white people there all have that one black friend who ‘talks good’, and works with them at the insurance agency or some shit. Then they go home to their suburban homes where black people aren’t a thing, and pat themselves on the back for how good they are by not being Klansman. Meanwhile, a lot of us live up there in project tenement buildings eight stories high with no fire escapes, and those places are to be avoided. Even when they’re slapped into the middle of the Coney Island side of Brooklyn. They’re little fuckin’ concentration camps, and a whole different world. You can claim Marcia that talks white as your friend, but you are blind to the plight of the rest of us.
One example I like to use is that if you’re actually racist, and not just prone to stereotypes and slight biases, you are not inviting black people into your home to raise your kids or cook your meals. I don’t know. I guess I am prejudiced myself, but southern people are probably the best people in the country, and that applies to most of the white folks down there, too.
	In other words, what you believe about us is bullshit. I am as much a southerner as I am black, if that makes any sense. That’s not to say there aren’t people in the south who I wouldn’t miss at all if something bad happened to them. But I have encountered that all over. Up north, it’s a lot more low-key and behind the scenes. If you hate black people, I’d prefer to just know that up front, and not have you smile in my face and then go home and talk shit about me. The real problem racists, to me, are in the bank boardrooms, zoning commissions, and executive offices. Inbred, sister-fucking Jethro isn’t really too much of a threat to me. 
	(Janice just said that I didn’t sound all that different from them, and she’s probably right. We all pick up some of these biases from experience. All she knows of black people, though, are high-society women much like herself. You can’t really claim to love and understand black people unless you’ve lived among the worst of them.)
	For that matter, having worked construction for years, when you’re around a white person who genuinely hates black people, you can feel it. They can fake it all they want, if they have to, but somehow, you know. I do, anyway. The rest of us all just cut up and say whatever we want, with no real offense taken. There’s a big difference, and I think that distinction is lost today. 
	Anyway, that might not be my final discourse on the subject. (It’s almost like a theme is developing… - Janice.)
	After lunch had settled a bit, though, Aunt Til brought me two pills and a glass of water. Sominex. Which is just some over-the-counter stuff, but she suggested I sleep during the day, and leave in the evening. I had never driven though the southwest, and that turned out to be some damn good advice. 
	When I woke up that afternoon, she had another set of maps and directions laid out on the table for me. She had put the presents by the door, along with some sandwiches she had made for me. I brought my stuff out to the living room with everything else, and it was a lot more than I had came there with. When we sat down to go over the route, she had some pretty specific instructions.
	“When you get to El Paso, you are to go to this auto shop. When you get there, call me so I know you’re okay. They’re friends of mine, and they’ll check your car out.”
	The way she explained it, it was harsh driving conditions, and more than half of my trip would be through Texas. Those western states are no joke when it comes to heat, and west Texas is just as bad. It all sounded pretty reasonable, and just underlined her concern for my well-being. That I would be driving at night was something that escaped me, at the time. I didn’t want anything to happen to my ride anyway, and the least I could do was accept her hospitality.
	We went over motel stops along the way. Speed limits. The importance of not pushing yourself when you get too tired. She knew when you should fill the gas tank all the way up for those long stretches of nothing that exist along the way. In short, she had thought of just about everything, instead of just letting me set out on my own, ignorant to what the trip would entail. Classic Aunt Til.
	Then we loaded all my stuff into the trunk. The two presents I was to take home, all my clothes, my own little library of a few books I had acquired. It ended up taking a lot of the space not occupied by the spare tire. The lunch went on the seat by me, along with a bunch of water. When we went back inside, she brought me a big box of cassettes. 
	“I like my vinyl more, for some reason,” she told me. But I think she was just being sweet and making excuses. “It’ll help you from getting bored. You’ll probably have time to listen to all of them, and I recommend you do so. We should always strive to expand our own horizons. No one else is going to do it for us, Clarence.”
	Aunt Til was always on some deep shit. 
	It was so fucking hard to say goodbye. Way harder than leaving my mother was, for sure. I loved that woman until the day she died, which thankfully wasn’t for many years. I got to see her on her farm in Alabama, and it was a beautiful thing, man. She never remarried or anything like that, but she was so at peace when she was there, even more so than when she was in L.A. It was like she was there with the man she had loved so dearly, even though his presence wasn’t physical. I got to see Maxxy go off to college. Don’t get all weepy like I did. This ain’t the end for her, although I probably don’t have a lot more to say about her. I do wish she was alive to read this, though.
	Imagine that. Me, with a book of my own. I can’t wait to tell Maxine when it’s published. All she’s written so far that I know of are research papers. Which is nothing to sneeze at. But I think we all expected that from her. Her mother was an astounding influence, and she raised an incredible daughter.
	Aunt Til made it easy on me. She hugged me tight for a long time, kissed me on the cheek, and said, “Get your ass on the road. You’ve got almost three days of driving ahead of you.”
	Maxxy, though… She jumped up on me and hugged me so tight. She kissed my face all over, which was something that she had never done before. “I love you, you big, stupid, scungelli-head,” she told me. This time, she cried a little bit, which was also a first. I couldn’t help but mingle a few tears of my own with hers.
	Instead of hanging outside and making things harder for all of us, they waved goodbye. Maxxy said, “Don’t forget to write! Use the cypher!”, and then they closed the door. I just sat there for a second and composed myself. When I finally put the car in gear, she ran back out and came to the window. “You forgot something, cousin…”
	She slid me the pistol, a souvenir of our little adventure so many months earlier. “If you want peace, you must prepare for war…”
	Goddamnit, Maxxy. She kissed me again, on the lips, this time. Since I don’t really care what you think, I’ll say it. It was a different sort of kiss. She was my cousin, she was a little girl, and it was brief. But it was a different sort of kiss.
	“Don’t shoot anyone I wouldn’t shoot,” she said, and ran back inside. I think she was probably crying again and didn’t want me to see it. I was, too. Thinking about it, that might have been the last time I ever cried, in fact.
	Now I was driving off with a gun in my lap. Which is really the most natural thing in the world, in L.A. But the first chance I got, I stopped and put that shit in the trunk, under the spare tire. When I did, I noticed there were four packages. One for my mother, one for my aunt, one that had both their names on it, and another for me. I couldn’t help but open it.
	It was ten one-hundred-dollar bills, and a Rolex. 
	“This was my husband’s,” the note from Til said. “Wear it with pride.”
	I still have that watch on me to this day.

Chapter 21 – Welcome Home

	It was still a few hours before dusk. I dealt with the usual L.A. traffic bullshit some more, which really helped me later in the Houston area. Houston might be a little more balls-out, because the traffic there actually moves, and Texas don’t give a fuck about speed limits. I hit the state line after dark. It got a little spooky. I was alone on my own for the first time, really. Me and the open road, facing the unknown. 
I had youthful confidence, but when you start seein’ signs sayin’ “Death Valley”, it’s hard not to think about the Manson family. I don’t think a lot of people today understand the significance all that carried with it at the time. Sort of like when King and JFK were shot for the generation before me.
Aunt Til really knew what she was doing with the cassettes. That’s what made everything cool. I started with Curtis Mayfield, and was able to enjoy Jimi Hendrix for the first time. That dude was a really inspiring character. I could probably write a book on that man. (You should. – Janice.) The tape after ‘Smash Hits’ was the Undisputed Truth’s ‘Cosmic Truth’. It’s some obscure Hendrix-inspired stuff that was the perfect album to hear next. The miles between me and L.A. were starting to add up. By the time I had put on Parliament-Funkadelic, I was making good time, and enjoying myself, besides.
Now most people don’t really believe in fate. Things that aren’t just coincidences but small miracles. Mystical shit. Most people are blind to the magic of life. Some claim to believe in it, but until you experience it first-hand, it’s not real to you. The next tape I pulled out of the case was Hotel California by The Eagles. Aunt Til had taped a thin joint to it. Holy shit. That’s not the magical part, though it was some of it. The people who got me out of prison and did so much for me had made it their theme album. In other words, it later became a huge part of my life. 
I pushed in the lighter, and when it popped out, I fired it up immediately. It only made sense to listen to the tape it was attached to. I can’t tell if it wasn’t an intentional pairing or not, and I didn’t know the bigger significance at the time. “On a dark desert highway / Cool wind in my hair “ made the hair on my arms stand up. I got chills. It’s hard to explain. The timing, the trip, the lyrics, the whole situation all added up to something much bigger. Better yet, later in life, I learned that the line “Warm smell of colitas / Rising up through the air” uses a made-up flower name, and it was actually intended to mean weed. So, there were even more parallels going on than I even knew about at the time. 
I was already familiar with Peter Tosh and Bob Marley from my time with Til and Maxxy,  and the reggae rhythm of the title song also resonated with me. I still don’t know what it’s about, but I love it. I have had discussions about it with various people, and their opinions have ranged from stardom and fame (Janique), The Church of Satan (Chris), to something called Simulation Theory (A dude I know named Leo). They all make good points. Except Leo. I have no idea what he’s talking about a lot of the time. He’s definitely brilliant though. 
I made good time that first night. ‘Life in the Fast Lane’ did make me drive too fast, so I had to slow down twice in only four minutes. The second time I did, I passed a cop hiding behind a billboard, and it’s a good thing I had. I wouldn’t have wanted to face an Arizona cop at night while stoned. Not at that age, anyway. The last tape of the night was a guy named Sun Ra and his Arkestra. I was never a fan of horns, big bands, stuff like that, but this guy was a major head. Probably right up there with Hendrix himself. He’s a little obscure, but check him out sometime. Maybe when you’re out driving beneath the stars of a clear night sky, and are a little high. I was not only in a bubble, but with my head in the clouds.
It was almost 5 a.m. I decided to stop, based on Aunt Til’s route. I got a motel room and slept my ass off until that afternoon. Then I found a little diner after I left and got a chicken fried steak with mashed potatoes for dinner, missing Til’s cooking already. After that, I fueled up, got snacks and water, and set out on the second night of my journey.
I only wished I had more weed. Though it was a good thing I didn’t. Somewhere in the middle of New Mexico I got pulled over. I did the things Aunt Til had taught me. Got all my paperwork together, then put my hands on the wheel, with the ignition off. The cop was relatively cool. I hadn’t been going very fast, so I wasn’t sure what the deal was. When he asked me if I was carrying any illegal drugs, I got a little shook. Then he told me to get out of the car while he searched it. I didn’t even have a roach but still, it was unnerving. My heart really started racing when he told me to open the trunk. My gun was in there. But when he looked and saw my clothes, shoes, books, and the presents, he let out a laugh and told me to have a safe trip. Cops can be a weird blend of decent and reprehensible, and it’s almost a coin-toss at times as to how they will treat you. 
He drove off ahead of me, and I had to collect my thoughts. I think it was the presents that made him decide I wasn’t a criminal. I made one last glance at them before I closed the trunk to note that he didn’t take anything. My mood was a little blown, just like the last time, and every time dealing with a cop. But some War and Sly and the Family Stone in the tape deck slowly took away the edge. I pushed on all the way to El Paso before I stopped. Aunt Til had made arrangements for a motel close to the shop where I was to get the car checked out and call from, so I checked in and got some rest. 
In the morning, though, well, evening. However that works. I went out to the trunk and got some clean clothes before I showered. I swear to god one of the presents was gone. I looked all over to be sure. It definitely wasn’t in there. It didn’t make any sense, but I couldn’t do shit about shit anyway, so I let it go. It was the big one, too. The one intended for my aunt and mother both. Between that and getting pulled over, my comfortable life was starting to show signs of its first visible cracks.
I got a late lunch, gas, and my usual array of road food and drinks. It was probably around seven o’clock before I drove the few blocks from the motel to the shop. Aunt Til assured me the time I got there didn’t matter. Sure enough, there were people waiting, although not any other customers. Here’s the funny thing, they were Vietnamese. I don’t know why, stereotypes or even my own limited personal experience, but my time at the other mechanic shop sort of had me expecting a black-owned business. It kind of felt the same as if I had walked into a Hibachi place and saw Black Belt Jones serving up fried rice. Just slightly surreal. That desert driving hypnosis makes everything weird.
They were decent people, of course. They greeted me politely, and brought me into their office to call Aunt Til while they took the car into the bay. We had a good talk, and I didn’t even hear my voice crack but once or twice. Just talking about Aunt TIl or thinking about her affects me on a deep emotional level. I assured her things were going well, thanked her for all the help, and promised to call again from the house.
The car was done only a few minutes later. They said they had checked the oil, topped off the radiator with coolant, and aired up the tires, but it was otherwise fine. I tried to pay them, tip them, anything, but they weren’t having it. “Your aunt’s good family friend,” the owner said. “Tell her Phuc say hello.” I about died laughing inside. I managed to hold it until I got up the street, and then just let loose. It was the funniest thing I had heard in days. Things were kind of grim inside the car most of the trip. Just serious. It’s like your body becomes a part of the vehicle, and it in turn becomes your shell.
My longest night of driving was in Texas. It’s hard to believe, but somehow, the majority of your trip is there. On the map, it looks like about half. But the way Aunt Til had planned it, I drove extra in the other states so I would only have one long leg of the trip left.
I got into deep driving mode once again and only had to stop twice to go to the bathroom, getting another tank of gas during one of them. I had finally reached the end of the case of cassettes, so I started playing the ones I had enjoyed the most. Everything felt a little more like home, the closer I got. San Antonio area was comforting. I had been there before. When I hit Katy, it was like reaching the suburbs on the far outskirts of home.
	My mom and aunt had gotten a house in Channelview, which is a nice, quiet place populated mostly by tradesmen. Deer Park was the nearby town where the industrial hiring offices were, all in one little strip mall. But the drive was going to take me right by fifth ward. That’s when I did a pretty stupid thing. Or things. I decided my tired, wigged-out from near sensory deprivation-ass, needed to drive my fly car down the strip in my old neighborhood. 
	Not only was that dumb for obvious reasons, but it also took me off script. Instead of Aunt Til’s carefully written instructions and annotated maps, I only had a gas station foldout of the area that I had stopped and gotten. I’m not sure if this part is smarter or dumber than the rest: I also got my gun out. I’m not entirely sure what I was thinking, at the time. I didn’t have enemies there. What I did have, though, was what had to be the freshest ride in the whole state. I wanted to show it off, but I damn sure intended on keeping it.
	I made my way sort of clumsily down to the ward. I felt like a fish out of water, somehow. The streets were strange after learning to drive out west. It was a foreign land to me, now. L.A. was gritty, but it was a bright, sun-washed, up-front sort of funk. Houston looked dark, and wet, and gloomy. You could feel your consciousness adjust to the new vibes. It was the sort of grim I had never experienced in California. 
I think all the tall buildings in the area had something to do with it all. Some places never really saw the light. So to speak. Out west, we got the full dawn and dusk, and the whole day’s sun. I felt I had been taken from my warm, sheltered life and plunged into shadow. Even when I finally got to the streets I grew up in, it was a struggle to navigate. I knew everything from being on foot, and had to adjust. That was when I learned the bigger truth about the boulevard people rolled up and down on through my neighborhood. The two ends where people turned around were both pick up/drop off spots for hoes. I saw two or three big asses covered with stretchy dresses, and my dick stirred a little. 
It’s funny how when one is out of your life, you already start looking ahead. I have learned at this stage, it’s imperative you stop looking at some point. When you find the right one, shut it down. Keep her by your side until death do you part. The right woman is going to give you everything you need, anyway. That’s what helps make her the right woman. Find you one that you just want to spend years with alone, twenty-four hours a day. Anything less, and it’s not going to work out forever. I mean, it’s just some advice. You do what you want. 
	Chances are, if you love one woman deeply enough, and let her know she is your sole focus in life, you will probably be overwhelmed by the closeness you get as a result. A comfortable, secure woman is capable of things no whore would comfortably, willingly do. Instead of feeling ashamed of herself, she’ll feel pride in her every act. This is a pretty heavy ideal to live up to. Your wife, your number one, your fiancée, whoever she is, you had better be the person she deserves, and has dreamed about. Don’t fuck with their feelings. I’m not sure younger people can pull it off as easily as people who have learned these things the hard way. 
	Now, the pimp side of this is to fake everything I just talked about. That is the difference between pimps and regular people. They know how to push exactly the same buttons. But they do it from behind a pane of glass. It’s all calculated and fake. The game he runs on you will be the exact same game he ran on the previous one, and the next one. Because it works. You would think women could see through the insincerity, feel it, somehow. 
	Being a daddy means several things, fatherhood aside. You can either be the caring, concerned male figure in her life, who keeps her on track, provides love and discipline in lavish proportions, and become the very ideal of fatherhood (with a bonus freaky component that just makes it all the more satisfying), or you can manipulate weak minds who are already on some sort of self-destructive path and put them to work for your own gain. One variety tends to favor a reward system more heavily. Pimping always ends up being a punishment-oriented approach.
	The things I had learned about pimping from my studies mostly all indicated that the first thing you should do with a new trick, after fucking the shit out of her, is to put her in the closet and beat her ass mercilessly with a coat hanger. It’s a life or death sort of game to be playing, and there’s no room for half-stepping. Obedience is rule one. Except for that cat David. I didn’t recall anything about him beating any of his bitches. They were free to come and go as they pleased. Once they quit working for him, new ones would step up to take their places. That approach seemed a lot more civilized, and better suited for me. I couldn’t fake my feelings if I tried. 
That’s not to say I won’t spank the shit out of a woman if she needs it. But that’s never the opening salvo like it is in pimping. There it’s used as a brainwashing technique. They’ll get severely punished just because, to keep them in line. Never knowing when you’ll be beaten, or why, can have a terrible effect on your psyche. I gravitated to those with stronger intellects. They were more intelligent, they had a capacity for love, and the maturity to rationalize things. Women like that are the sort who taught me how to discipline them properly, and not the other way around. The best ones acted out often to insure they were treated the way they needed to be, at times. It’s a whole different, beautiful thing.
A pimp with the soul of a poet. That makes sense, now. To see both the ugliness and beauty of pimpin’, and to set out of put your own stamp upon the art.
I rolled down the strip to the other end, where I saw the other dope spot. Because any hoe spot is also a dope spot, by default. I made the loop and headed back the other way again, ready to go home and go to bed. It wasn’t anywhere like triumphant as I had hoped it would be. But two blocks up the turnaround there were three tricks standing outside of a gold Coupe Deville. Ragtop down, this year’s model with the spare tire mounted on the back. He was leaning over on the passenger side, no doubt collecting money and making sure they were working hard enough.
The hottest of the three lost her mind when she saw me and that car. “Hey, Daddy” came out of her mouth spontaneously. That made me slow down so I could get a good eyeful. That motherfuckin’ pimp was out of that car so fast, I was awestruck. Didn’t just slap the bitch, neither. Cold punched girl in her jaw. She was laid out. When he drew his pistol on me next, I was caught off-guard just like she was.
The other two tricks screamed and ran off. You know this motherfucker shot at me? I think he hadn’t had a lot of experience with things, because he seemed as surprised as I was. Blew out both side windows with a single shot when I was right in front of him. The bullet probably passed no more than six inches from my nose. I slammed on the brakes. But then he froze for a few seconds after he pulled the trigger. And had done fucked up. I remembered my gun, where it was, and had it aimed before he could raise his arm back up. I extended it out the window and caught him in his shoulder. 
Son. That fucking thing put a hole in him something fierce. It jerked his body around in a semi-circle, and then he just kept going down. I don’t know if he even lived or not. I do know if I had aimed for his chest directly, there’s no way he would have survived. I should have maybe read the papers. By then, I was in south Houston, far from the ward, with no real desire to go back. At least until the incident was forgotten about. I even had to fake an alibi in my head. I was nowhere near Fifth Ward, and was still en route from El Paso. Where they could even corroborate my departure time if need be. Or give a more favorable timeline, even.
I already had a use for Maxxy’s cipher.
When I finally got home, my mother and aunt came out to greet me. After a big round of hugs, which felt a lot warmer and more sincere than I remembered them, mom said, “Did you have trouble, baby?” That was all she ever said about the car or windows both. Instead, they insisted I unload everything and go to sleep. My life had almost literally been shattered, but we could catch up in the morning. I grabbed a handful of clothes, and I shit you not, there were three presents in there. They grabbed them, my shoes, and my books, and I headed inside my new home. It had a lot of live up to, because it would forever be compared to Aunt Til’s.
 I didn’t even wait to see them open them, and just went straight to sleep. It was a new room with my old bed, and that suited me just fine. I had enough adventures for the time being.

Chapter 22 – Up for the Downstroke

	When I woke up, the first things I thought were that I hadn’t called Aunt Til, and that she was moving weight with the Vietnamese. That is a lot to open your eyes to. Now, I am no dry snitch. Rest assured, this document, rough appearance aside (Hey! – Janice.), has been gone over extensively by a team of very good lawyers. Names, nationalities, races, genders might all have been changed to protect the innocent, you don’t know. Plus, the statute of limitations is a thing. Many of the people involved aren’t even around anymore. For that matter, I might have copped to one murder in this book already. Let’s keep that to ourselves, shall we? (Large eyeroll. – Janice.)
	But it was the only thing that made sense. Her treatment at the restaurant, for starters. It had bugged me slightly ever since. The start of the mystery of Aunt Til. But it was the close association with the Nguyen family that took that tiny irritant and provided me with this now pearl of wisdom. I had thought maybe their close but standoffish behavior meant Aunt Til was sleeping with Hue’s father. In fact, I think it was both. His wife’s well-hidden dislike for us didn’t feel primarily racial. That was also part of it, though, and maybe difficult to separate entirely. I think she knew or suspected.
	But it was the presents and the auto shop that tied it all together. Relatives at the midway point between the two worlds. They had somehow been given a trunk key, took the package while I slept, and then replaced it. The only question that exists in my mind is as to what exactly got moved around. Most logically, it was two kilos of coke, swapped out in El Paso for cash. When you look at everything as a whole, it’s the only thing that makes sense.
	Now, part of me felt betrayed by Aunt Til, for sure. How close did I come to having my young life ruined, and never even known I was at risk? The bigger, deeper part of me, though, felt admiration and pride. I respected that she was doing what she had to do to make shit happen in this world. My understanding of Aunt Til was complete. She was a hustler supreme, and didn’t even get her hands dirty. That she trusted my abilities to that degree shows the utmost confidence. We were all risking a lot. Love and family are the only things that make such things worthwhile. Gambling your life away just to increase your stack means you can lose it all for nothing.
	It also explained more fully Maxxy and Aunt Til’s small arsenal, which was a pretty well-regulated militia, even by L.A. standards. The guns weren’t just in case of neighborhood issues. They were both drilled on worst case scenarios. All I know is that those guns looked very natural in their hands.
	My mother and aunt, though. As far as they knew, I had my windows shot out trying to deliver them a package, and they barely acknowledged it. I decided I would take the risk of the shooting on my own, armed with this new information. No need to concern Aunt Til unnecessarily. I also knew I would play entirely dumb with my mother and aunt, and see what came of it all. Because I wasn’t going to be used like that a second time.
	I dressed and shuffled out in my socks to check out the new place. It was honestly even nicer than Aunt Til’s house. It was much newer, for one thing. It didn’t have that lived-in quality that made hers so comfortable, but it was a huge improvement over the old apartment. But I walked out into an empty kitchen. I worked out the coffee pot situation and got that going. Then I drank some orange juice, which was nice, and unexpected.  
	I settled on buttered toast. When the coffee was ready, I just sat there at the little breakfast bar and thought about shit. I was in a little bit of a trance. A daze. I had been through a wildly life-changing few days. I didn’t even have anyone to share the experience with. Maxxy was great for that sort of thing. At that moment, I missed her intensely.
	That bullet passing through my windows completely summed up my present state. My life had been blown apart in an instant. The pieces that remained weren’t worth salvaging. Everything I knew had changed. It was already the second time in my early youth that I decided that beatdowns and shootouts weren’t for me.
	I needed to get my tools and get back to work. But before I could do that, I had to deal with my windows. This was a pain, because it involved a map, the yellow pages, and what felt like an hour on the phone. By then I was full of coffee, and set out to get to the shop, where they said they could have me out that day if I could wait. I had the sense to at least bring a book with me. 
	I didn’t have to wait but an hour before they started installing them. In the meantime, someone vacuumed all the damn glass out and got everything ready. They even took the panels off the inside and got all the glass out of the doors. Meanwhile, I’m reading Maxxy’s cipher book, the thick one, and I am falling asleep. I mean, it was interesting. But I was hot, and tired, and it was long-winded as hell. I eventually just closed it, and then my eyes.
	I fully dozed off, chin on my chest, for a long while. But when I opened my eyes, they were closing the doors. It was damn near two hundred dollars, but I felt a part of myself had been restored. Plus, I was a little more out of danger about the shooting. Heads turned when they saw me coming, and that was a good feeling. Flies in the face of my life philosophy, but that’s what being a kid is. Contradictions. 
	Mom and auntie were home when I got back. I peeked in the garage and saw an innocuous minivan. A prudent choice. Lowkey, housewife shit. All it needed was a “My Child Is An Honor Student At…” bumper sticker. 
	Inside, mom asked where I had been, and I told her. Confirming my suspicions, to me, she reached in her purse and handed me a hundred-dollar bill. It also said a lot that she didn’t ask how much it was, or offer more. But, cool. It felt like a partnership of a sort, already. I didn’t want any part of anything criminal, but I was now official security, regardless. I took that shit seriously.
	 I decided to take the day to get used to my new place. The next role I saw that I needed to take on was cook. There was a nice assortment of groceries, though, and that helped a lot. Just being around Til had prepared me for this day. I did everything the way she would have. I got all my ingredients out, then my pots and pans.
	I was chopping onions and garlic, slicing mushrooms. Pretty soon I had seasoned ground beef sizzling in a pan, and the smell was maddening. The water was boiling, and after I had drained some of the fat from the skillet, I added two jars of sauce. I even knew enough to add a little sugar to take some of the tang out of it.
	It wasn’t much longer than twenty minutes later when I sat down to eat, and had even cleaned everything up while I waited for the noodles to cook. The smell was enough to bring both of them out of their rooms.
	“Damn it, boy. Did you go to culinary school when you was out there?” my aunt asked.
	They served up their plates, and it did me good to see them eat something I had cooked. Mom poured us all milk, and the only thing we really lacked was garlic bread and sprinkle cheese. Next time, I swore. It was a pretty chill atmosphere, and not nearly so far removed from what I had grown accustomed to out west. There were no books or high-minded discussions, but it was calm and quiet. I guess they had also had enough of the wild life themselves for a while.
	They both still worked, which is also an important lesson. Always have a cover job, and a cover story. You might think you’re a champ for not needing any extra income, but it marks you as a potential target. No one pays attention to the working men and women, going about their boring routines. Mini vans and jobs are more baller than flashy rides and leisure, when you get down to it. It’s not just about making the money, but keeping it. 
	“What are your plans now?” Mom asked. “Til told me you got your G.E.D. That’s really good.”
	“Thank you. I think I’m ready to go back to construction.”
	“Go back? Til said you were learning to be a mechanic.”
	“I’m a welder, mom. And a pipefitter.”
	“Well, get your ass to welding, then. We’ve got a house to pay off.”
	The next day I went to see George, but he wasn’t in. I used the dolly and gathered all my tools. I needed a paying job. I was sure he would understand, and agree. The damn box didn’t let the trunk close. I ended up taking all the tools out and putting them in the car first, and just tied the trunk shut until I got home. I had intended on going see Yolanda, but having my tools up for grabs in the back of an open vehicle held no appeal for me. I already didn’t like the looks the car was drawing. 
	I resolved to come back the next day and deal with them both. Hopefully Yo-Yo was waiting patiently for me, and George had a good new story about his wife. I had to come to understand that he talked a lot of shit about her, but he loved her dearly. 
	At the house, I had to move the now empty box, put it in the garage, and then unload all the tools into it. Then I would have to repeat this process again if I got hired on somewhere. Already, my car was feeling a little impractical. If I got put on at good pay, I decided I would eventually need a truck.
	I caught up with George on the phone later that evening when I knew he would be home, extra beer on the way or not. He told me not to worry about coming see him, and to enjoy the tools. He was genuinely enthusiastic about my life, and supportive. It was nice having a male in that role for a change. He also gave me the number of a local contractor and said they would be expecting a call from me. I was set. Life, even pimping, is about networking. Failure to do so is failure to thrive.

	“They all say they’ll be back.”

“Try not to come more than once a week. Wear that, bring tools. After nine on a weekday, before two.”

	I’d like to say I never went back. But I’d be lying.

Chapter 23 – Working Man

	My mission had dovetailed once again. Get a job, and communicate with Aunt Til. Because the more I thought about it, the more incensed I got. Primarily because it seemed hypocritical to her character. Of the things I was faced with, getting hired on doing construction seemed the far easier of the two.
	As instructed by George, I showed up dressed for work, toolbox tied down in the trunk, and knocked on the door of the construction trailer.
	“Yeah.”
	“I’m, uh, Clarence Jackson. My friend George told me to come talk to you,” I said through the opened door.
	“Right, right. Come in.”
	The project superintendent was a big, brick-headed white guy with big rolls of fat down the back of his neck. Looking at him, you’d never suspect he held a wealth of construction knowledge inside. He kind of stood up to shake my hand, then I settled into the chair opposite him. 
	“So, I hear good things about you. Prompt, capable. Good work ethic.”
	“Thank you, sir.”
	“Don’t thank me. Thank old George and his ball-busting wife.”
	I laughed.
	“Well, he says you’re good at all-around construction work, plus you weld and do piping.”
	“Yes, sir.”
	“I can put you on full time at nine dollars an hour. If things work out, we can talk about making it ten.”
	“Yes, sir. Thank you.”
	“If you want, start today. I’ll pay you since the start of the interview. I could really use a right-hand man on this one. Just unload your box and come back and see me.”
	It was that easy. Which is one reason I am a strong proponent of networking. Construction work is also one of reputation, and I appreciate that immensely. It’s largely a meritocracy. As I went to my car, I spied a dolly off to the side of the trailer, and unloaded my box onto it, then took all my tools out of the trunk, arranging them carefully. I was getting better at dealing with it all, and finished it out with my stinger, gloves, and hood on top. I was even able to fully close it on the first try, and put my lock on it.

	“Ready,” I said.
	He handed me a hard hat and gloves.
	“Ward,” he told me. “Let’s take a walk.”
	We headed under the red steel structure that was part of the add-on the hospital was undergoing. 
	“We don’t have any welding happening at the moment. As the steel goes, up, there will be endless fixes and modifications you’ll be doing.”
	He walked me over to a small shed with a combination lock.
	“’7-5-3’ is the combination.”
	I unlocked it, and it was full of brooms and shovels.
	“Grab a digging shovel and a flat one and come see.”
	He headed off toward a series of two-by-four forms, and I dug through the equipment to get what I needed. I definitely hadn’t signed on for this. I hadn’t not signed on for it, of course. But it’s really not how I hoped my first day on the job would be.
	When I caught up with him, he pointed to a pink string that was stretched tight from one end of the forms to the other.
	“These forms all have two inches of clearance right now. What we need is three. Get your tape, and do your best to lower these beds down evenly.”
	“Yes, sir,” I said. I was disappointed, but I was also making good money. Plus, it was my first construction job, and I would be welding. I still wasn’t even eighteen yet. Now, in retrospect, I can tell you that being Ward’s right-hand man, such as it was, started out as a curse, and ended up being a huge blessing. I was taken through almost every stage of a commercial renovation job, and managed to learn multiple new things every day.
	But that first day was bullshit. Not because of the work involved, which was bad enough, but because it involved fixing someone else’s mistake. One thing I quickly picked up was that was largely what the general contractor did, made everything work with everything else. All we did was fix mistakes, it seemed like. I looked at my hood and gloves mournfully as I got my tape measure out of my toolbox.
	Leveling the forms was just a problem to be solved. But let me tell you, that shit is back-breaking by the end of the day. I started loading dirt into a wheelbarrow that had been chained to the toolshed, and by the time the lunch whistle blew, I had two entire forms re-leveled. I had even found a tamper that I used to give everything a solid footing.
	As I came back from my car with my lunchbox to find a place to sit down, Ward called me into his office.
	“Sit down and eat in here, kid. It’s much cooler.”
	“Thanks.” 
	I pulled up my chair, and we both ate sandwiches in silence. But after we were done, Ward showed me where they were in the project, what would be happening, and what our role would be. This went on for a good forty-five minutes, by the end of which, I felt like something of an expert on things. It was not only a good gesture on his part, but it really involved me in the job, and prepared me for what was coming. I felt like a junior partner.
	Then I went back to my shovel.
