More of that positive cultural mixing, I think, although I can see how it could upset some people. But, what, you’re mad at someone for adopting pimp style? You protecting the pimps’ feelings? Are all pimps black?
He was on his own at the ball, though. My goodwill can only extend so far. Plus, he didn’t get down and open the door.
So, we got in the back, and planned our entrance. The girls would sit on my right side and exit first, flanking me as we did our walk.
“Ay, man. You’re gonna have to open the door for us when we get there.”
“No problem, bro,” was all he said as he pulled off.
It was a great little ride, which lasted all of ten minutes. Hardly enough time to us to prepare mentally. I realize that sounds kinda, I don’t know, weak. I think the girls were fine. I was a mess. But my exterior couldn’t reflect that. I couldn’t relay my concerns, assess my fears verbally. I couldn’t be weak. I didn’t have Aunt Til to lean on. 
“Okay, one more,” I told the girls. I was too nervous, and I didn’t see any other options. I applied a little more chemical band-aid to the problem.
I lined us out another rip of what I had pre-chopped, me first. The girls were enthused, anyway. They weren’t worried about anything, at that point. Which is why my attitude mattered so much. Before I put the case away, though, I lined out one more and knocked on the window between us and the driver. 
He rolled it down and said, “Yes, bro?”
Morgan leaned forward and held the mirror, handing him a straw. He glanced back and sideways at her in skepticism, and then shrugged, polishing it off all in one go.
“Whoooooooo!”
My man had never did it before in his life.
“Bro. Fucking bro!”
And just like that, we were there. I could see the Imperial Palace up ahead and on the right, and even from a distance, you could see there was an event. It was a cusp. A turning point. I could have let my (entirely baseless) fears sweep me away, or I could man the fuck up. I set my jaw as if I was entering a war zone.
The whole silly front was demolished in the first minute.
The cars! Two ahead of us in line was a white stretch Caddy limo. But this thing had rims. It wasn’t a rental. Behind that was a goddamn Silver Shadow Rolls with Colorado plates. These cats were not fucking around. At all. I was already losing my self-consciousness (coke is also good about that), and just getting caught up in the scene.
There were throngs of people on either side of the entrance. The most colorful bunch in existence. It was hard to even focus on the beautiful women, each determined to flaunt their assets in the biggest way possible, because the dudes were dressed so flamboyantly. Every time someone got out of a vehicle, they all whooped and hollered and cheered. 
We were next.
They got even louder when my man got out from behind the wheel, and tipped his top hat to them before putting it on. Even our driver was a pimp! They were rolling, and I don’t think it had ever happened before. Plus, he was kinda geekin’ and feelin’ it hard. He couldn’t contain his elation, and it translated into some elaborate motions on his end, as he opened the door and extended his hand to help Morgan out, then bowing and gesturing with his top hat, welcoming her arrival.  The crowd ate that shit up, and got even louder when Angela followed.
I don’t know if this was something they planned, or it was spontaneous, but before they let me out, they turned to each other, embraced, and kissed deeply and slowly in front of everyone. This brought on a new round of shouts and even applause. It was somehow the perfect thing to do at the moment. Then they backed up on either side, and I emerged, cane first. 
What I got instead of cheers, for the most part, was a lower sort of rumble. It was kinda respectful, even. I got the feeling that they sensed I wasn’t someone to trifle with. I’m not sure what it was, really, or why. But it was cool. I wasn’t whistled and yelled at like some hot girl, although they did herald my entrance. A tiny, subtle shift, but it had a big impact on me. By the time I was standing upright, my chest was puffed out, my jaw was set into game mode, and every motion was measured and smooth. 
The girls both bowed to me as I emerged, and it was cool as fuck. While I had envisioned us all three walking in, arm in arm, Morgan and Angela apparently had different plans. 
“You go first, Daddy,” Morgan whispered.
A pimp’s life is a series of small crossroads, all the time. I guess you can say that about anyone, really. But most of y’alls crossroads are what car to buy, where to get a job. Shit like that. Mine was whether to slap the shit out of her right in front of God and country for having the audacity to literally TELL me to do something. But after that half-second where I almost reacted poorly, I knew she was merely offering sound advice, albeit phrased poorly.
I made an attempt at a strut down the carpet. Nothing all exaggerated. Just a tiny bit of swagger to my usual step. Morgan and Angela fanned out and flanked me, keeping in step a few measured feet behind me. It was a highly respectful move, and I had almost fucked the whole night up over it. As I walked, I could hear people talking to each other about me, trying to have discussions over the noise of the crowd. I couldn’t really make out even a full sentence from anyone, but I heard a lot of, “Yo, that’s…” and the like, as people tried to put a name to my face, or recognize what city I was from. 
I don’t think anyone there knew who I was, although some certainly seemed to think they did. “Freshest muhfukka alive…” was the fragment I heard that stuck with me the most. It had to be the shoes, money. But I was getting pumped up, and I was only halfway to the door. The roar doubled, and I had to turn around and see why. Behind me was an incredible Cadillac. No car in a movie could ever hope to eclipse it. And you know who the fuck stepped out from behind the wheel?
Isaac motherfucking Hayes.
Y’all already know, musicians mean far more to me than any actor. If anything could even come close to hanging with Mayfield (who *was* Superfly, in my mind), it was being in proximity to the man I considered Shaft. Talk about your mind being blown. At that point, I don’t think I would have been surprised to run into Bootsy and George Clinton, if not James Brown. 
Hayes threw up both arms in a V for victory, and everyone went nuts. He tossed his keys high in the air behind him like he could give a fuck, and the valets scrambled to catch them. This big, bald motherfucker drove his own car. He had a chain on that looked like it weighed a pound, the first dookie rope chain I had ever seen, years before they reached national consciousness with, I don’t know, Run-DMC or Salt-n-Pepa or whoever. He was wearing the white furry coat the girls had tried to convince me to wear, and rocking it like a champ. 
Meanwhile, I am standing there, half turned around, frozen like a deer on the highway. 
He didn’t walk the carpet. He went up one side of the crowd, slapping palms, nodding, smiling, and just having a great celebrity moment. A legend. I had to shake myself out of my stupor and play it off. Isaac Hayes. Cool. But it took everything I had to look away and walk inside.
There was a single door locked open, with velvet ropes extended out on either side diagonally, making a little funnel. Surprisingly, access was controlled by one tall, thin cat with a single gold loop earring in his left ear, and a kerchief on his head. He looked like a Barbary pirate. You just knew this dude had a big, ugly knife on him somewhere. The scars on his face suggested that not only would he use it, he would enjoy it.
And he was all smiles. 
“A bill each, brother,” was all he said. 
I peeled off five, and he gave me the nod, while slipping it into his interior suit pocket. Game recognizes game. Whoever he was, he was clearly a beast to be a one-man security team there. As soon as we were inside and past him, we could only stop and turn around and continue to soak in the spectacle. Hayes had completed his circuit up and down the sidelines. When he reached the door (and I noticed he didn’t have to pay shit to get in), he turned around, and pointedly and exaggeratedly pulled out a huge knot of twenties. They couldn’t have been hundreds. Couldn’t have been, I told myself.
He unfolded the stack flat, held it up at eye level, and with a rapid movement of his other hand, bills began to fly through the air and land on the ground. The first cat I ever knew to literally make it rain money.  The pimps kept their cool. It’s what we do. 
The hoes went wild.
They started running up on him, and in seconds, there were at least thirty women at his feet, crawling on the ground, snatching them from the air. All I could see were asses, titties, and money flying. And he kept that shit up until the last one dropped. Then he turned around and walked inside, smooth as shit.
I think my mouth was hanging open. 
	We were standing there dumbfounded when he walked straight up to me and said, “Excuse me, cousin,” and pushed on through between us sideways. It was both a friendly and aggressive gesture. If anyone else there had tried to pull that shit on anyone else there, it could have been a war. He made it clear where he was in the scheme of things, because he did it without a care in the world. The first five minutes of Player’s Ball was already bigger than anything the three of us had ever experienced. And we were barely even through the doors yet.

Chapter 32 – Best Worst Night of My Life

	I bet you were all prepared to read about the biggest night of Tokio’s life. If you don’t know already, life ain’t that simple. Nothing is, and if it seems like it is, it’s an illusion, and you should check yourself. Instead, this was a bittersweet turning point. A hollow, and Janice tells me, Pyrrhic victory. What I should do is tell you all what you want to hear. How I killed it at Player’s Ball, became the force to be reckoned with that I am today, the end.
	Life ain’t like that. And although life is, yes, primarily bullshit anyway, I would be doing you a grave disservice. If life is to have any meaning at all, there has to be some truth, somewhere. The more I learned about things I had never considered before from Brad and Leo, primarily, although Dana himself is a pretty amazing philosopher with more than a smattering of knowledge acquired from his brother’s proximity, the more I realize that all of life is an illusion. From the things we see, hear, touch, smell, and taste, to the chemical reactions in our brain which control our actions. It’s all kinda… fake.
	At the same time, this is as real it can get for us. At some point, we have to accept the base reality and proceed from there. So, while I have always been forthright and upfront to this point, I will let you know that here is where I faltered in my storytelling. Or almost did. Conscience won out. Stroking my own ego at the expense of reality is an insult to the universe itself.
	This was the night I lost my soul. What little I retain today is a fragment of who I once was. 
	So, there we were. Dressed to the nines, feelin’ fine. There was a bandstand at the back of the ballroom, and I heard that they were tuning up and getting ready to play. This time, I put out my elbows, and the three of walked in that direction, led only by my movements. As we got closer, Angela, “Ooh, Daddy, can we get a drink?”
	Fuck yes, we can get a drink. I needed several. Even my cocaine confidence was wearing a trifle thin. The girls got some fruity concoctions, and I had a Long Island Ice Tea. Take it from me, you don’t want to drink too many of those in a hurry. Especially in Vegas. But moderation, strangely enough, was the order of the day. I threw the cat a fifty, and we resumed our trek to the stage area.
	We got what I considered close enough and stopped, sipping our drinks and taking in the scene. I even dropped my babygirls a little bump each onto the side of my fist. It was an incredible night, by all accounts. When we started the evening, we may have never been happier.
	They occupied themselves by commenting to each other on every girl that walked past us, while I just studied the band setting up. I have a deep fascination with music, although the idea of me attempting to play anything myself always seemed silly and fantastical. It was somehow crucial to existence when anyone else played music, but the idea of me doing so myself seemed frivolous, somehow. 
	So, the cat who appeared to be the band leader didn’t look like much. He was kinda older, kinda fat. He was wearing just some normal ass clothes, as were the rest of his band. They seemed really out of place compared to anyone else there. And they didn’t seem to know or care. That’s real pimp shit. The rest of us were outlandish, strutting our stuff, proclaiming our self-perceived greatness to the world. Instead, I watched him tune a guitar, a beautiful big-body Gibson that looked more like it belonged in the hands of Roy Orbison, Roy Clark. Any of the Roys. It wasn’t chosen for looks, anymore than his nondescript blue jeans and baggy generic t-shirt.
	But the care he showed that guitar sound alone, began to affect me. He seemed to approach the world with a reverence one normally associates with a Buddhist monk. It was solemn. After a long while, he seemed satisfied, and then approached each of his bandmates one by one, and conferred with them individually. It was a few words, and some nodding, but it was obvious that he took his craft seriously, and respected the fuck out his compatriots. 
	Now, you gotta understand, this wasn’t no rock band. No bass/drums/guitar/vocals bullshit. Eventually, I counted twelve musicians on stage. Horns were still a thing, at least for certain genres, falling as we did at the end of the disco era. Black bands, anyway, were serious and large. I will again cite P-Funk as example, or James Brown. In fact, The Cosmic Truth was the only black band I knew about at the time that maxxed out at five members.
	One thing this dude didn’t do was acknowledge any of us in the crowd at all. He might as well have been in a rehearsal studio, practicing. We didn’t seem to exist. The soundchecks had begun, and that brought some of the more raucous and vocal elements of the crowd to the fore. People were starting to call out requests, bullshit like Chic, Diana Ross, and fuckin’ Lipps, Incorporated. Now, no one knew who the fuck Lipps, Incorporated were. But they were calling out for Funky Town. 
	Okay, I mean, that shit was cool. But it was sort of an obvious slap in the face to this man, all the same, if you were hip to any sort of bigger picture. He was undeterred, however. 
	He stood near the edge of the stage with his back to us, and pointed to each member in turn, left to right, as they gave a quick sputter of notes on their respective instruments. This cat was a true bandleader. Almost a conductor. His band might have been variously high as hell, on pills, drinking on stage, but they were as disciplined as any military marching band. Finally, he raised both hands, and everyone hit a sustained note at once while the drummer brought in two cymbals in a crescendo, raising everything to a fever pitch. 
	When he brought his hands back down, all sound ceased. The people now packed around the stage all fell silent as well. Then he turned around, and spoke into the mic as everyone jumped in on the one, perfect synchrony. The only other band I ever saw perform with that level of tightness was Frank Zappa.
	What, you didn’t think Tokio knew about Zappa? I may have gotten into him a bit late, and he struck me as a tad racist, if even unintentionally, for often adopting some horrific parody of black speech. White people just thought that shit was hilarious in the seventies, and I’m sad to say Zappa was the primary proponent of the whole thing. I realize that it wasn’t malicious, as such. A lot of white people literally want to be black on some level, for some reason, and it expresses itself in terrible ways. Zappa had the whole country (the male subset that listened to rock at the time, anyway) talking like that. So, you will have to understand that I feel he was a pretty impressive musician nevertheless, for me to overlook that shit.
	While we’re on the subject, Cheech and motherfucking Chong did some fairly foul shit back in the days, too. Maybe I’m a bit hypersensitive on the subject, as they skewered a lot of groups and people with their humor. But “Basketball Jones” is sort of hilarious and painful at the same time. I don’t believe a lot of us looked at it that way back then, but in retrospect, yeah. It’s pretty disrespectful. George Harrison played on that fucking track. The same guy who considered himself some sort of enlightened guru of a man.
	Any fucking way… 
	He looked right out at us as he began playing, and addressed the crowd directly. 
	“Evening, folks. Y’all look wonderfully ridiculous. I hope you enjoy the set. I might have helped set this all in motion, and I now regret it. But as long as the check clear on Friday, right, players?”
	Cheers erupted at the mention of money, and acknowledgement of the hustler lifestyle. They didn’t even seem to realize they were being subtly berated. My man had a honey voice. The first song was pretty complex, compared to shit on the radio nowadays. The song was “Tell Me Why Our Love’s Gone Cold”, and it was poignant. To even be singing about love in a place like this seemed absurd. 
	His emotion was palpable. The music seemed almost recognizable, but I couldn’t place it. Must just be some cool-ass local band was all I figured. Unknown, undiscovered cats playing for exposure. This went on for a few songs. A few girls danced, the players profiled, but it was almost a non-event. People drifted away over time, and the band was soon to be relegated to background ambience, if that kept up.
	Not that my man cared. I mean, he had a dude doing a minute-long flute solo. It was almost contemptuous of the people in attendance. I didn’t take it personally, for whatever reason. I was intrigued. Even more so when three immaculately-coifed black women walked on stage to their mics, wearing gold, sparkling dresses that hugged their forms and extended almost to their ankles. 
	He had been fucking with us. As soon as they hit the next track, with about a second separating the two songs, the crowd raised an ear-shattering cheer, almost like a single unit. It was the theme to ‘The Mack’.
	Holy shit. It was Willie Hutch himself. 
	This song, if y’all don’t know, laid down so much on us, all at once. Hutch and The Mack were the completion of the holy trinity of pimping and ‘blacksploitation’. Shaft, Superfly, and The Mack. You get one guess as to which song meant the most to pimps.
	It was so energetic, and there was such a surge of enthusiasm, all at once, even the pimps lost their cool, to a degree. They started doing that Soul Train shit where everyone formed a line and danced/walked away from the stage in pairs (or more), doing their damnedest to outshine the next group. Truth be told, it was what most pimps lived for. You don’t dress like a fuckin’ peacock if you don’t want to be noticed.
	To give you some idea of how monumental this was, not just to me, but to everyone in attendance, our Mr. Hayes himself made his way front and center, and actually bowed down slightly to Hutch. In return, Willie kissed his fingertips and pointed at Isaac in acknowledgement. This was some historical shit going down. Little did I know then, it signified the end of an era.
	It was going down like some choreographed video. When that track ended, along with the pimp struttery on display, he went into “Mack Man”, which is some gritty, slow, dirty shit. This was the true spirit of Player’s Ball, right here. 
	When he said, “You better get out there and get me my money”, cheers erupted once again. 
	Man. Fuck. I could almost see his eyes watering. I felt that shit so hard. Not what everyone else was feeling. But what he was feeling. It was deep regret. I got the sense that his biggest success in life was also his biggest failing. His cautionary tales instead ended up being the promotion of a culture he didn’t enjoy at all. And now, here he was, eight years or so later, and witnessing the culmination of something he was personally involved in creating.
	Not that pimping came in with that era of music and movies. Not by a long scratch. But it did explode, and it was harmful. At that time, any two-bit wannabe hustler out there was trying to make it happen. It definitely led to even more negative shit than was already associated with The Life. I was so deeply affected that I had to take a long rip, right out of my little amber bottle. I think half of that thing went up my nose at once.
	I didn’t even acknowledge the girls, who silently requested some with their eyes. I was in the middle of a life crisis, to a degree. I was of two minds, and they couldn’t be resolved into one mindset. A few songs later, “Slick” just bagged on each and every motherfucker in the room. He was calling us pimps shit, and our women stupid bitches. Right to our faces. 
	He was mostly correct. 
	I stood there and watched in a great cloud of melancholy, right until the last songs. He actually went back and played “The Mack” AGAIN. Giving the people what they wanted. It went over even bigger the second time.
	He ended, though, with “Now That It’s All Over”, which was slow and somber. It made me think my ass off, and I wonder how many other people there were feeling what I was. At the time, it seemed like only he and I knew the deeper meaning of everything he was saying. When it ended, before anyone even started clapping, he said, “Goodnight, y’all. Enjoy ‘The Life’, because it’s the only one you’ll ever get…”
	It took everyone by surprise, resulting in only a smattering of applause. He turned his back on us once again, and walked off. Literally, because as far as I know, he never did another show like that again. Not another Player’s Ball, and from what I have been able to piece together, he never performed any of that music again in his life. He was telling us something, but we were all too high on ourselves to listen. 
	The soundman recognized this as a pivotal moment, and put on the damn disco all the girls wanted to hear, not really giving anyone time to let anything sink in. None of it made a damn bit of difference to the pimps, of course. Which is what again reminded me that I wasn’t one of them, as such. I was kind of worse, because I had a foot in both worlds. I knew better. I knew who I was, and who I was turning into. I consciously chose the wrong path. There’s a guilt there that can’t be easily assuaged or rationalized.
	Anyway…
	We had been standing there for over an hour. The girls needed to use the bathroom. We all wanted another drink, some more coke. After all our needs were attended to, we started to just walk around the ballroom, and check out the little side rooms and what was going on in each of them.
	Now, two of those rooms are part of why I call this the end of an era. It wasn’t just the historic coda of Willie Hutch’s entire career. The first was a closed-door event, and this bouncer cat looked the part. I know I wouldn’t have fucked with him. 
	“V.I.P.,” he said. “Who’s you?”
	That stung, more than a little. Who was I, indeed? Still quick-witted, though, I said, “The Ben Franklin Five”, and slid him five bills. At this point, the lavish spending was just stupid. But it got us inside, and my curiosity was satisfied. Because we only stayed about five minutes.
	There was a small, round stage and a stripper pole. This was one of the new ones, the kind that silently glided on oiled ball bearings, and gave the girls a solemn, graceful appearance. I didn’t know that was even a thing, at the time, and I just thought they were somehow the most amazing athletes in the world. So that part was cool. Around that time, I think a lot of pimps moved their girls into clubs. Because strip clubs ain’t nothing but whorehouses, and any dancer or club owner who claims otherwise is lying.
	We ordered drinks from the little minibar set up in there, and walked around the room, looking for a spot to sit. But as we passed the back wall where the best seats were, round booths that could hold eight people, I noticed cats smoking out of these big glass contraptions. They were using tiny blowtorches. And this wasn’t no weed, son. It was ether-freebased cocaine. The same shit that I later learned George Clinton had been on for several days straight while recording ‘Atomic Dog’. The same shit that Richard Pryor had tried to kill himself over. 
	Actually, there are two versions of events, there. One is Pryor (who was in The Mack, by the way…) dousing himself with 151 rum and setting himself on fire, a suicidal move inspired by the tragic lows that can come after blasting your brain into the stratosphere for too long. The other was that his freebase rig exploded in his face.
	Either way, I already knew about this shit, if only hypothetically. But looking at the intensity on the faces of the people basing, and the anxious anticipation of the girls in attendance, I fully got the vibe, and it was evil. When two girls started getting down on the floor right in front of everyone as people cheered, and a different chick caught a backhand across the face for being impatient or disrespectful or something, I had already decided it was time to go.
	In a sense, it was the best five hundred dollars I had ever spent. I definitely managed to avoid that pitfall, unlike so many of my contemporaries. To say nothing about the hundreds of thousands of girls who were used up and thrown away, their youth drained by age twenty, many dying in one foul way or another shortly thereafter.
	“What was that, Daddy?” Angela asked. There was an enthusiastic quality to her questioning that I didn’t appreciate.
	“A busted lip if you ever bring it up again,” I said, ending the discussion once and for all. 
	One room was a sort of open chill-out room, and the vibe was more my speed. There were vents in the ceiling, air intakes, and they were sucking up mad pot smoke. I don’t know where it went, but I bet you could smell it up and down the strip. Everyone was on couches with coffee tables, like some surreal, giant living room. These were the sensualist pimps and hoes. Almost Parisian in their attitudes. But I was too coked up to appreciate weed at that moment, anyway, so our travels continued.
	Another, bigger room, separated off from the rest by a set of double doors, had a sizable crowd, many of them dancing, pimps included. Dancing… is not some pimp shit. It’s ultimately silly looking, and puts you in a vulnerable position. No pimp worth anything would ever be caught dancing in public. That’s one man’s opinion, anyway. 
	Now, at first, I thought it was more of the same. A DJ spinning records. 
	Then this cat put his hand on one of them, and another on the fader, and added a, well, scratching sound to the mix. People thew up their hands and cheered. I mean, loudly. This was the wildest shit I had ever heard. I later found out this was a clique of DJs out of New York, and that they were the people who had pioneered this new style of music.
	It was epic, and I could see the writing on the wall. No matter what your band did on an album, these cats could always top it, by using your own music, and reinventing it in new ways. I never thought it would lead to the death of musical instruments themselves. Yet here we are. Other than one last gasp at the end of the 80s, when there was one final black rock movement, bands were done.
	Like I said, this was the end of an era. Reagan had now been in office for a little while, and the whole country’s attitude changed dramatically. Even though we were just coming off of some pretty terrible economic times, and the perceived prosperity was beginning to spread, life was taking on an uglier tone. A win at all costs mentality was pervading American consciousness. Suddenly, we were a nation of pimps. Cold, calculating, money over all fucking pimps. Even the women.
	
	But damn if the music wasn’t fun. When this cat put “Rapper’s Delight” on both turntables, and cut them the fuck up, working the vocal tracks into stuttering starts and stops, people lost their minds. The dawn of rap. Hip-hop. Whatever you want to call it. I mean, I had even heard it on the radio before, it had been out a little over a year, and got a lot of radio play. But I thought it was some sort of one-off novelty record. I never imagined at the time that I was hearing a whole new genre of music being born. One more solidly black than R & B, or gospel, or anything you could name. For a while, it was ours.
	Truth be told, we got it from the Jamaicans. It was dancehall ‘chat’, Americanized and commercialized. Jamaicans moved into New York with their massive sound systems, and instead of just playing rub-a-dub records, they actively spoke over them, in their own unique, singsong fashion. The next generation picked up on this and ran with it.
	And to be fair, it wasn’t really *entirely* a black thing. I only learned much later that some of the important people in those scenes were Puerto Ricans and such. Even a few white dudes. So, it was sort of ghetto music. From the ghettos, for the ghettos. It was ‘ours’ collectively. 
	Or ‘theirs’, really. It was for the youngsters. Which is sort of a dumb attitude, considering I wasn’t but a few years older than that generation of people anyway. But we draw lines of demarcation within our respective timelines, and I was solidly on the other side of the fence. Little did I know at the time that the amazing music produced by bands, the very art that fed the turntablists, was on its way out forever. Even the horns were eventually phased out of hip-hop. 
	It’s a sad fucking thing, man. I guess everyone feels that the changing of the guard is always for the worse. I really feel it applies here, though.
	Shit.
	I didn’t feel any of that at the time, however. I let the girls dig on it for a good while, until they were sweaty and thirsty. The air was hot and oppressive, like any good concert, and it was time to move on. We got another round of drinks, throwing the bartender a mere twenty dollar bill this time, and headed back to the main stage. The curtain had been drawn, but the disco played on. 
	It was ‘Good Times’, at that particular moment, and it was the right song at the right time. It sort of lifted away some of the darkness I was feeling at the moment. The girls were definitely with it, though, and I gave them each a decent little blast. They started dancing away some of that energy, drinks in hand, much like most of the other women around the stage area were doing. In fact, there ended up being a circle formed around everyone who was dancing. Since there was no band, we watched them instead, and it was quite a show.
	Now, how do you feel about premonition? Foreshadowing?
	Whoever was controlling the music faded from the classic Chic track, the genesis point of all that hip-hop stuff, into Queen’s “Another One Bites The Dust”. It was sort of smart and mirthful. Both songs had a similar bassline, so it was a seamless transition. But that song… man. It’s pretty fucking dark for some disco shit. People were with it, though, and the dancing never stopped, ironic selection or not. My own enjoyment was shattered a few moments later. In fact, my entire life started to change, although, again, I didn’t know it at the time.
	A particularly beautiful girl, darker than the pearl in that Steinbeck novel, a little taller than myself, sort of speed walked past the crowd of non-dancers, and made a beeline for us. There was a calm urgency to her steps that was puzzling, although it wasn’t quite alarming. 
	When she approached me specifically, though, positioning herself between me and my girls, she said, “Ooh, I’m for you,” and then turned to face Morgan and Angela, greeting them as if they were old friends. Morgan shot me an inquisitive glance, and I responded with a shoulder shrug and nod that indicated she was to play along, wherever this was going. The three of them exchanged pleasantries as best they could over the music, until I saw the reason for her haste and unusual behavior.
	He was a tall, gaunt cat, about six inches taller than I was, and the motherfucker was pissed. At her, primarily, but now at me. This was the sort of pimp drama I had heretofore avoided entirely. Life comes at you fast. Like an angry pimp. Since I had turned to face him, and was between him and my girls, I straight-armed him in the chest, stopping him cold. It wasn’t the same thing as hitting him, but it was close enough.
	“MotherFUCKER!”, he said. And his hand moved to his inside suit pocket. This cat was already two strikes before he went for his shit… 
	When it reemerged, clutching a snub nosed .38 revolver, I acted on pure impulse alone. As he drew down on me, I twirled my cane up and caught him hard in the wrist. The gun clattered to the floor and slid away, and when he looked at it in disbelief, I swept his legs out from under him with one of my own. He fell flat on his ass. Before he could even get back up to his feet, I had unsheathed the sword, and pointed the tip at his throat.
	“Bad move,” I said. There was an uproar from the crowd that drowned out the music entirely.
	It all happened fast. What happened after that was equally quick. Someone materialized from the crowd and made the gun disappear. Two others ran up on the dude, and seemingly offering him assistance in getting up, bum rushed him all the way out the back door. I’m pretty sure he got a beatdown over that. We do police our own, and this sort of behavior was highly improper, to say the least. He was threatening the security of us all.
	Two much bigger dudes were at my side, moments later. They took the sword and sheath, not aggressively, but took them, all the same, and one disappeared with it.
	“Hey!” I said to the one who remained. He had me by a hundred pounds and a foot of height.
	“Cool out,” he said authoritatively. “You’ll get it back.”
	
	The reason everything went down the way it did was because there were definitely a few undercovers there. Some of them were obvious. In fact, some might have been obvious looking to cover for the real undercovers. Not to mention they likely had a few snitches around as well. So, some sort of weapon-related incident was to be avoided at all cost. Not even everyone around the dancefloor knew what had happened, at the time. It was handled, and correctly, I think, because no uniforms cops ever showed up.
	“Come on, playboy,” he told me, and walked off. I quickly realized he was offering me a lifeline here, and it was also a fairly direct order, politely phrased. Not a threat, but sage advice. He didn’t look back as I followed, and neither did I. It wasn’t until we had exited the ballrooms and stopped at an elevator that he turned slightly and acknowledged me again.
	“Let’s go upstairs and let things settle down a bit before we come back. Make sure shit’s cool. Can’t have any more incidents.”
	I nodded toward him as he pressed the elevator call button. Then I turned around to check on the girls, and there were three of them. Fuck my life. This chick was the cause of all this drama in the first place. Morgan and Angela fairly crushed me between themselves, squeezing me tight. This was both in response to the incident, and to the presence of this unknown female threat, I daresay.
	But she just stood back and smiled wanly.

Chapter 33 – Tube O’Caine

	Man. Writing is hard. 
Okay, I’m just dictating. If I said dictating is hard, no one would agree. But this is getting to be more draining than I ever anticipated. Not physically, but it’s mentally and emotionally exhausting. Janice and I basically had our first real argument. I hope it was also our last. She took issue with the last chapter, which I describe as the night I lost my soul. Because, she said, she didn’t see any soul-losing going on in that one. A little retrospection. Some doubt. Guilt, possibly. Naturally, I reacted defensively.
And she was right, which is the worst quality about Ms. Livingston. She’s generally right. But I can’t juggle editorial decisions like that. All I can do is try and lay it down as best I can. If I get some details wrong, stylistically, that’s for her to fix. Or not, it’s her book. Right now, she’s giving me the silent treatment, anyway. (“…“ – Janice.) This chapter length stuff is hard, too, because I don’t know how long a story is going to be, really, in terms of pages, when I title a chapter and start talking. But these are some first-world problems, am I right? 
	Needless to say, the vibe at the elevator was a strange one. Homeboy was all business, cool and calm. Another day at the office. I had just about gotten shot and/or decapitated a motherfucker. Plus, I had this strange chick who had somehow adopted me, and was the catalyst for the events in question. It was kind of feeling like some sort of set-up, and I was ill at ease. As were the girls, obviously. We all rode in an awkward silence, but the uneasy part didn’t apply to everyone. 
It stopped at some non-descript floor near the top, and we trudged down the length of the hallway behind dude, with no idea of where we were going, or why. Near the end, my man stopped abruptly, turned to us, and said, “This is one of Mr. Juan’s suites. I expect you all to be on your best behavior.”
Woah.
He opened the door with a keycard, which was a new thing, and first implemented in Vegas, and stepped inside, leaving us to follow. He seemed unconcerned with me and my entourage at that point. But I soon saw why. We rounded the corner into the room proper, and were in the midst of four cats who looked like feds. Behind them was a room clouded not with pot smoke, but cigars. Great stacks of bills were being run though counting machines and banded. By old white dudes.
They looked like Feds, but were Fruit of Islam. They patted me down before I really knew what was happening. The girls were ignored. 
“A formality, my brother,” one of them said, and smiled, stepping back. Another went and retrieved my cane and handed it to me. “The organization apologizes for the unpleasantness.”
I accepted it and nodded, not really sure what to say in this situation. 
“We hope,” he continued, “you accept this token gesture as an apology, and enjoy the rest of your evening without further incident.”
He handed me a folded piece of official-looking paper, slightly bigger than a check and metallic gold like a Willie Wonka ticket, which I accepted without opening. I nodded again, and turned and exited, girls in tow. Three girls, remember. One I didn’t know, ask for, or trust. We left the suite unaccompanied, and headed back toward the elevators. I had a headful of questions, but none I would have dared vocalized in that room.
Angela did, too. *** WHO?   FIX THE HOTEL SITUATION PLOT HOLE SERIOUS DISCONTINUITY
“Daddy, there wasn’t a pimp in that room,” she said.
“I know,” I told her. “Not in the traditional sense of the word.”
Which was true. Lots of y’all out there pimp on a daily basis. You pimp your company, a product, a partner. The system itself, if you can. Those cats were pimping the pimps. If that was indeed Don Juan’s organization, I was impressed. All of that money couldn’t have come strictly from door fees and the like. A riddle wrapped inside of an enigma.
It didn’t make me no nevermind, ultimately. I had my cane, was no worse for wear, and the evening was still young. The night seemed wide open to us, without obstruction. I pulled the voucher out of my suit pocket and glanced at it. It was for a suite, and was dated for an entire week, starting the next day. But it wasn’t even for the Imperial Palace. It was at the MGM Grand.
Damn. 
Caesars and the Grand were the two top spots in Vegas, and were places I didn’t even consider, budgetarily. The girls didn’t ask anything about it, and I didn’t offer any info. The whole confluence of events had caused me to mostly forget about who all I had in tow. That lasted about ten more seconds.
“Here, Daddy,” a voice dripping with sweetness said from behind me. She pushed her arm forward between me and Morgan, and put a very sizable knot of bills into my hand. It was an unbelievable power move. I considered the heft, and put it away. The night kept getting crazier, and I still didn’t know what to make of it all, but my suspicions were fading fast.
There were no questions I could ask her at the time that made sense to vocalize. Especially in front of the girls. She wasn’t vetted, no matter what had gone down, or she had handed me. In fact, accepting that money could have just as easily landed me deep in prison, if she wasn’t who she appeared to be.
That’s something I learned way, way back in the days, when I was still fucking hoes and not pimping them. When a girl on the corner looks way too good, acts way too obvious, stands out in unusual ways, it’s a cop. I didn’t get that vibe from her, exactly, but the idea stayed in the back of my mind. Plus, you didn’t have to be a cop directly to be involved with them. Not that they had any reason to target me, of all people.
A player plays on. In the elevator, I needed a blast. I dumped some into the cap of the bottle, hit it, and then gave the girls each a taste off of my hand. I did it for the inexplicable new girl in my life, and when she leaned in for it, out of sight of Angela and Morgan, I saw her wipe it away with her thumb before pretending to snort it. She knew I knew, however.
"Thank you, Daddy,” she said.
“What’s your name?” I finally said. It might have been the first words I actually spoke directly to her, I’m not sure. I think so. It was pointed, but not overly so. 
“You can call me Patra,” she said. 
Even her phrasing was suspect. It was the shape of things to come. I doubt the girls picked up on the vibe, but there was a dynamic at play that defied ordinary expectations. I remained in a fog of uncertainty. Loyalty is everything to a pimp, and until and unless that is established, there can be no relaxing and letting your guard down.
“This is Angela and Morgan,” I told her. She had no reason to know my name, and I didn’t really throw ‘Clarence’ around, as a rule. And that was that. She was more or less tentatively a part of the pack. Unofficially. More like part of the night’s clique. It definitely enhanced my credibility when we made it back to the ball. I felt a hundred eyes on me when we stepped into the room. I was now known as some sort of black bushido, and had one more girl with me than I came to Vegas with. I was also up a bit on my cash again. Overall, reservations aside, I was feeling like a champ.
“Drinks, girls. Daddy needs to relax.”
I reached into my pocket and drew two bills from the inside of my newly acquired bank, thinking they were twenties. They were hundreds. 
I looked Patra in the eyes, and she gave up nothing. Just smiles. She really did radiate a warmth, overall. It was disarming. I handed one of the bills to Angela, still conscious of the pecking order and the need to keep up appearances. The whole balance of power seemed to be shifting, though. And maybe it wasn’t all just between the three of them.
We had ended up back near the dance floor and stage area, as it was where most of the real action remained. I profiled and waited, keeping my head on a swivel like a nervous scuba diver, but hopefully without looking obviously nervous. The bigger the fish you are, the more likely to attract unwanted attention. People did keep their distance from me, and I appreciated that much.
When they came back, I was given the change, which Angie put into my lapel pocket. I’m pretty sure she didn’t tip shit, and her skimming even a penny was unthinkable. Looking back now, she really was a one of a kind. A real number one chick. She handed me my drink, and it had never tasted better. I was still coming off of the adrenaline, which was as strong as the coke. The girls had their little paper umbrella drinks, and Patra had what looked like a screwdriver, but I knew at once was only orange juice. 
The crowd at the stage area was getting thicker, and something was clearly afoot. Since it was my first time at Player’s Ball, I didn’t know what to expect. Everyone else seemed to, though. We stood in a loose huddle and faced the curtains that hid the stage, and the girls actually engaged Patra in small talk. Such was the power of this woman. Already, she was winning them over. I didn’t think such a thing was possible. Winning me over was something else entirely, and it would take more than a suspiciously large amount of money to do that. It did help, though, not gonna lie.
My guard was dropping, however. Not just toward her. Because about twenty minutes later, I felt a light hand on my shoulder. I turned at the waist, startled, to see that it belonged to the man himself. Don “Magic” Juan. He was wearing a green fringed suit, with a matching top hat, those Bootsy-style star-shaped sunglasses he favored, and he also had a cane. A cane that appeared to be plated in gold.
“Son,” a voice said behind me, and I turned around fully at that point, “Good to see you here.”
It was Mayfield.  The hand had left my shoulder as stealthily as it had landed there. 
“Yes, sir,” I said, unable to think of anything else. “Good to see you again, too.”
I nodded to Magic, and got the blessed nod in return. 
“Don’t forget about Sunday,” he reminded me.
“That would be impossible,” I said. It was the raw truth.
“Play on,” he told me, and the two walked away, headed toward the backstage area.
	I was reeling in stunned silence. 
	“Daddy…” Angela said in a hushed tone. She clearly understood the general significance of the event. 
I thought I did, too.

Chapter 34 – Pimp or Be Pimped
	Now, look. Things get a bit fantastical, at this point. It is understandable that you may not fully believe me. The temptation for anyone writing an autobiography to improve upon real-world events for the sake of a story is probably irresistible, to a degree, for anyone. However, this is documented history. And I was there when it all went down. I can hardly believe it myself, and that is only taking the night up to this point into account.
	The lights went down as the sound system seamlessly changed from a record to a live band again, and the crowd made some noise, although I don’t think any of us knew what was coming, really. But we knew it was something big. Because a series of chords began that was instantly recognizable to everyone in attendance. And it sounded better than the record ever did, which was really sayin’ somethin’.
	The curtain didn’t come up, though. There was about five or six feet between the footlights and audio monitors and the red velvet drapes that hid the band from view. The guitar told us instantly that it was “Sittin’ on the Dock of the Bay”. And not just any version. The guitar kicked the whole thing off, but then the horn section added to it, and it sounded majestic.
	So much so that the crowd began clapping spontaneously in time with the laid-back tempo the drums provided. When the lead guitar came in after that, I got chills. It was a Hendrix Fuzzface guitar tone. Goddamn, this shit sounded good. That guitarist laid down a melody lead, and then a second tone layered over that as the distorted lead faded back a bit and joined the backing music.
	This time, it was unmistakably a Shaft-styled tone. Now my interest was really piqued, because I knew my man was on site. Ain’t this some shit, I thought. The same sort of sequence repeated, and the Hayes-sounding guitar (I refused to fully accept that it was really him) followed the Hendrix tone, replaced by another funky sounding lead.
	Finally, the guitarists fell into what you would call a harmony lead. A three-part harmony lead. It was some of the most epic, glorious music I had ever heard in my life. *This* felt like the culmination of a life’s dream. Not the money, the girls, the cocaine. None of that mattered one bit at that point. It was like a grand musical orgasm, and I didn’t really care who was playing it. I just existed in that moment, and wished it could stretch on to eternity. If I controlled the universe, I would still be standing there, swaying along with my two top chicks cuddling up on either side of me as we listened in blissful, rapt attention.
	Now, I knew it wasn’t motherfuckin’ Otis Redding, just like I knew it wasn’t Hendrix. They had both died about a decade earlier. Intellectually, I knew that, I should say. But it was like I could feel their presence, nevertheless. Their essence came through in the music, just as real as if they were behind that curtain. It was an almost religious awe I felt.
	That shit lasted about ten more seconds. 
	A dude with a bit of a fro, wearing a sparkling black cape and top hat kinda started sliding in from stage left sideways, with his back to the crowd. More cheers, whistles, and cries went up, and the clapping in time with the music got louder. As he got to centerstage, still facing the curtain, his hands went up, one holding a mic, and he wiggled his fingers indicating that he needed some heavy applause. It was a brilliant bit of showmanship. He was already commanding the crowd, and we didn’t even know who he was.
	He turned around as he started singing, looking not entirely unlike Richard Pryor, and for a moment, I was unsure.
	Then he began.
	“Shittin’ in the mornin’ sun…”
	The crowd went fucking nuts. Me and my girls seemed to be the only people there who had no idea of what was going on. And the dude had a silky smooth voice, too. He did a very passable Redding impersonation. It was only spoiled, if you can say that, by the bizarre lyrics. 
I think it must have shown on my face how upset I was initially, and damned if I didn’t get the sense that he saw my expression as he scanned the crowd. The clapping in time to the music continued, and by the time he had started the second verse, half of the crowd was probably singing along. 
	“Shittin’ on the dock of the bay…”
	By now, I was kind of in on the joke myself. This dude was funny as hell. He was a consummate performer, but he used his considerable talent to parody what was pretty much one of those holy grail, untouchable artists of old. All I could do was smile and fall into the groove along with everyone else.
	Naturally, it wasn’t too long after I was lulled into a trance, listening to this man desecrate one of my mother’s favorite songs (which sort of made it one of mine, as well), that he flipped the script on me once again.
	“Aye,” he said, and the music stopped in an instant. It was clearly a scripted part of the act, the way they reacted that quickly. “Fuck that fucking old coon bullshit. Play *my* motherfuckin’ track…”
	Woah. 
	It wasn’t enough to defile one of the most good-natured, beautiful songs ever written, he had to stomp it to death, right there on stage. It was outrageous. Now, I didn’t know what the fuck punk rock was back then, in any way, shape or form. Matter of fact, if you tell a black person you’re a punk, they will most likely take a step back away from you. It has an entirely different connotation in our world. But this was the most punk rock thing I could imagine happening, for that time and place. Some highly rebellious shit was going down. Revolutionary, in a sense. (Editor’s note: This seems the only likely time to mention that the most prominent punk rock album of that time period was Black Flag’s “Everything Went Black”…)
	But not necessarily revolutionary in a way I agreed with personally. Part of me wanted to slap the shit out of this cat. But the more sensible part of me, the one that appreciates art in all it’s many forms, laid back and watched the show. I mean, it was funny, once I got over the shock of what he was doing. Another factor that chilled me out was the fast little bassline that came out of the speakers next, the intro to the next song. It reminded me that, whatever was going on, it was met with the approval of the band behind the curtain. So, if they were good with it, I had to be, too, and I relaxed a bit.
	This Richard Pryor-looking motherfucker let out a crazy laugh, and immediately broke into a rap of his own. And with a live band, which is what I think could have changed the face of music entirely, had that trend continued. But it wasn’t just a rap, which was kind of new and fresh to us, at the time. I say “at the time”, because everyone should have known about Pigmeat Markham back then.
	Now, I daresay almost any of us from that era was familiar with his work, albeit unintentionally, without context. “Here comes the judge” was a phrase almost all of us had said or at least heard in our lives. Not just once or twice, but many times. I didn’t know why adults said it, or why it was funny, but it was.
	It was only later with Aunt Til that I got the scoop on all this stuff. Til didn’t feel education started with reading, and ended with math. Her approach was what you would call holistic. There were things to learn about everything, and she viewed them all as almost equally important. She said that we had an infinite capacity for learning, and that it was a sin, for lack of a better word, to ever stop that pursuit of knowledge. That the search for truth is a never-ending process, a sifting of facts into more fully realized and understood facts. There was no final truth, in other words, but just an improved understanding of what truth might be.
	Like I said, Til was one of a kind. 
	So, she had introduced me to Markham’s 1961 “The Trial” comedy album first. It was some pretty revolutionary stuff in its own right. Funny as hell, it begins on the note of saying “The judge is high as hell, and everybody’s goin’ to jail”, and just continues on with all sorts of deep humor for an hour. On the surface, it sort of seems like that Amos and Andy shit that Maxxy detested. But it wasn’t. You might even say it was some trojan horse stuff. Biting political and social satire going on about its business, while appearing as more minstrel show nonsense on the surface.
	My point was, initially, that this album starts off with a little ‘rap’ of its own, from the second the needle drops. In fact, rap of a sort was already kind of a thing with The Last Poets, for example. It’s not like these hip-hop kids invented rhyming poetry. Certainly, they have never reached the level of Shakespeare, or the Iliad or Odyssey, or Ginsberg, or anything like that. At least in my old-ass opinion.
	Predictably, Janice had to chime in here and point out that most of the stuff listed above doesn’t actually rhyme, including Howl, which I am well familiar with. The Greek poems were written in ‘heroic verse’, or as she likes to call it, dactylic hexameter. I think that sounds like some chemical dinosaurs must have used. But she knows what the fuck I mean, and so do you. Rhyming words, even set to music, isn’t a particularly amazing feat. It’s the basis of all lyrical music. Rap is sort of just singing converted to aggressive talking.
	Anyway, “The Trial” just set the stage for 1968’s “Here Comes the Judge”, where he had moved from comedy to James Brown and soul-inspired funk. So many things were derived from this song, it’s unbelievable. You can follow the lineage straight up to ‘Rubber Biscuit’ by the Blues Brothers. Markham set the tone for the next 50 years, unbeknownst to him. That beat alone is priceless. And, funnily enough, “He is the judge” is what the song primarily says, despite the title. At least from what I can remember. It was a long time ago for me, now. He did it again with “Who Got the Number” in 1969, lest you think it was a fluke. It’s basically a reworded “Judge” with a great keyboard sound, this time, to be honest. Still classic. 
	I’d have to say, having learned a great deal more about things since I was a youth, the Jubalaires were probably some of the earliest progenitors of the style. And that was in the damn 40s. You can actually hear the evolution in their work, as they progressively deemphasize the harmony aspect of what they were doing, until it was almost just a straight lyrical flow. It wouldn’t surprise me if some historian pulls out a rap from the 20s or 30s next. Not at all.
	So where was I?
	Oh, yeah. Janice rolled her eyes at my recent sidebar, and I walked the fuck out.

Chapter 35 – Fuck Yo’ Chapter Titles

	Okay, rough day yesterday. We actually had it out today, already. 
Janice, in her apparently fallible wisdom, told me that this was an autobiography, and not a half-ass ghetto musicology course. Now, ain’t that some privileged white bitch way of looking at things? I mean, I thought the point of this story, in part, was that I was more than a mere pimp. Music, and music history is as much a part of me as anything else. In turn, I told her that she should write her own fucking book, then. She said she is, and that it will sell more copies.
	I said, yeah, but it won’t be better than mine.
	After a long, tense moment, we both laughed. Janice doesn’t like a lot of construction work talk, either. But any real writer knows that the story goes where the story goes. Maybe these content decisions can be made by an editor. What she and I are doing, working out the content in real-time, essentially, is probably a terrible way of doing things.
	Fuck it. If ya’ll don’t want to hear about an essential part of my background, skip ahead. Or don’t read this at all. I honestly don’t give a fuck. Anyway, I’m trying to see where I was before Janice dropped her dusty fucking bloomers and laid a big pile of shit in my Cheerios.
	So, yeah. Blah blah, the history of rap. I had a fucking point, some time ago. It’s mostly lost now. But what I was getting at in trying to frame the context of this performance I was witnessing, which kind of blindsided me, was this. Less than a year after Sugarhill Gang dropped “Rapper’s Delight” (Is this sentence okay with you, Janice? Too much boring black people history?) (“Tokio, please get on with the book and stop being such a butthurt little bitch.” – Janice.), this motherfucker spit this on the mic, right from the jump:
“This is Blowfly, the master of class
And I’m here to sock some soul to your ass
Guys and girls, bulldaggers and faggots
Gonna rap in your ear ‘til your ass gets ragged”
	Bruh. I was a little shocked myself. But it wasn’t what he was saying. That was how everyone in the room talked. How everyone I *knew* talked. But that sort of stuff didn’t happen in music. It had never been part of a song like this. He had taken the, sorry, minstrel show of Sugarhill, who rapped about corny kid stuff, eatin’ mashed potatoes and shit, and proceeded to fill our ears with the most foul shit you can imagine. He crossed the line in a way no one ever had before, or since. 
	And it was funny as fucking hell. Not only did he just go off for what seemed like forever, he told a series of humorous stories. Hell, he referenced the whole big rig trucking fad of the past few years. Talked mad shit about police and crackers. The man said exactly what was on his mind, made us all laugh hard as hell. It was straight pimp shit, son. The not so subtle art of not giving a fuck.
	Because, being forced to think about it, and a lot of things, (Thank you for that, Janice.) I will say that is what being a pimp can be distilled down to. It’s not about manipulating women to your advantage. It’s not about making money. It’s about knowing what other people consider right and wrong, and still doing what you want anyway. It’s a conscious decision to live outside the bounds of even what other criminals consider kosher. 
	That’s the admirable side of pimping, even if every other aspect can be considered abhorrent. Which it can’t, because until you have lived The Life, and are intimately familiar with the players involved in the game, you’re just passing judgment on shit you don’t understand. Hoes are gonna fucking hoe, man. Pimps are gonna pimp. Crackers are gonna cracker. 
Old, rich white bitches are gonna old rich white bitch. (“That’s quite enough Tokio, thank you.” – Janice.)
What I’m getting at, though, is that this cat, who I learned was called Blowfly (allegedly, his own grandmother said to him when he was still young, “Boy, you nasty as a blowfly”…), was an incredible ground-breaking performer. He had already taken this rap thing to its logical extreme nearly a decade before anyone else did. *
So, as I’m absorbing all of this, and being captivated by the enormity of it all, another voice cut in over the P.A. A familiar voice, at that.
“Get this little nuttin’-ass boy off my motherfucking stage….”
The music all stopped at once. Dude turned around to look at the band he couldn’t see anyway, and two of those big gorilla ass bouncer cats bum-rushed him out of there, stage left. He was shaking his head, protesting, and looking upset. It was staged, but it was extremely well done. 
“Because it’s time for the pimp awards…”
You know it was Rudy Ray Moore. But not even Moore. It was fucking Dolomite.
People didn’t even clap or yell or nothin’. I think a lot of them were taking the Blowfly bit seriously, and were a little thrown off by that. The shock of this man appearing from nowhere, following that little scene, had stunned us all into relative silence. And now here he came, strolling out all slow, *three* of the hottest bitches there on each arm, wearing the most garish possible parody of a pimp suit imaginable.
I mean, how can you even parody a pimp suit? 
He did it, though. Wider lapels, bigger bellbottoms, puffier shirt. But it was probably the fact that it was made out of actual American flags that did it. Which was kind of a bold move in and of itself, at the tail end of an era that saw a lot of our people getting their asses stomped by the police for disrespecting their piece of shit rag. It was the big-ass Uncle Sam hat that really put it over the top, though. 
	He made an unhurried walk to centerstage, and by the time he got there, some of those Fruit of the Loom Islam guys, more nimble than the big bouncer types, had brought in a .* The front read, “Dolomite for President”. He tapped the podium mic once with his index finger to make sure it was on, and dropped the handheld one to the floor, where it gave a thump and was turned off at the soundboard.

I didn’t bother with our cabbie for such a short hop, but resolved to call him later for the limo. There was something about her presence that made things feel festive. Upper-class Black Vegas. I liked the sound of that. I rolled the cart right up to a waiting cab, and for once I didn’t feel compelled to tip beyond the norm.  
The MGM was a whole other world. It really had that old timey feel, back then. When luxury really meant luxury, not the gleaming, modern sense of the world. It was in the feel of the aged wood and the brass doorknobs. The lobby alone was so stately as to make you feel like you were in a movie. 
	It was a breezy check-in, thanks to Patra, who had indicated I just need present my voucher and driver’s license. Less than a minute later, a bellhop had taken our cart on ahead of us to our room, leaving it inside for us, and not even hanging around for a gratuity. On the elevator, I started to ask what floor we were on, and she hit twenty-six ahead of me. It was the top button.
	Even the hallway ceiling was vaulted. There were alcoves with comfortable couches at either end of the hall. It truly was grand, and I’m glad I got to see it. Little did I know it would burn down that very same year.
	The room, if you can imagine, looked like a tasteful Liberace set. It was all oak paneling, old world-looking stuff, even on the ceiling. Hanging from sixteen feet up was not one, but two chandeliers. There were sconces on the walls that were electric, but closely resembled candles. It had none of the cheesiness and whimsy of the last ‘honeymoon’ suite. The furniture all looked and felt expensive. It’s part of what I mean about old school luxury. It was very sensual. The wood was tempered by the windows, which spanned the length of the room, and continued around the corner.
	When I say ‘Liberace’, I’m not just making a joke. There was a full grand piano in the room. On top was a huge vase of red roses, and a chilled bottle of Dom. Next to that was, literally, a lit candelabra.

She ran over to the window and practically pressed her face against it. It was indeed a stunning sight. Morgan joined her only moments later, and they were lost for a moment in the dazzling lights. I can’t imagine a better view of the city.

Then we both stood there for a moment and took in the sight of the girls taking in the sights. Finally, she let go of my hand, and instead of just dutifully grabbing the bags herself, she walked over to the girls and put her hands lightly on the small of their backs. Without saying a word, she led them back to the entry where we all grabbed something and our luggage was stowed in closets at once. 
	Angela opened a door on the wall to our right past where we came in, and it opened onto a bathroom that was nearly the size of a normal hotel room. Someone was thinking ahead when they included three vanities. 
	“Finally, a bidet,” Patra said.
	The shower would easily accommodate four.
	“There’s no bed, though,” Morgan said. Then she walked around the corner.
	“You guys!”
	What had appeared to be some sort of partition wall by the windows to our right was really the beginning of a hallway. The windows ran the length of it, extending that same incredible view all the way around the far corner. To the left was a balcony. To the right, the hallway opened up into a dining and entertainment area, complete with a bar, a full kitchen tucked off behind the lounge.
	The girls were torn, but ended up speed walking down the hallway with glee. I followed behind, not keeping up, and Patra was behind me. 
	“Oh my god!” Morgan was saying ahead of us.
	“I know!” Angela added.
	The bedroom was the size of the previous room that housed the piano, with the same high-arched ceilings and paneling, but this time with a skylight. As luck would have it, a three-quarter moon hung over us in the sky.
	There was both a loveseat and a couch facing the view, and the bed itself sat on a platform a step above the floor. It was a hand-carved four-poster bed with a canopy, and it looked like it could sleep six people comfortably. Very comfortably. Like a museum exhibit, but one that begged for you to climb onto it, even in the middle of the day. Beyond that, there was another full bathroom, and a door we assumed was a closet.
	Then I noticed Patra wasn’t there. Felt it. It was odd, to miss her presence already. I left the girls, and found her out on the balcony. Her arms were on the rail, and she was staring past the twinkling lights to the mountains beyond.
	“It’s beautiful,” she said.
	For what may be the first time, I approached a woman with pure affection. I wrapped my arms around her from behind and said, “You’re beautiful.” She melted back against me, and for a little while, we just swayed slightly and absorbed the view. Moreso, we absorbed the situation.
	When I saw the girls coming out of the corner of my eye, I reflexively let go of her. She didn’t seem to mind, although she definitely noticed. I guess you could say she understood. 
	“So pretty!” Morgan was saying as they stepped out into the wind.
	Patra excused herself as the girls chatted animatedly about their surroundings. She quickly returned with the champagne and four glasses on a tray, motioning for us to follow her. When we entered the bedroom suite again, she was popping the cork, and soon handed us each a flute.
	“To us,” she said, making a toast.  Our glasses all clinked, and she surprised me by drinking with us. When we had each finished the small amount she had poured, she collected them, filled them to the brim, and put them on the nightstand along with what was left in the bottle.
	Then she pushed the girls onto the bed.
	“Okay. You guys can do your devil powder now…”
	She really did know me that well, already.

Chapter 39 – Superfly

	Patra eventually let us doze, or at least lay there with our eyes closed, and ordered a light lunch. She seemed to live primarily off of fruit alone, although I eventually learned she was a vegan. And then I learned what vegan meant. The rest was other sorts of finger food for us, which suited the moment. Instead of rousing us all together, she pulled the girls out one at a time and directed them to the shower, while she made some calls in the lounge area.
	Eventually, she brought me into the shower with her, and closed the door behind us. By now, I don’t think the girls even minded.
	“Are you happy, hus-bun?”
	I couldn’t think of any reason not to be.
	“I am,” I said. “Are you?’
	“Patra is always happy.”
	But the look on her face slightly and momentarily betrayed her. Actually, I think what she said was true. Patra was always happy. Wilma possibly never was. Her gifts were also her curse. She was too good, and too smart for the world. I think her roleplaying was escapism, and an attempt to defray boredom and world-weariness. 
	When I had dressed and emerged from the bathroom, the girls had the closet door open, and were looking inside and talking to themselves. It was lined with benches, and had a bowl of round, gray rocks.
	“What it is?” Angela asked me.
	“Shit, I don’t know.”
	Patra walked in past us, and reached around the wall, pressing a button. Steam slowly began to rise from the bowl.
	“Sauna. It takes a while to warm up. We’ll have plenty of time. It’ll be wonderful after the gym.”
	None of us said anything about that. A gym wasn’t really a part of the girls’ lifestyle, and I got a decent amount of exercise at work. When I worked, that is. That world was starting to feel sort of distant already. 
	She made sure we were all fed, dressed, and understood the itinerary for the afternoon. I no longer bothered with our old cabbie, and Patra directed our new driver to a downtown galleria sort of place (they’re too fancy to just call them malls) similar to where we had shopped at Caesar’s.
	This time, shopping was painless. The girls walked off chattering and examining the suits, first, then heading off into the dresses. I didn’t bother trying to keep up, but looked at everything, which mostly didn’t appeal to me, and I assumed this stop was primarily for them.
	I was wrong.

After continued discussion that didn’t involve me, down to my measurements, Patra selected a pinstripe suit with a decent cut, lawyer-like, I would say, and took it off the rack.
	“This will fit best,” she told me. Morgan had already grabbed a shirt and everything else with Angela, and I was promptly shoved into a changing room.

It was the first time I had seen Patra wear anything other than floral print wrap-style dresses, and she looked even more impressive.
 They wore matching pearls, and the look was completed when Patra placed the other hat on Morgan, to my mild surprise. She beamed with delight. I think even she assumed the matching hat was for Patra. As I said, she was a master of psychology. In her rare case, she didn’t use it for malicious or selfish purposes. The champagne and lunch were also on her, I found out much later, along with many other things that week. She never sought credit for any of it. Anyone else would have charged it to room service, at best. If they had the forethought to even do anything like that in the first place.
